THE RED PAGE.
There are people who say that the before-
breakfast cigar is a good one; but personally we
have doubts about it which we have never been
able to get up early enough to solve. The follow-
ing is therefore a rhyme of only four cigars, and
anyone who tempts Providence and insolvency by
smoking five must make the necessary alterations
for simself.
Everyone who read in a recent Bulletin the
tense, attractive fragment by John Reay Watson,
of Gympie, Q.—“ Some of Fate’s Puppets’*—
must look with interest and expectation to hiß
just-published book, “In a Man’s Mind.” The
book is clever. A London publisher’s-reader
hailed Mr. Watson as possibly an Australian
Howells : local criticism has more truly found in
him something like a minor Meredith. His
imitation of Meredith’s manner and mannerisms
can hardly be unconscious. But—reasonably
enough—he isn’t Meredith. When he walks on
Meredith’s stilts without Meredith’s wit and
learning to balance him, the spectacle is tedious.
Page follows page of verbal contortions and pos-
turings like—
He put his arm round her and kissed her quiokly.
“ Your hat protests,” he said laughingly.
«It is more modest than the wearer. It is a new one,
and has never seen you before, and does not know if you
are doing right.”
“ And am I ? ”
‘‘Oh, you are doing what I expect of you.”
He put his arm round her again. “ That is kind of you,
as I read.”
She broke away from his arm. “ Head it backwards
and see what you make of it," she said triflingly.
“Nothing.”
“ That is what I frequently make of you.”
“Don’t let it trouble you, dear.”
“ It doesn’t. Nothing troubles me,” she said, arohing
to him.
“Very often, I find.”
“ Nothing from nothing.”
‘Or its equivalent."
The chief characters talk in this fashion
frequently. Space is wasted; the reader is
fatigued ; and sis conception of the story or of
its people grows no clearer—grows indeed more
confused, since man and woman in the dialogue
above are thoroughly opposed in capacity and
temperament, yet their remarks might be without
incongruity interchanged. So, generally speak-
ing, throughout the book. The persons of the
drama seem often puppets, speaking the author’s
puns in the author’s hardly-disguised voice. Their
differences and individualities you gather mostly
from the author’s asides. This is bad art, and
makes tiresome reading.
w * * •
There remains much to praise. In the oombin-
ation of literary skill and intellectual capacity, as
developed in fiction, Mr. Watson has hardly a
rival among Australian writers, past or present.
There is real power in his book —power of insight,
power of reflection, power of analysis, power of
presentation. In the dissection of motives and
impulses our noted names are tyros to him. He
is always concerned with thoughts, not with
things : has interest in deeds only as they mani-
fest men and women. And a _ very considerable
creative imagination sustains him to the close of
his story. As hinted, there is a Byronic mono-
tony about his dialogue : Jessie, and Arthur, and
Amy, and May talk always more or less as doubles
of John Reay Watson; but there is indubitable
talent in the talk, and often a ring of moving
emotion. For the twelfth chapter of “ In a
Man’s Mind ” Meredith might not be ashamed to
take credit. A page may be quoted. [Jessie a,nd
Arthur are at flood of a shallow mutual passion
born of propinquity and the need of loving—
mingled, on her side, with respect and self-
gratulation (for Arthur is exalted, desirable). But
she has thought him merely amusing himself, and
in her mind has resented his attempts at endear-
ment.]
She had no knowledge of how eager he was. She
thought that he was following her in conventional hyper-
bole, with a masculine touch stronger than her own. His
arm went round her; she thought it the utmost of
cousinly freedom and impudence, and blamed herself, but
him—she hated. She thrilled as he bent over her, and,
for a moment, a thought of him supremely manly burned
within her ; quickly darkened by her past conception of
him. She was silent; and turned to him appealingly.
He stooped and kissed her, speaking his secret. “Jessie,
you darling, I love you."
“Arthur!” The word rang in discordant notes to
him, all interpretative of he;' confusion ; he went on
eagerly—
"ls it so much of a seoret, Jessie? Did you never
know ? It has been burning within me since you came.
You darling ! you darling 1 I love you ; how does it
sound ? New and strange to you, Jessie, but to me the
dearest words in the world, they have been my nearest
oompany these many days.”
She stood chilled to inexistenoe by the breath of his
passion. He had loved her, and had always been man-
fully honest. To see her shameful past conceptions of
him falling in ruins, and he standing firmly amid that
wreck, exalted her to the clearest view of him. She was
filled with an infinite pity for her self-deluded self. The
limits of control were behind her, and with her hands to
her face, her hate of him dissolved in tears.
He took his arm from her gently, reading in her sobs,
her resistance, a recoil in listening to him, her personal
incompatibility and regrets for it. For a moment he had
a knowledge of what a small part he had in the universe.
The toil of being serious, of acting concordantly with
what manfulness lay in him, enchafed him. The touch of
death passed over him.
But she was still tearful, and her helplessness roused
him.
“ Jessie, I understand," he said; “I will not ask you
to speak. It is very sad—for both of us in a way. It is a
strange world altogether. You would that I had not
spoken, but how was I to know it would be useless? It is
my sunshine and my pain to love. You must not ask me
to give that up yet, and I will not ask you for hope. I
know now that it is impossible. Forgive me.”
He rose to divine heights in her view ; spiritually she
was at his feet. She did not love him, she knew ; but
reverence and pity, the component parts of love, she had.
But her own existence sank from her in a resolve that he
should not be sacrificed. She saw only him, not herself.
She turned to him with fire in her blood, and a pitiful
consciousness that her words were true only of the
moment.
“ Arthur, I love you, but .” She kissed his hand.
“ But I am so humbled.”
Voices filled the air; he was stunned to silence, without
one sign of mastery of her words. The surprise of them
struck him, not unconsoious of their meaning, but rather
with a fatal fulness under which he was powerless.
Her attitude was constrained. Her tears had ceased
when she spoke, but she had a handkerchief to her eyes.
He put his arm round her, and she yielded to it, with her
head on his shoulder.
They lingered in silence; her thoughts ebbed and
flowed, his endeavored to unveil the subtlety of a female
conscience, as to why she was humbled. And he came
near the truth.
Presently she lifted her handkerchief above her eyes
and looked archly at him. He kissed her, she giving the
smallest evasion of it; then she was repentant and drew
his face to hers and kissed him. She stood up, free of his
arm, and he spoke:
“ Sweetheart, shall we move ! I belong to earth again.
I am feeling airily happy.’ He paused a little, but she
stiil stood. ‘ This spct is very sacred to me. Sweetheart,
shall we move ? ’
She was idle enough to think him as only complimen-
tary, and it was here that she made a vow, out of her
happiness, that she would set herself to loving him. She
so much wanted to love him, to give him his deservings.
There was no other issue for her—she wanted no other.
• * * *
Detached, this reads a little exaggerated, ex-
travagant ; but the book is keyed up to it. ’Tis a
very well-made hook —not a set of mdepende n
episodes strung on the thread of a name or w ,
out closely inwoven to the climax, lor pi •
Arthur Palmer, clever, proud, reticent, scorning
the thought and life of the profanum rulgus, has
arrived at the lonely stage, with emotions warmed
by the spectacle of the pleasant home me
and nice fat baby where he lodges. »> ben
his cousin Jessie comes to Brisbane—it all passe
in Brisbane, and there are seductive references to
Kangaroo Point and the ferry that puffs—it is
reasonable that her plump womanhood should
stir him to something like passion. But his real
spiritual affinity is for Amy Stevenson, a rather
plain, dowdy, middle-aged shop-girl with intel-
lectual tastes and more depth than the ordinary
superficiality in petticoats. So the book shows
him drawn by the flesh towards Jessie, and y
the spirit towards Amy; and he becomes en-
gaged to Jessie and breaks off; and be wants to
become engaged to Amy also, but she, wit
superior vision, sees that he is in most things
so much her better that she would in-
evitably be a clog on his life so she
makes the grand refusal, and the curtain falls.
There is a cousin named May of considerable
interest, but her conversation is very old and
Watsony for her age —fifteen. It is of Arthur
that one gets the clearest impression—difficult
to avoid thinking that he images the author ;
but the women are good also. And on a second
reading Mr. Watson’s fantastic irrelevancies efface
themselves, and one realises how much he has
labored to make his book good and true—and
how much he has succeeded.
+
Of course Charles Tyne’s life of Parkes (George
Robertson & Co. ; 17s. posted) is in no accurate
sense a biography. Mr. Lyne was one of the
solid, stolid journalists whom the S.M. Herald
nourishes—industrious, meritorious, and dull. If
his relation to Parkes was not exactly that of
Clodius his umbra, it h'ad close affinity with that
of Gamaliel his feet-sitter. Lyne skirmished
round for the crumbs of news which fell from
the great politician’s table, and occasionally, no
doubt, the mouse helped the. lion.. The lion
rewarded the mouse with a civil-service position
worth .£7OO a year or so, and with an intimacy
which gave hopes of something more lasting than
the brass. Sounded, Parkes seemed. not averse;
and Lyne began to collect materials for. the
“life” he has just published. The spirit of
devout humility in which he approaches his task
is not without its esoteric charm. He pants your
brue Miltonic invocation —
What in me is dark
Illumine, what is low raise and support;
That to the height of this great argument
I may assert eternal Providence,
And justify the ways of Parkes to men.
But to justify the ways of Parkes to men would
strain the powers of a much abler advocate than
Mr. Lyne. Mr. L., therefore, has merely excused
them —by wholesale. At the end of his book
Parkes stands whiter than the snow. There
remains in him not one flaw, on him not one fly :
Mr. Lyne has covered all over, brushed all away.
He has even the air of regretting that Parkes
was not more imperfect, that he might better
show how easily his prodigious faith can swallow
the greatest imperfections. His is the old maid’s
creed : “Yes,l do believe that the whale swallowed
Jonah; and if the Bible had said that Jonah
swallowed the whale I would have believed that
too.” The “life” is a defence, an apology : had
Mr. Lyne eloquence it would be a psean. Nor, as
a rule, does he shirk the raspers. The “ Pure-
foyed ” cheques, the Kiama ghost, the Federa-
tion flight—Mr. Lyne ambles up to them, goes
through them, under them, or over them, and is
prosing away in the distance while the jibbing
reader is still looking for a decent gate. Obvi-
ously a biography written in this spirit is no
biography. Even had Mr. Lyne the capacity to
be critical, he has not the presumption. He has
barely touched Parkes’s private life—slurring over
incidents like his third marriage, which a poor-
spirited “society” finds offensive. Of the inner man
—the real Parkes—he has attempted no estimate.
The merit of his work is as a skeleton record of
Parkes’s public life; and for the conscientious
pains taken to make this accurate, and to give a
fair idea of the progress of government and the
relations of parties, Mr. Lyne deserves credit.
The labor of preparation was considerable, for he
had to disinter his facts from many musty news-
paper-files and heaps of buried reminiscences, and
to condense forty years of N.S.W. history into
three or four hundred pages. Mr. Lyne’s book is
misleading to those without independent know-
ledge of his subject; but he has done much
valuable drudgery by which Parkes’s future
biographers will benefit.

them trom the ranks of current criticism. Not
that Mr. Byrne was ever a critic : he has not the
critic’s instinct or capacity, and rarely lightens
his subject with the critic’s illuminating flash.

Little items—
There is wit and wisdom in I. Zangwill 3 collected
essays, *‘ Without Prejudice ; ” hut they must be read as
written, one at a time, if utmost interest be sought. And,
on the whole, they do not charm so well as in the PAU
Mall Mao*. —where they charmed by contrast. . . “Mar-
garet Ogilvy,” J. M. Barrie’s stone for the cairn of his
dead mother, is mossed with many thoughts humorous or
tender—all bringing a slightly exotic air, as if they had
been gummed on instead of growing. Of the three sup-
positions —that B. wished to pay reverential filial
tribute; that hei wished to make good copy from his
mother dead, as he had made it from her living, and
worked the pay-vein to the last load ; that his reverence-
sense and his oopy-sense were mixed —one must keep tha
most altruistic. But, in the way and with the advt.
which Barrie has managed, no base Southron could have
published his mother’s bones in a collected edition. . . .
H. G. Wells’s “ Wheels of Chance " is a readable, ephe-
meral story which kills his promise abruptly. Plainly he
is not strong enough to do permanent work. These very
bright young men lack the pith and substance of
their grandfathers—probably because their grandfather*
wasted pith and substance on riotous living. They ate
the grapes: our teeth are artificial. By next generation
the hectic weeds will have died away, and the slow,
strong men will take their turn in the cycle. . . .
Some complimentary thief gives the English review
The New Saturday, Farrell’s poem “ The Last
Bullet’’—first published in Bulletin a long time ago—
with a new title, “Lost,” and without a signature. As
an example of the now-familiar manner in which our
British brethren show their appreciation of the humble
Antipodeans this is rather more ungrateful than gratify-
ing. . . . Alex. Montgomery’s little book of fifteen
stories from The Bulletin, now issued by George
Robertson and 00. (posted from this office, Is. 9d.),
gives a fair taste of his quality. Fuller considera-
tion of its merits is this week deferred
“ Crites ”: Don't altogether agree. It is not necessary to
wonder at the outspringing of “life” from “matter.”
Wherever two or three atoms are gathered together
there is life in the midst of them. The bond which
unites molecules of sand to form a crystal is rudimentary
3ex: the force which unites them is rudimentary life.
“ Rudimentary ” if concentration be higher in
the scheme of things than expansion. There is
no evidence that it is. Probably vital concen-
tration and expansion are related as force and
recoil: life moves in a circle: and there is question not
of rise and fall, but only of change on a single plane.
Tyndall wrote of “ Heat as a Mode of Motion.” He might
have written as truly of “ Life as a Mode of Heat,”
“Matter as a Mode of Life,” “Force as a Mode of
Matter,” “ Motion as a Mode of Force,” and circumnavi-
gated his thesis. Labels for the different manifestations
of existenoe are mere conveniences. Essentially, every-
thing is one thing. This is the newest philosophy, and
the oldest, and the surest.
THE FOUR CIGARS.
There are four cigars in a good man’s day:
Four—that he loves with a good man’s love;
The first when breakfast is cleared away;
The second at lunch descends like a dove ;
With the coffee at dinner the third you light,
And a fourth at bed-time to charm the night.
The first cigar is a good cigar:
It puts to flight the mists of the morn.
So calm I so cool! ’tis an avatar
Of blisses held by the day new-born:
Tou front the world like a god of war
As the nerves thrill taut to the first cigar.
The second cigar is a good cigar:
It brings a pause in the day’s hot deeds,
And sets to the outer strife a bar
For the sacred hour that the spirit needs:
Tou are heartened and strengthened—the man go<6 tar
Who gives due weight to his lunch-cigar.
The third cigar is a good cigar :
It lights a glow in the veiling eyes ;
And the brain-clouds gathering eve to mar
Pass with the clouds that it bids arise.
Ho ! Cupid spies it—the chink ajar
In the armor loosed by the third cigar I
But the best cigar is the last cigar ;
For the grey days teem with worries and woes
To be soothed away, and the g Aden are
By its magic drawn to a golden close:
And I fly with Mab in the fairies’ car
As the smoke curls up from my last cigar.
And fast through ether we glide till loom
Luxurious realms of reverie, sow’n
With roses, roses snatched from the bloom
Of the girl I love, yet never have known:
Whose eyes, caught once on a crowded pier,
Sent her soul for ever to linger—here 1
And when the last sweet breath is thrown
To the fragrant air, and the sad stump dies,
I seek my pillow—but not alone,
For the dusk is lit by her shining eyes ;
And I clasp her fondly—my love !my atari
In an ecstasy born of the last cigar.

CHITIS AND ASTHMA RAPIDLY
CURED BY Hearne's Bronchitis Cure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as. taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.
“Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —Your medicine has
cured me of bronchitis and asthma, from which I suffered
for upwards of seven years, during which period I was
scarcely ever free from coughs, and frequently the diffi-
culty of breathing was so distressing that for nights in
succession I had to sit up. I write you this acknowledg-
ment from a sense of duty, as in my case every other
treatment had failed. For a year previous I had been
getting very much w’orse, and at the time I obtained your
medicine I was confined to bed-suffering from a most
violent cough, expectorating blood and matter, and
apparently beyond hope of recovery. The first dose of
the medicine gave me welcome relief, and I steadily
improved as I continued the treatment, until I became,
as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely, H. WALKER,
Balmain, Sydney.”

The noise in throat and along back of lungs, and the

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The cabled story of a certain Mazhar Bey
is interesting as showing the ways of British and
other kinds of justice. Mazhar, a Turk, was ac-
cused by the British and Trench embassies of
having a hand in the murder of a French priest
and according to the cable news the ambassadors
demanded that he should be tried and that the
feultan should take all needful precautions to pre-
vent his acquittal. He was tried and acquitted
an d it is now cabled that the ambassadors demand
he should be tried again, and that this timegreater
care shall be taken to make sure of a conviction
Under these circumstances it seems that the miser-
able Turk might as well be hanged without a trial.
If he is to be tried over and over again till he is
convicted and the Sultan is to take special
measures to see that the court convicts him, then
he is bound to be convicted some time whether he
is guilty or not. Why not simply demand the
head of the lost Ottoman in good old piratical
fashion, and drop the bogus trial altogether ?

GLENROWAN.
MY CHRISTMAS.
0n a r^ g all hot and dus ty, in the God-forgotten
Where the lurid sun in fiery strength is burning,
1 am tramping with my bluey, on this so-called
day of rest,
And the while my thoughts to other worlds are
turning;
And my senses, as they wander, place me in a
city’s throng,
now the well-dressed masses as they
And their jdad and laughing faces as they gaily
Tell of peace to-day, whate’er may come to-
morrow.
In “y faru; y for a moment now I hear a tolling bell
Kingmgout acheerful, happy Christmas greeting
But the shoulder-strap reminds me of this blind-
ing, burning Hell,
06 ear st;arv ed sheep
And is this the day they’re singing “Peace on
earth, goodwill to men ! ”
P’ra’ps it’s proper I should, too, be humbly
kneeling; 3
Put your parsons and your godly on my : lonely
track, and then
At the end of time enquire their honest feeling.
Once to-day a buggy passed me, in a cloud of
reddish dust,
And their “ Merry Christmas ! ” through the
mist came ringing.
To the swagman slowly toiling such a greeting is
unjust, &
Makes the bitter, longing feeling still more
clinging.
Is it wonder that the bushman, with the wretched
life he lives—
With his pleasures ever limited and scanty—
Wastes his substance and his money for the
company it gives,
On his lonely travels, at the wayside -shanty ?

Held avoids with a great deal of success the
part of an over-impetuous federal leader.
Instead of rushing on to any dangerously
forward ground, he adopts a policy of exces-
sive caution, which those who have looked
to him for inspiration and stimulus will re-
gard with feelings of disappointment.
This is more like
the tone of a paan agreeing to follow than of
one eager to load. And there Is in it some-
thing resembling a note of appeal against
the road being made too hard.
Only an imperfect con-
sideration of the matter could induce anyone
to commit such a statement as this to print
» Mr. Reid is sin-
gularly unfortunate
Surely this sounds
more like a languid, protest against being
bothered.-than the. strong pronouncement of
a constructive federal statesman vitally in-
terested iu the carrying out of some definite
plan of action. . • ' * '

Government as a sort of travelling s ow,

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, acknowledge in the
1 Correspondence" column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission

tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 30, 1897.
Parochialism in the Commonwealth.
Since the date for the election of delegates
to the Federal Convention was fixed, candi-
dates, formerly very scarce and very silent,
have begun to appear and to put their views
before the electors. So far, those which
New South Wales has turned out are a dis-
appointing lot. They are mostly affected in
a greater or less degree with the idea, so
strangely common in the province, that, for
some reason which is never very clearly ex-
plained, New South Wales, as a place of
special and awful importance, can’t federate
on quite the same terms as any other and
more ordinary province might. Also, every
one who, so far, has expounded his views at
length is mostly concerned about how to
withhold all possible influence from the
Federal Government and make it, to the
best of his ability, a mere shadow, a well-
meaning concern entrusted with a certain
sketchy commission to implore its component
parts to behave themselves, and nothing
more. The powers which most of these
provincial-souled individuals propose to en-
trust to the central authority are so small
and mean and narrow that, if they have their
way, the Commonwealth Parliament will be
of less account than the mere State legisla
tures, and the part will be greater than the
whole. It is to be something like one of the
far-back emperors of Germany—a Wences-
laus or a Richard of Cornwall—in the time
when ,a German emperor was next door to
an outcast, and often had not where to lay
his crown. If these narrow views were ad-
vanced by the small fry of politics it would
matter little. But when a man of the calibre
of that eminent lawyer, R. E. O’Connor,
M.L.C., seriously expresses the opinion that
the railways and the public debts should be
left under provincial control, and when other
men of local note follow his lead, it is time
i) ask these individuals what there is left
that is much worth federating about. The
railways, more than anything else in Aus-
tralia, need to be put under federal control,
and the reasons which Mr. O’Connor gives
for excepting them are just the reasons, above
all others, why they should not be so ex-
cepted. Said reasons are announced in the
candidate’s published address as follows :
I am altogether opposed to handing over the railway
systems of the several colonies to Federal control. Our
railway policy has always been to build railways, not
only for the benefit of particular localities, but also for
the general benefit of our own colony. Our railway sys-
tem has been devised and maintained with the object of
keeping and concentrating within our own borders the
trade of our own people. In the other colonies a similar
railway policy has been pursued. I cannot understand
upon what equitable basis railways constructed on such
a system could be handed over to the Federal power, nor
can I see any advantage in exchanging the existing con-
trol and management, with its local aims and policy,
for a Federal control and management, whose policy
would necessarily be of quite a different kind. It will be
necessary, no doubt, to give the Federal power control of
the railways for the movement of Federal troops or muni-
tions of war. Except in regard to such matters I see no
reason why each State should not carry out its own railway
system just as at present.
» * * »
The above are exactly the reasons why
The Bulletin holds that the railway sys-
tems of Australia should be under
federal control. The object of the present
system, as Mr. O’Connor truly states, is
not to give the producer an outlet to the
nearest port, or to give him the shortest
and cheapest access to a market, but to
make him send his goods to and get his
supplies from Melbourne or Sydney or
Adelaide or Brisbane for the benefit
of the importers and traders and
commission-agents and auctioneers and
boomers thereof. The present system flatly
refuses to supply means of communication
to a port 200 miles away if it is in the terri-
tory of another province, even if the alterna-
tive is that the farmer and squatter have to
pay 500 miles of railage to a port in the pro-
vince to which they accidentally belong. It
is a deliberate policy of sacrificing the in-
terests of the whole country to the interests
of three or four cities, and its declared object
is to prevent trade finding its easiest and
most natural outlet in order that the city
parasites may benefit thereby. There is
probably nothing which has done more injury
to Australia than its chaotic railway policy,
and if Federation does not put this matter
right, then its principal advantage will be
deliberately thrown away.
* * * *
There are, in continental Australia, three
distinct railway gauges. Passengers and
goods coming northward from Victoria have
to be unloaded from one set of vehicles and
loaded on to another at the New South Wales
border because the cars which were built for
the sft. 3in. gauge of the southern province
are of no use on the 4ft. gauge of New
South Wales. Further north they have to
be transhipped again where the 4ft. B|in.
gauge of New South Wales changes to the
3ft. 6in. gauge of Queensland. Going into
South Australia, the Victorian cars can travel
as far as Adelaide, and then on the northern
line from Adelaide the sft. 3in. gauge changes
to 3ft. 6in. again. Though 4ft. B|in. is the
standard gauge in New South Wales, one of its
local railways is built on the sft. 3in. principle
while the gauge of another is 3ft. 6in. In case
of invasion it would be impossible to rapidly
concentrate men and munitions from the
various provinces on the point where assist-
ance was needed ; much of the South Aus-
tralian rolling stock which might carry rein-
forcements right through to Sydney or Bris-
bane would be useless anywhere this side of
Adelaide, and if other trains and engines
could not be provided in sufficient numbers
amid the confusion, the assistance which was
so urgently wanted would be left behind.
The Victorian trains could not carry assist-
ance to Sydney, nor those of New South
Wales to Brisbane or Melbourne, nor those
of Queensland to either of the southern
provinces, and in the general excitement
and over-strain and breakdown such scanty
forces as Australia could command would
probably be left stranded in instalments at
the various borders. The present system
also causes much needless expense in load-
ing, unloading and re-loading. It and the
divided management prevent economy in the
matter of rolling-stock ; the vehicles which
are not wanted in one province where trade
is bad cannot be utilised in another where it
is unusually active, so they lie by and rot on
sidings and under sheds. It is generally
agreed that the break of gauge is a great
evil, and each province is willing that it
should be remedied by all the rest adopting
its gauge at their own expense. But there
is no authority which can decide on a uni-
form gauge, and make the needed alteration,
and charge the expense thereof to the
country at large, and until the railways are
placed under Federal control there never
will be.
* * ' * *
The provincial policy which Mr. R. E.
O’Connor and others are so anxious to per-
petuate is still more ruinous in other ways.
A great part of western New South Wales
beyond the Darling might be watered with
artesian wells, and made valuable, if there
was a sufficiently cheap outlet for its pro-
ducts, but the expense of building an enor-
mous length of railway to connect with
Sydney is too great, and the produce could
not stand the necessarily heavy railway
charges if the line was built, and the bill for
interest would be too great to be covered
by any traffic likely to be obtained for
at least a century. And the great pro-
vincial principle forbids any railway con-
nection being made with Port Augusta,
which is near and convenient, so the West
remains a lost province, which, because it
can’t be developed from Sydney, must not
be developed at all, and New South Wales
loses much through a fear that its hated
neighbour may possibly gain a little. There
is a large extent of fertile country in the
extreme east of Gippsland whose most con-
venient outlet would be by railway to the
splendid harbour of Twofold Bay in New
South Wales. But the parochial railway
policy forbids Victoria to supply any outlet
except by far-off Melbourne. South-western
Queensland has a short and convenient outlet
to the sea by Port Augusta, but Queensland
policy dictates that it must do without rail-
way communication until an enormously long
line can be completed to take its produce at
twice as much expense as is necessary to
Brisbane, and while it is waiting for a boon
which will be very little worth having when
it arrives, south-western Queensland must
remain a desert. Under the provincial sys-
tem railways cost vastly more than they need
do, because no matter how much extra dis-
tance is involved they must end at the local
capital for the local capital’s private and
individual benefit. Therefore, many places
which might easily have railway communica-
tion are deprived of it, and many which
have it are burdened with longer distances
and heavier fares and freight charges than
are at all necessary. Federation ought to
remove these disadvantages, but it is actually
proposed that special arrangements be made
to perpetuate them, and that they should be
preserved as something beautiful and sacred.
Australia is chokeful of anomalies created
by the provincial railway system. Most of
the Riverina district could find an outlet for
its exports at Melbourne with just one-half
the distance to travel which is involved by
dealing with Sydney, but provincial exi-
gencies require that its system must connect
with Sydney, and that connection with Mel-
bourne shall be as far as possible cut off.
The western Riverina is deprived of railway
communication altogether because Sydney is
so far off that a line would be hopelessly un-
payable, and it is contrary to the great Kil-
kenny cat policy to promote any connection
with Melbourne or Adelaide. The western
region of Victoria is a desert, largely
because of the same policy. The transcon-
tinental line, which would make the deserted
northern coasts accessible and habitable and
add another huge province to Australia, re-
mains little better than a dream because no
one province is rich enough to build such a
work by itself. If it ever is built it will
doubtless be worked, in the good old pro-
vincial way, to bring all the available trade
to Adelaide, and any connections east-
ward with the systems of adjacent
provinces will be avoided as far as pos-
sible lest they should divert business. All
this is irrespective of the unnecessary cost
of so many separate managements, and the
losses through the reduced rates granted
where two provincial systems meet, and
where each province struggles to attract the
business of the other to its particular Little
(or middle-sized) Pedlington.
♦ * * *
The proposal, which seems to have many
advocates among New South Wales politi-
cians, that the various public debts shall
also remain detached and under provincial
control, is even less comprehensible. The
separate-railway idea is obviously the out-
come of narrow provincial jealousy ; the
other is the outcome of no visible cause
whatever. It cannot be supposed that one
set of Australian politicians would be more
incapable or dishonest or extravagant than
five or six, or that the credit of all' Australia
would be worse than that of the detached
provinces, or that the prominent men who
commanded the politics of the Federation
would be less trustworthy than the second-
rate ones who were left behind in the State
Legislatures. Probably, if the Federal Par-
liament is constituted on the lines suggested
by some prominent New South Wales poli-
ticians, no one will regret it more than they
—later on. At present they are mostly
members of local Parliaments and think
maiidy of the authority they are giving
away, and the political impotence to
which they are reducing themselves ; conse-
quently their desire is to give away as little
authority and to give it as grudgingly as
possible. Later on, many of them will be
members of the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment, and will look at the matter from
a different standpoint. Then they will
find that they have deprived themselves of
power, and made themselves as weak and
useless and impotent as possible, and that
they have been agitating to prevent the
people entrusting them with authority to do
much good for Australia. R. E. O’Connor,
as a parochial M.L.C., demanding, from a
parochial standpoint, that the Parliament of
the Commonwealth shall be deprived of its
most important functions is one thing. The
same O’Connor, as a member of the
Commonwealth Parliament Avith no autho-
rity worth mentioning, compelled to look on
while Wilks and Whiddon and Smailes and
the other small men ivho are left behind
mismanage the largest concerns of the com-
munity, and constantly reminded that, by his
own showing, Wilks and Whiddon and
Smailes and the other parochial remains are
the only men who can be fitly entrusted with
such important matters, will probably be
quite another and a different matter.
Some Federation Critics.
Sydney is the one Freetrade city of Australia
therefore in Sydney the importer is dominant!
And the importer is mostly British and German,
lhere are 4| columns of him in the Sydney
Directory and a large proportion of the names
are something akin to Feldheim and Schutze
and Dietrich and Wkbendorfer and Max
Something-or-other, and the atmosphere of the
Fatherland is over the page generally. And
the names that are not suggestive of the Rhine
are largely Hebraic ; and the names that might
be either British or Australian mostly pioxe,
on close investigation of their owners, to be
British, and to be those of men who are merely
agents for British firms and representatives of
British capital. The importer, as a rule, is not
Australian. The destiny of this country in-
terests him very little, except in so far as it
relates to the country’s ability to pay for his
foreign-made goods. The making of an Aus-
tralian nation matters to him about as little,
because there is no reason to believe that an
Australian nation will buy more imported calico
than the six dissevered provinces would do.
The arousing of a national spirit affects the
importer whose name sounds like Book or
SCHWILLE.N'SADFENGEKMANSAUSAOESCHEWE-
dischturnipstein not one cent, because Bock
and the other man live very well under the
present system and don’t want to take any
risks. The same remark applies to the importer
whose name is Smith or MAC-Something, and
whose head-quarters are in London instead
of Hamburg. The importing crowd live
by open ports and by discouraging local
industry to make room for imports. Fede-
ration will probably put an awkward bar-
rier across the open ports and sacrifice
the foreigner for the benefit of Australian in-
dustry, therefore the importers are against
Federation. The importers own the Sydney
morning press, body and soul, therefore the press
is more than lukewarm. The importers and
the press together own Premier Reid, and
Reid owns Brunker and Jacob Garrard —
therefore the N.S. W. Government is also tepid,
or even a little colder than that.
* * * *
The importers’ conspiracy against Federation
rage 3 furiously in the correspondence-columns
of the morning papers. It is backed up by
astounding lips and garbled figures, or figures
that are pure invention, to show the losses
which will fall on New South Wales by a union
with the other provinces, and how New South
Wales will be plundered for the benefit of
everybody, and will lose everything and gain
nothing. The most pertinacious correspondent
of all is one Freeman Kitchen (secretary of
the Patriotic Federation League, headed by
Mr. L. F. Heydon and shored up by Major
Lassetter, of the big Sydney iron-importers),
a gentleman who runs a little paper which is
owned by the aforesaid iron-importers, and
therefore speaks in the importing interest as
one having authority. The following is one
item in Kitchen’s logic :
As New South Wales can borrow at 3 per cent., and is,
as a matter of fact, paying- less per head of the population
for interest than any other colony, she loses heavily by
pooling her debt with the rest of Australia. For example,
New South Wales pays £1 Bs. per annum per head of popu-
lation for interest, Queensland pays £2 16s. per head,
and Victoria pays £1 14s. per head, so that we are practi-
cally burdening ourselves with a vast amount of extra
debt for the sake of creating an ideal federal union, which
will benefit everybody but ourselves. The handing over
of our railways to federal control is also another way to
impose a further loss upon this colony. We are paying
interest upon the cost of construction ; last year our rail-
ways returned £3 12s. per cent, upon their capital cost;
Queensland, which borrows at a higher rate than we do,
only managed to show a return of £2 13s. Bd. per cent. ;
while Victoria is still worse, only yielding £2 6s. per cent.
In all, we should have to share an added annual burden
of several millions as our part in the glories of federated
Australia—to be made up by additional taxation.
* * * •
This would be a convincing statement if it
were true, but it isn’t. According to Coghlan’s
“Seven Colonies” (page 392),in 1896 Westralia’s
interest bill was £1 9s. sd. annually per in-
habitant ; Victoria’s was £1 11s. lOd. ; that of
New South Wales was £1 15s. lid. Therefore
the figures of Freeman Kitchen stand con-
victed, on the best official authority, of being
pure invention, and the story built upon them
about the loss incurred by New South Wales if
it allies itself with its neighbors is convicted of
being invention of a very mean kind. Further,
the N.S.W. railways don’t pay interest on
cost of construction and have only done so
once or twice in all the years of their existence,
and those rare occasions are so far back as to be
almost out of sight and memory. The only
railways in Australia which pay interest and
working expenses are those of Westralia ; S.A.
has the smallest deficit, and New South Wales
ranks third with the next smallest deficit. But
even if the statement above quoted w r ere true,
which it certainly is not, it only covers half the
case. When the various public debts are
handed over to the Commonwealth Government,
and along with them the railways and the rail-
ways deficits, the posts and telegraphs and any
other revenue-producing assets which were
built with the proceeds of the said debts, New
South Wales will show a deficit of £1,158,000 ;
Victoria a deficit of .£1,012,000; S.A.’
£488,000; Tasmania, £302,000 ; and Wes-
tralia £33,000. Taking them all round, accord-
ing to population, Victoria and Westralia are
the only provinces which would lose anything
by federating on equal terms, and they would
lose very little. New South Wales would gain
a trifle, but not much, and S.A. and Tasmania
would gain rather more. Also, N.S. W., instead
of getting “a vast amount of extra debt’” would
shunt a large portion of her burden on Vic-
toria, which is, according to population, far the
least indebted province of the four. In fact
every one of the alleged sacrifices that are
dwelt upon so pathetically by the editor of the
impoiters publication is absolutely non-exis
tent.

another gruesome picture—
If all the goods for the Riverina, the Darling, and the

He would be howled down. Yet what has been proposed
is infinitely more ruinous, for it means handing P ovFr all
Presumably the Sydney importer hasn’t a very


lngh opinion of the intelligence of his audience
when he passes this eminently dull piece of
stupidity upon them. When the Federal
Government is responsible for the debts and
sundry other liabilities of all Australia, it must
pay them out of the customs revenue. Even if
all the Sydney customs revenue was. collected
at Melbourne, or all the Melbourne revenue at
Sydney, or all of them both at Booligal or
Mallacoota Inlet, they would have to be applied
to the same purpose, and each province would

of the provincialists who pervade the daily

the province will also lose its “outstanding
assets secured on lands ” —whatever the assets
may be and however they came to be “secured.”
The dreadful L. F. Heydon writes to say
thatN.S.W. will be “stripped of her terri-
tory,” and points out indignantly, as an evi-
dence of the malice the other provinces bear
against N.S.W., that they refused to vote
for Windeyer, of Mount Rennie, as Austra-
lian Judge on the Privy Council ! Heydon
also points out as a dire injustice that “Mel-
bourne and Adelaide will have two votes to
Sydney’s one,” which seems quite likely. Also,
Sydney and Adelaide will have two votes to
Melbourne’s one, and Sydney and Melbourne
will have two votes to Adelaide’s one, and Mel-
bourne, Adelaide and Sydney will have several
votes to Hobart’s one or fraction of one, and so
it looks pretty even all round. It would be
interesting to know if Mr. Heydon could sug-
gest any equitable device by which things would
be otherwise.
* * * *
Heydon, however, is merely a circumstance,
and not much of that ; the importing interest is
the real mainstay of the provincial movement.
All over Australia the importer is distinctly
against Federation, and men named Schwabe
and various things ending in ’hoch are rising in
protest with surprising unanimity. When it
seemed likely that Queensland would stand in
with the movement, the air was rent with the
plaint of the Brisbane importers that union
would mean the absorption by Sydney of the
trade of that sad and sweltering city, and it
would sink into ruin, and grass would grow in
its streets and banana-trees spring up on the
tram-lines. The fear that Sydney would scoop
the trade of Brisbane did a lot to scare Queens-
land out of the movement, and the fear that
Brisbane will seize a great deal of the trade of
Sydney is being used to scare N.S. W. out of it
also. Another bogey is that Federation will
enable S.A. to take the trade of Broken Hill,
which is a wild irrelevance, seeing that S. A. has
got it already. In Melbourne, the importing in-
terest is comparatively weak, but w'hat there is
of it is also bitterly hostile, the theory being that
when the border tariff is abolished the trade of
Maoriland, Queensland, and the Asiatic and
American ports will centre at Sydney, and
goods will come in at the back-door via Albury,
and Melbourne will become largely superfluous.
And a tearful voice from Adelaide points out
that just now the S.A. ports have all the
Barrier trade (the same trade which Freeman
Kitchen alleges S.A. is just going to annex
when Federation comes in), and that it repre-
sents a most valuable part of their commerce.
But the Federal Government, it is reckoned,
may try to establish direct railway communi-
cation between Sydney and Adelaide, and, if
so, the Broken Hill ores will go straight
to the N.S. W. sea-board to be smelted
where the coal-supply is, and the S.A. ports
will be left in the lurch, and something large
and vague and ghostly will ensue. So the
traders of four Australian capitals are troubled
more or less —three of them very badly—lest
Federation should take the trade of all of them
away and leave them desolate like Babylon or
Ramoth-Gilead or some unpronounceable place
in Holy Writ. And the importers of Sydney,
who are (or think or pretend they are) Free-
traders to the last German and the very last
Englishman, and who demand at election-time
that trade should be allowed to “ follow' its
natural channels,” now write to the papers and
damn the natural channels with a great damn,
on the ground that if something happens some-
thing dreadful will ensue, and the conse-
quences wdll naturally be awful. And, as a
smaller damn comes from everywhere else, it
would seem that no Australian city has suffi-
cient confidence in itself to reckon that it can
hold its own against the others without artifi-
cial assistance, and that they are the worst-
scared lot of capitals going.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Law and the Lunatic.
The suicide of one “ Professor ” Eckenstein,
in Melbourne, the other week, had an unlooked-
for bearing on the administration of justice—so
unlooked-for that nobody thusfar appears to
have remarked it. About a month previously
Eckenstein figured incognito as a principal
witness in a hotel licensing case. He gave evi-
dence on behalf of the police who opposed the
renewal of the license, and he created a public
scandal (which now appears more scandalous
than at first) by obtaining the court’s permis-
sion to withhold his name from publication.
Eckenstein, the “ mysterious witness,” testi-
fied that during a visit to the hotel in question,
he had lost a scarf-pin, value 155., whilst enjoy-
ing a private tdte-a-tete with one of the bar-
maids. His tale, of course, was uncorro-
borated, even the statement of his having
worn a scarf-pin when he entered the bar was
open to question, but the man raved about his
loss until the landlady gave him 15s. to
stop his mouth. Then the contemptible
brawler was engaged by the police to
-ome forward and accuse the landlady of
keeping an improper pub. It happened that
the license was renewed. The Court, having
made an ass of itself in permitting witness
Eckenstein to conceal his identity, ruled that
the police case had broken down, or something
to the same effect. As though for the purpose
of bringing the Court into further ridicule, and
putting an end to the “ nameless witness ”
business, this same Eckenstein put an end to
himself with a revolver, thereby revealing at
one fell pop the worthlessness of his evidence
and the reason why he desired to keep himself
dark. A coroner’s inquest proved him to be an
intermittent lunatic, suffering from a morbid
belief that his brother was always seeking
opportunities to “ put him away.” The brother,
likewise the mother, of the suicide told the
coroner s jury that their unfortunate relative
had delusions.” A boardinghouse-keeper
had noticed that “ he used to exaggerate,” and
she “,put him down as a fearful story-
teller.” In short, mad Eckenstein’s state-
ment on oath was not worth a curse.
His story about losing the scarf-pin at the pub.
was probably a hallucination, yet it might have
succeeded in stopping the landlady’s license but
for his other hallucination prompting him to
ask the Court’s leave to keep his name dark.
The inquest on Eckenstein shows at once the
necessity for post-mortem enquiry into the
public histories of such men. Justice demands
to know whether he, or any other proved vic-
tim to brain disease, has given important evi-
dence in any sort of law-case. A lunatic, dur-
ing his time of generally accepted sanity, might
hang an innocent person, “in the interests of
society,” therefore, as soon as the truth con-
cerning the poor devil becomes known to the
authorities, the authorities should look back to
discover whether the hallucinator, acting as
their friend and ally, has brought anybody to
undeserved grief. Hoes the Law keep an index
of important witnesses in criminal or civil cases,
in which it can make an easy search for the
names of suicides and lunatic-asylum patients?
The Man Who’s Dead.
From a legal point of view, the awful murder of
bookkeeper Brady, in Melbourne, would seem
not more shocking than the murderer’s confes-
sional statement, when the whole process bf its
literary manufacture is considered critically.
Brady having been slaughtered at the store
where he kept the books, certain detectives
were called in to find a possible cause for his
suicide or murder, and the newspapers promptly
announced a discovery of 500 letters, from an
indefinite number of Women, in the dead man’s
desk. Within 48 hours of his death, Brady
figured in the daily press as a colossal libertine,
a wholesale seducer, a huge black spider en-
tangled in the meshes of his own intrigues with
Women. Upon a modest salary of £4 10s. per
week the amorous bookkeeper had —in the
newspaper picture-maintained illicit relations
with Women by the score The tragedy might
be a case of suicide or murder, but, as to Women
having brought it about, there was no journalistic
doubt whatever. Presently the detectives felt
sure that Brady had been murdered, and
one Phillips, a former clerkling in the office,
was arrested on strong suspicion. Then the
press, already convinced of Phillips’ guilt and
treating him accordingly, proceeded to link
Brady, Phillips, and Women together. The
man and the youth had been twin brothers “on
the mash,” sang the chorus of morning and
evenin’ horror reporters, and the crime was
probably the outcome of jealousy concerning
Women. Circumstantial evidence against
Phillips came rolling in, until at last his un-
happy father, who had engaged a firm of
solicitors to defend the youth, sent word to
him that his family wished him to speak out
and conceal nothing. Whereupon a clerk of the
firm went to Melbourne gaol—but let him give
the game away in his own words to an Argus
reporter:
I saw the boy. I told him the arrangement we had
entered into with his father, and he expressed his pleasure
at the evidence of his father’s interest in him. He asked
at once for the newspapers, and I handed them to him.
He commenced to read them carefully, and when I
questioned him on several points he answered me at
random. He appeared to be making up his mind to do
something, and at last he said, “ I am going to tell you
as my legal adviser that I did the murder.” He then
related practically the same circumstances as are set out
in his statement.
The clerk’s narrative allows one to assume
that he carried a bundle of newspapers
to the gaol, which newspapers were full
of the sensational “ Women ” theory. The
murderer read them carefully, he was so ab-
sorbed in the Women revelations that he
answered questions at random, and when he
had made up his mind as to the best way of
turning the Women to account, he told his
little tale. The clerk carried the tale to
Phillips, senior, and the wise father, perceiv-
ing that it was good—the best thing possible—
for defence purposes, advised its immediate
publication. The law-clerk went back to the
prisoner, prepared a rough draft of his confes-
sional yarn, and “took it away, promising him to
make a copy of it, and bring it to him next day to
sign.” Then the lawyer-man edited the rough
draft, and padded it judiciously, and next
morning he placed the autobiography before the
prisoner, who signed it sheet by ‘sheet, saying
“ Yes, that represents what I dictated to you,
and it is perfectly true.” A few hours later
Melbourne was flooded with the news that
Phillips had been aided and abetted by Brady
in a mad pursuit of strange Women. One result
of his “successes with Women,” said the ean-
did penitent in his edited statement, was a
craving for new shirts and collars, which led
him to steal money from the shop amounting to
£1 in all. After getting discharged from
the shop, he was in the habit” of call-
ing upon Brady to talk about Women
and the shortage in his cash, and when Brady
finally required him to pay £2O instead
of £1 back into the till, or petty cash, he slew
the bookkeeper in a moment of passion.
Phillips had shaped his story to suit the news-
paper accounts that he read so carefully. For
the next few hours he appeared to superficial
readers of his “confession” as a much-to-be-
pitied victim of circumstances arising from
Brady’s evil counsels and bad companionship
re Women. A sympathetic public agreed that
it was just what everybody had expected.
Then the newspapers, having in the meantime
published idiotic leading articles on the precious
“ confession,” began to burst up the Women
theory. Brady’s 500 letters from innumer-
able Women were boiled down to 300
highly respectable communications from four
young ladies of “ unimpeachable character.”
The alleged intimacy between Brady and
Phillips was denied, and all the other original
“ Women ” disclosures were declared to be quite
inconsistent with the apparent facts of Brady’s
career as far as his friends and employers can
speak from knowledge and reasonable belief.
At the present moment, young Phillips’ con-
fession looks like a romance put into his head
by the daily press (the great, impartial daily
press), and its publication through the medium
of a firm of solicitors—well, as usual in Mel-
bourne, it’s the man who’s dead that did all the
mischief.
The Q. Labor Split.
The Q. Labor-party is an interestingly awkward
squad. Its strength lies, for the most part, in
the Western bush-laborers, though the miners
are usually among its supporters, and it has
adherents not always reliable among the
artisans of the towns. It gets small help from
the farmers—who are either servile or conser-
vative ; too suspicious for union and too selfish
for sacrifices. At the head of the unions which
raise its funds are delegates—sometimes sensible
and earnest, often glib-tongued and windy,
securing their own advancement by lurid ora-
tory about “ the Cause.” The extremist section
of the party lives among the bush-workers—as
a rule incapable of apprehending the gulf be-
tween desire and performance, and cheering not
for the leader who shows them what is possible,
but for the one who promises what they want —
heedless of possibilities. The motto of The
Worker —“ Socialism in Our Time is now
chiefly maintained as a sop to these extremists,
who would probably make the Brisbane
“ leaders ” totter in their seats were any with-
drawal attempted. It is excused on the plea
that it is merely set forward as an ideal to
strive for, not as a reform which is demanded,
or even one which is keenly desired. The
aggregation of unions and their delegate repre-
sentatives makes the first considerable force
in the Queensland Labor-party. The second is
the knot of officials—nominally elective, but
really made permanent by uninterrupted
exercise of authority—who are the mouthpieces
of the unions, and form the coterie directing
the subsidised union newspaper, the Worker.
It consists of a few men of ordinary abilities,
whose conscientious persistence has won the
respect of even their opponents. It is unfor-
tunate that they are tethered by their past and
by a policy formulated when they had hardly
commenced their political education. The
third substantial force of the Queensland
Labor-party is the score of members elected to
the Legislative Assembly—who have changed
surprisingly in the atmosphere of Parliament.
And on the wing of these hang th'e representa-
tives of what might be called moderate Demo-
cracy-men like Kidstoh, of Rockhampton,
who express a general sympathy with the
Labor platform, but object to “Socialism in
Our Time,” and refuse to be dominated by the
Labor caucus. To all these elements the
recent Govt, proposals to keep the Queens-
land N. Bank afloat came demanding an opinion,
and most of the bush workers considered
that the institution was proved rotten root and
branch, and the sooner it was cut down and
cast into the fire of liquidation the better it
would be for honest business and honest poli-
tics. The idea that Labor would suffer materi-
ally by the pricking of false values and the call
upon overdrafting politicians to meet their
engagements was laughed at. Sheep and cattle
would still increase, mines would still yield,
crops would still be harvested—and the in-
famous business of feeding the bank with pub-
lic loans of which the community lost the in-
terest and would possibly one day lose the
principal, would be ended once and for all.
The Worker espoused this view of the case
vigorously, and its able exposure of the bank’s
utter rottenness put to shame the pretentious
and time-serving Courier. But the Labor
members were found suddenly and singularly
lukewarm. The Govt., realising the import-
ance of the occasion, made tremendous en-
deavors to influence them to favor its ends.
They were wheedled in detail, and most suc-
cessfully. The legislative Labor lion, roaring
ruin to the Q. JST. B. and all its works,
changed in one ludicrous fortnight to a
lamb breathing blessings over Palmer and
Morehead, MTlwraith and the ashes of
Drury. As a result, The Worker found
itself vigorously opposing its own allies. It is
opposing them still. It has had no trouble in
showing that its attitude is the reasonable and
consistent one : that it is the Labor members
who have inexplicably broken away from
tradition and argument at the wooing of
the Government charmer ; and probably the
bulk of Labor electors will support it. At pre-
sent, there is a decisive rent in the Labor
party’s garments, and everybody is wondering
how on earth it can be patched up.
A Dreadful State of Things.
It is about time the Sydney importing crowd
who write to the newspapers against Federa-
tion found something newer to say than the old
wheeze that Federation “ will leave New South
Wales at the mercy of the other provinces.”
No doubt if the other provinces combined
against New South Wales in the Federal
Parliament it would be at their mercy. Tas-
mania, Victoria, South Australia, or any other
province, would be also at the mercy of the
rest if they ever made a unanimous combination
against it. Every province is at the mercy of
the rest noiv in exactly the same way ; if all the
other provinces chose to combine against New
South Wales or Victoria they could burn Sydney
or Melbourne to ashes almost any day. Sydney
is at the mercy of the country in like manner ;
if the representatives of the country districts
and the other towns of New South Wales chose
to combine they could pass a law that Sydney
should be destroyed and its site sown with salt
and the capital removed to Wilcannia. Mel-
bourne, in the same way, is at the mercy of
the rest of Victoria, whose politicians
could combine, if they liked, and decree
that the boom-city should be shovelled into
Hobson’s Bay, right down to the last
brick. If Federation is ever accomplished
a combination of politicians might order
Adelaide to be blown up with dynamite at
once, or it might order all the population of
N.S.\V. to be shifted into the Northern Terri-
tory. Every province then will be at the
mercy of the rest (just as it is now) if they
chose to make some impossible combination
against it and order its population to be cooked
and eaten, or something equally horrible. And
in the same way the people of, say, Goulburn
have for the last 50 years been wholly at the
mercy of the rest of N.S.W., and the poli-
ticians from other parts of the province might
enact that the people of Goulburn should be
boiled alive at any moment. This horrible
danger has hung over Goulburn ever since it
first existed, and unless Federation comes along
so that the people of that city can get the pro-
tection of the other provinces it must continue
to do so indefinitely. Also, Moss Yale is quite
at the mercy of the rest of the province, and
bombardment and military execution may be
ordered at any moment. The alarmists who
point out that Federation will place N.S.W. at
the mercy of the other provinces apparently
imagine that they have discovered something
new. When was there a time when every State
or province or county or town in everywhere
wasn’t more or less at the mercy of everybody
else, if everybody else only chose to combine
against it ?

“R.A.F.’' to The Bulletin :
The Q. Govt, desires to “restore confidence”; also, it
wants to encourage the “small capitalist.” And some of
it is sure that the Q.N.B. assets only require to be
judiciously realised/’ and the loss would be small; yet
men acquainted with station property assert that unless
meat and wool increase in value a great deal more than
present circumstances warrant the hope of, none of the
stations foreclosed on, and very few' of those at present in
the hands of nominal owners, would pan out for their
indebtedness if sold as going concerns. The Q. Govt.,
however, could put those properties on the market in a
way impossible to a private institution; let the small
capitalist have his chance, largely benefit the land
revenue, give employment where it is badly needed, and
restore confidence in itself, or at least kindle the hope
that for the future it intended to live cleanly.
A heavily-indebted station means a highly-improved
one. Of course there are a few favored over-drafters
to whom this does not apply, but usually an advanoe
is made for purposes of improvement, and is spent in
conserving water, and in fencing and building. The
demand for grazing farms was, and is, so strong in Queens-
land, and the supply so short, that people who did not
want them for themselves put in for one, much as a ticket
is taken in Tatt’s sweep, sure that if they won in the
ballot a hundred or two would be forthcoming from an
unsuccessful applicant. So great had this evil become
that now, when two or more want the same land, it is put
up to auction and given to the person bidding the highest
annual rental. At present the lessee has to find water,
fence, buy stock, and erect buildings, and he is either
very energetic or very fortunate in his selection if he has
any return to meet rent and other expenses in the first
three years. With stations taken over by Govt, both
selector and Lands Department would be in a much
better position. Farms would be surveyed, so that
existing improvements could' be turned ‘to best ad-
vantage by incoming tenants, stock would be on
the ground at a valuation, station yards and sheds
would be available, and they would have cash re-
turns the first shearing after taking possession. More-
over, Govt, could give better terms than the average
cent.-per-center on the balance of purchase-money, and
it (the Govt.) could proceed to make its own bad debts in
a land-bank of its own if it felt that way, instead of (as
has been suggested) taking over the debts and liabilities
of the Q.N.8., a concern that nobody believes in, and that
tries the fortitude of the Q. Govt, to say it believes in.
To sum up : the Govt, has a million times better show
than anyone else of realising without loss the Q.N.B.
station-properties. The local demand for grazing-farms
is tremendous, many farmers coming from other pro-
vinces only to meet with disappointment. Thu grazing
farmer pays a much heavier rent than the squatter, and
so directly benefits the revenue; he gives employment,
acre for acre, to 10 men for the squatter’s one, and em-
ployment is scarce in Queensland.
Happy is the burnt-oufc householder who didn’t
insure his furniture. Andrew Pontin, a Gee-
long carpenter, was prosecuted last week on a
charge of firing his residence with intent to
defraud the Manchester Assurance Co. There
was practically no sign of his alleged guilt
beyond the fact that after the conflagration he
wanted money from the co. which had guaran-
teed him against fire. The co.’s suspicions
were aroused when Pontin sent in a claim for
compensation, as per contract, whereupon the
police, being instructed to sheet the crime home
to the crafty premium-payer, discovered that
some of his had been removed in a buggy
whilst his house was blazing. It was mainly
on the strength of the buggy incident that
Pontin got dragged into court, and in court the
truth happened to come out. The fire arose
through the curtain of a bedroom - window
getting blown by the wind on to a lighted
candle. Pontin, in pulling down the curtains,
knocked a lamp over and set the whole room
blazing. Then he yelled for the neighbors to
come and help him save his furniture (the house
belonged to somebody else), and he yelled so
loudly that a man came from afar off with a
buggy and put some of the rescued goods
therein. The court discharged Pontin without
a stain on his character. He had committed
nothing but an indiscretion, in having a motive
for arson. The insurance-policy was the motive.
A person who doesn’t take out an insurance-
policy can get all his property destroyed by fire
without the least danger to himself, whereas
the more prudent individual runs a grave risk.

The public lands administration of all the
Australias is bad enough in all conscience, but
for inartistic jobbery and muddlement the
smallest province of the group is easily first.
A year or two since the Bull earth-hunger
seized upon sundry syndicate agents, and nearly
everybody in Tasmania rushed to “go on the
land.” Then the V.D.L. Bank went to pot,
and 55,700 acres were promptly forfeited, and
£16,832 written off the State books as bad
debts. Crown pastoral leases in Tas. are
annually auctioned, and the successful bidder
pays six months rent in advance, then drops
into arrear. At the end of the year his lease
is declared forfeited for nonpayment of rent,
but the gentle squatter comes up smiling
to a new auction and pays another six
months rent for the same old lease which
he has occupied for half a year rent
free ! According to the Lands Department’s
own "report some £5OOO of these arrears of
pastoralists’ rents are annually written off as
irrecoverable. Tasmania appears to be the
happy hunting ground par excellence of the land-
thief and syndicator. Under which circum-
stances it isn’t surprising that the unhappy
island has least to show of all the seven sisters
for her wild carousal on borrowed money,

Something that isn’t wholly a drama or
distinctly a comedy or altogether a tragedy was
staged at Sydney Cri. last Saturday under the
title of “ A Woman’s Reason.” The beginning of
it suggests the “Ironmaster,” and the middle is
“ East Lynne,” and the end is blessed reconcilia-
tion and a deep resolve on the part of the erring
woman in a cottage to be good in future, and the
return of Rebellious Susan to her home in a better
frame of mind. Yet there are many good points
about this generally conventional play, and it
looks like a piece with a respectably prosperous
future before it. The heroine is the daughter of a
battered and penniless lord, whose estate had
been preserved through stormy centuries by the
family capacity for sticking to the strongest side
when they could identify it, and apologising to
the other side when necessary. But the property
which had been saved by many years of hard
grovel and explanation having gone at last in back-
ing the stiffened horse, the old lord with the dirt-
colored tuft on his chin sells his daughter to a
rich Jew. Seven years later she bolts from her
Jew for no apparent reason save weariness, and
goes astray with a captain. Then she is divorced,
and repents humbly in a brownish frock in a
cottage, where her only distraction is hearing the
ungrammatical servant - girl read the London
.lernil aloud, but the rich Jew offers to take her
backhand they lean up against each other and are
re united, and their small boy’s house of bricks,
which his mother’s skirts capsized as she fled with
her captain, is understood to be reconstructed.
* * * *
The story is only a thin streak of con-
ventionality, but it is adorned with some striking
characters, and several times the two authors
have had to hold each other back firmly by the
coat-tail to prevent them saying something abso-
lutely clever. A certain round, flat-footed, heavy-
breathing man of God, very cleverly played by
Mr. Carey, is an especially beautiful character, and
when he calls with a series of heavy thuds on the
earl’s divorced daughter, and offers to get her into
a home where there are hard beds and repentance
in the form of scrubbing all day long, he is such a
stately and apoplectic parson that the soul of the
audience goes out to him. The truly clerical idea
that scrubbing is synonymous with repentance,
and that the woman who has fallen can only rise
again by fainting gratis at the mangle for ever
afterwards, and being reproved daily by an old
woman kept for the purpose, is an interesting
study. Brough’s lord is among the best of the
many good things this excellent actor has done ;
he is a small, eager, scraggy hustler with the soul-
less eye of one who thinks he has just heard half-
a-crown drop somewhere, and he has the high-
born blastedness of the aristocrat combined
with an anxious slink after portable property
of all kinds. The new man, .Eerris, also pans
out well: Titheradge’s Jew is a fine individual
whose nose doesn’t at all suggest Jerusalem ; and
Mrs. Brough with much ability saves the heroine
from falling flat into the dreary rut of common-
place where the authors have done their best to
plant her; the heroine, as she is written, being
the most conventional character in the play.
The “Woman’s Reason,” so-called because she
hadn’t any, is deserving of support mainly
because of the parson and the lord; the plot is
essentially ordinary and old and tearful, and with-
out these two characters it would probably fall
with a whack that would be distinctly audible as
far as Thargomindah. The Bulletin urges every-
•body to go and see Carey’s worldly parson, and
consider how his large, flat feet pound through
the vineyard, and how his large, damp eye rolls
at the mention of cash, and be tilled with irrever-
ence thereby.
** * *
“Cheer, Boys, Cheer,” which now holds
the * stage at Sydney Royal, is to some extent a
departure from the ordinary groove of melo-drama.
This is in itself a considerable recommendation,
and it has others as well. The plot, by which the
depraved person of the story blasts the nice girl’s
character, is much better constructed than the
average plot; if it was done in real life, and the
matter came into court, Judge Windeyer would
at once say that the evidence was indubitable, and
would lecture the nice girl within an inch of her
life, and then order her to be thrown out with
loathing. The great fighting-scene, without which
no melodrama is complete, is a large and satis-
factory butchery. Everybody is killed, except
two, and of them one is wounded badly, and the
other mortally. A dense smoke drifts slowly
across the little mound of the dead ; and the blood,
and the tattered flag, and the remains of the
National Anthem, and the bush in the back-
ground, and the hair of the wronged girl (who has
arrived suddenly) falling over G. Rignold’s coun-
tenance, all make up a very effective picture.
The fight is preceded by a more than usually
bad attack of British Flag, and the band
of stranded heroes firing its last shots at
the enemy with one hand, and writing a little
note to mother with the other, and singing “ God
Save ” with the other, and waving the grand old
banner with the other, while with the other hand
it gives a final shake of reconcilation to its long-
lost friend, and clutching its gun with both hands
at the same time, is a very effective piece of work,
and one which ought to keep the drama going for
a good while. The method by which all the
important characters are collected together in
South Africa, so that they may be shot and re-
conciled and cleared up and married to each other,
is not more complicated than usual; a Jew, and a
mine, and Plimmer, as a dead-broke speculator
who lives by swindling the aristocracy in Flynn s
office, bring it about in part, and the other part
seems to arise chiefly from Brown.
* * * •
There are a number of good situations in
“ Cheer, Boys,” in addition to the great shooting
episode, and a beautiful flight of marble steps in
the last act, and a certain amount of horse ; and
the dialogue is respectable, and the ultimate down-
fall of the bad woman is great and calamitous.
Thirty-eight people bear the heat and burden of
the scrimmage; twelve of them are distinctly
connected with the story, and the others are
mostly the women who didn’t come _ to Miss
Hughes’s reception in order to mark their disgust
at the nice girl when the cloud of obloquy settled
upon her. Miss Hughes makes her first big suc-
cess in this play, in the character of a tempestuous
lady with a noble heart who makes all the doors
in creation slam furiously as she goes by. Other-
wise the best work is done by Plimmer, Rignold,
Sweeney, and Hans Phillips; and _ the scene
mostly lies either in Matabeleland, or in Barrabas
Buildings, London.
* * * *
The good old reliable “ Silver King” will
be “ Sinbad’s ” successor at Her Majesty’s, and is
announced for Saturday. The “King” has not
been seen in Sydney for a good while now, and is
quite due for revival. Scot Inglis, reputed, on the
strength of his Melbourne success, to be the best,
as well as the youngest, silver monarch in Aus-
tralia, fills the part of Wilfred Denver, and a very
strong co. has been engaged.
* * * *
The glory of the Goatcher management at
Sydney Lyceum was of very short duration, and
by the time the remarks in last week’s Bulletin
about the utter cussedness of the show came before
a discriminating public it had flickered out alto-
gether. The Firm for years kept up the Lyceum
as the ultra-fashionable theatre of Sydney, despite
sundry drawbacks about the structure itself, but
when Williamson departed it became immediately
a very ordinary place with no mana worth speak-
ing about. “Lady Blarney” was the first piece
of genuine ill-luck which came upon it, and “True
Irish Hearts ” was the crowning disaster. Two
nights of the funny Irishman dropping the boulder
on Inglis to make sure that he was dead fully
satisfied the current demand for Hibernian humor.
The end of the season came suddenly ; there was
an announcement that the management couldn’t
see its way, &c., &c., and a sound of sudden feet
on the stair, and a door banged, and the co. found
itself dispersing in the street, and the much-
boomed McCarthy frost was finished. Apparently
the Lyceum’s luck is over for a while at least, and
it is now going to scramble for an existence, and
have its transient openings and shuttings, and its
periods of emptiness, and its other periods when
an embarrassed management takes its watch
hurriedly up to the Mont de Piete, like any other
establishment whose mozzle is suspended pending
reconstruction.
* * * *
Sydney Palace is showing some small signs
of decadence. The name of “ Phil. Goatcher,
lessee,” has disappeared off the advertisements, so
it would seem that that gentleman’s brief and
stormy career of theatrical management has ended
there as well as at the Lyceum. Knowles is in
the bill no longer, though the other notable stars —
Lee, Wilton and Stack, the Delevines and Miss
Antonio—still shed a lustre over the show. Sundry
very well-known variety people who have become
more or less ordinary through long acquaintance
fill the empty spaces more or less.
* & * *
M‘Mahon’s Cinematographe is adorned
with more new pictures, the best of the lot, and
one of the best up to date, being a view of a
steamer rolling in a troubled estuary, with big
greenish waves wobbling by. It is a thing which
no person addicted to sea-sickness could view with
an easy conscience, and if only a drowned woman
would bob to the surface on a wave-top for a
second or two, and then go under again, the effect
would be complete. The Bulletin recommends
that arrangements be made for including that
suicided woman at once. The Lumi&re “Cin.”
keeps its Melb. Cup pictures still on view in
response to the popular demand for horse, but
sundry new military subjects are thrown in to
vary the monotony of the noble animal.
* * * *
No new people have been turned on lately
at Sydney Tivoli, Chirgwin and Godfrey being
apparently equal to all requirements. The im-
mensity of the popular demand for Chirgwin
grows unaccountable. Judgingby experiences so
far, it seems as if that lonesome Zulu with the
baleful eye could go right on for years to the satis-
faction of the local gods, and despite the fact that
he is very funny sometimes and moderately funny
the rest of the time the furore seems quite as large
as there is any reason for.

The improved edition of “ Matsa ” bounds
along buoyantly at Melb. Princess’s, where a
flashlight photo, of the thirty-second audience
bore witness that the Firm’s gaudy spectacle has
a long future before it. On Saturday night the
Khedive’s variety entertainment was strengthened
by the engagement of R. G. Knowles, who sang
the same old song and wheezed the same vener-
able wheezes as of yore with the same cheerful
appearance of being in a violent hurry all the
time. The suspicion that Knowles’s humor
chiefly consists of violent hurry is a matter
not worth troubling about. In “ Matsa ”
he is merely required to do one tura at
high pressure and retire amidst loud applaus
Knowles fulfills the contract, also his presence
under the circumstances suggests a probability
further additions to the Princess’ panto, from the
source whence he sprang.
