** * *
This is the last week of “ Miss Brown,” at
the Bijou. Next week, Brough comes along with
his “ Woman of No Importance,” consequently
Melbourne must wait awhile for the promised re-
appearance of Frank Thornton in sundry popular
characters. “ Miss Brown ” has been quite popu-
lar enough for the present season, so that terrible
spinster, together with the major, the hungry
G-erman tutor, the virtuous lady principal, and her
choice collection of girls will descend upon Sydney
with every hope of making a good impression.
** * *
The Allisons and Carl Hertz’s ltaest illu-
sion are principal factors in another great boom at
Melb. Opera House. The dancing of the Allisons,
singly or together, is remarkably clever as regards
leg-work, and pretty in sentiment—the best
“turn” of its kind that Rickards has given to
his adopted country. Also, Harry Shine is back
again, almost as good as new, and Charles God-
frey will return for a farewell season on Saturday.
Meantime Coleman, Alice Leamar, Granville, and
Whitburn are appearing as usual, and the glory
of the Opera House bill for next week passeth all
understanding.
** * *
At the Alexandra, a hard- working common-
wealth management produced ‘ ‘ After Dark ” on
Saturday. The Bulletin begins to respect
Glover, Wilfred Shine, John Forde, Katie Towers
and the rest of them for their desperate endeavors,
and it hopes—in the time-honored language of
many melodramas —that “ After Dark ” will come
the Dawn. All the same this journal has doots on
the point.
At Poverty Point.
Sad is the old age of the stage in Australia—and pro-
bably everywhere else. Awful will be the latter years of
the Thespian who, when he was young, spent all his
money on drink, cards, velvet collars, Sunday buggies
full of peroxided females, bear’s-grease for his hyacinthine
locks, totes, and other vanities. More than one clever
old actor is in Liverpool (N.S.W.) benevolent asylum ;
another is in the Rookwood poor-house, which thought-
fully provides for the benefit of the old people who have
had to take refuge there a noble view of N.S. Wales’
biggest graveyard—Sydney’s City of the Dead. Then
there are two or three poor old septuagenarians begging
at the street corners, for to-day is the Day of Legs and
their time is past. A benefit for these unhappy old men
wouldn’t draw a cent,
“ Whom the gods wish to destroy, they first make
mad.” Which is what the gallery does to a certain kingly
personage. They decline to take him seriously even in
his most tragic moments. And so, cries of “ Nark it,”
“ Leave a bit of her nose, old man,” &c.", are received with
counter blasts of “ Brutes,” “ Cads,” “ it all, can’t
you stop your noise.” The writer has seen Irving, owing
to a gallery riot, ring down the curtain in the middle of
the trial scene, Shylock come forward, calm the noise
by a few adroit sentences, ring up the curtain, and start
the scene again. And the play proceeded to a close with-
out further discord. Verb. sap.
Having determined to stop theatre - overcrowding,
N.S.W. Colonial Secretary Brunker should behave simi-
larly with the churches. On specially holy occasions,
these edifices are crammed full. They are mostly built on
the go-as-you-please plan, and a tour of inspection has
proved to the writer that not more than a dozen Sydney
churches conform to the demand for exits. Temporal
fire, at least, burns the worshipper as well as the scoffer,
and it is surprising that St. Brunker should endeavor to
safeguard only the wicked theatre-goer.
Melb. surgeon Fred. Bird the other day removed from a
woman an overgrown spleen weighing s|lb. Evidently
part of a dramatic critic got misplaced when she was
a-building.
Wily R. S. Smythe lately started a newspaper contro-
versy touching a new Shakespearean text interpretation
by Locke Richardson, from which circumstance The Bul-
letin assumes that Smythe thinks of booking that brilliant
reciter for another Australian tour. The controversy in
question would have enthralled the elderly gent sketched
by Dickens in “ Nicholas Nickleby,” who wrote a bulky
pamphlet on the question whether the deceased husband
of Juliet’s nurse was indeed a merry person, as stated by
his relict in the play, or merely a nice man in her connu-
bial opinion. Richardson (with his tongue in his cheek
no doubt) suggested that when Dame Quickly heard Fal-
staff babble of green fields, the dying reprobate was trying
to quote from a certain hackneyed Psalm, and R. S.
Smythe —may Heaven forgive him ! —commends this
. beautiful idea ”to the notice of the Bard’s fervid admirers
in this country. The beautiful idea is, of course, working
its fell purpose among Shakespearean cranks. Some of
them swear by the Psalm theory, others think that the
fat old creature of fiction was babbling about the fields
where he gathered daisies in his childhood, whilst learned
David Blair wades in with another fellow’s doctrine that
Jalstaff s death-bed was covered by “an old-fashioned quilt
formed of lozenge-shaped patches of silk and satin of many

wild flowers were neatly embroidered. Falstaff s closing
eyes dmily discerned the flowers and green patches with
which his fingers were fumbling, ancf he very naturally
babbled of green fields. Shakespeare forgot to throw in
any reference to the quilt which David Blair has
pictured for us, but it is satisfactory to know

Australian tenor Walshe has of late made successful
public appearances at various London halls and clubs
Walshe ha,s no particular love for concert work. His chief
desire is to make a London success on the staire a In
Hayden Coffin. He has sung to D’Oyley Carte arid ’other
centlemen whose opinion is worth obtaining, and has met
fvith considerable encouragement. He will return to
America as soon as he has convinced Londoners of his
musical prowess.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent writes : Vocalists
Arthur Deane and William Walshe both have very good
Emrlish prospects near at hand. The Sydney baritone’s
voice and vim have won him compliments and kudos of
coin and the tenor’s advice to “ Take a pair of sparkling
eyes’” is cordially approved of on every occasion of
delivery.
, young Percy Grainger, the 11-year-old Melb. blue-
eved golden-haired cherub, pronounced by his partisans
to be a piano-prodigy, lately played at Frankfort, where
he is showing. He is said to possess a soft, velvety touch,
and has evinced a marvellous knowledge of technique.
Eric Lewis, the Firm’s to-be-imported comedian, hails
from Northampton, England (Bradlaugh's and Labou-
chere’s town), where his father had something to do with
tailoring His off-stage name is Frederick Lewis Tuffley.
“Haresfoot” makes a bad shot in comparing him to
Arthur Roberts, whom he does not at all resemble. He
was for years a drawing-room entertainer, like Grossmith,
whose quiet, funny style he follows. His mother was a
good professional vocalist a half century back,
t Haresfoot: “ Lastly, re tiny Alma Grey. She is not a
puppet, but enchants by her intelligence. Nor does she
sing suggestive songs.”
Siegfried Wagner is likely to visit Australia shortly.
Son of Wagner the Great, and of Cosima, whom he
snatched from his enthusiastic friend Bulow, she being
the daughter of Abb6 Liszt by Countess d’Agoult. Sieg-
fried has made a bit of a name in Berlin and London as a
conductor, but, of course, was born with his bread and
butter cut.
Wien-and intends to celebrate Schubert’s centenary by
an appropriate organ recital, culled exclusively from
Schubert’s compositions. Australian audiences are so
seldom treated to selections from Schubert that it will be
a treat to harken to the delicious melodies of the illus-
trious song-writer.

Melb.: “ Pastor Walters’ ‘Joseph in Canaan’ is at the
bookshops. My surprise over this work is that it appears
rather the emanation of an intellectual playwright some
Boucicault than that of an unpractical clergyman,
brimming over with poetry. But I found Walters, in the
pulpit, a delightful man of shreds and patches. The
poetic treatment of the legend by living writers may be
found in Watts’s (?) ‘Joseph and His Brethren’ and

Before the brig Phillis—the craft rescued
from famine by a boat’s crew from the s.s. Orient
—left Rockhampton (Q.) several weeks back, one
of the ship’s company, charged with desertion,
stated that it was unseaworthy, but was ordered
to return to duty, and the brig was allowed to
proceed to Melbourne, the Rockhampton shipping-
master having given the necessary certificate.
N.S.W. Premier Reid is plainly on the
fence re Federation. “If this Convention does
not do it,” he says to a S.M. Herald reporter,
“no other arrangement will bring Federation
about. ... I want to know whether this Federa-
tion movement is a practicable one or not, so that
if it is not it can be dropped, and if it is it can be
brought to a head. . . . In view of the possibilities
of the future, it would be a great thing for the
colonies to have a well-thought-out Constitution
ready for immediate adoption if necessity should
arise.” Aw! Handy little thing to have some-
where about the place, doncherknow.
The N.S.W. Agricultural Society will
hold its exhibition from 14th to 20th April inclu-
sive, and the exhibition will be on a monster scale.
Last years’ chestnut about Federation will be
served up again this year in the form of a grand
naval and military tournament, wherein all the
sheep-dog warriors of Australia will show off.
Other attractions will be the Lady’s Hunting Con-

The Wild-Cat Column.
The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney has
a net profit of £40,066 for the half-year compared with
£40,303 and £39,474 as shown in two previous balance-
sheets. When the 8 per cent, dividend (£40,000) is paid
there consequently remains just £66 to add to previous
balance. The Commercial of Sydney, with its £1,000,000
paid-up capital, £1,010,000 reserve-fund, £2,391,000 coin
on hand and £840,000 of other liquid securities, and its
constantly-increasing business, hardly calls for criticism ;
so far as can be ascertained from its figures it is absolutely
solid and beyond any shadow of suspicion. Therefore the
fact that it nowadays pays away practically all its profits
in dividends is of less account than it would be in the case
of a concern with less solid resources behind it.
* * * •
A notable revival has lately commenced down
South, and the balance-sheet of the Bank of Victoria, like
most other Melbourne balance-sheets this month, gives
solid evidence of improvement. The half-year’s profits
are £17,928, against £15,068 last half, when the institution
touched low water-mark. A 5 per cent, dividend on
preference shares accounts for £10,419, and the balance,
added to £31,410 previously on hand, makes a credit to
profit and loss of £38,920. The ordinary shareholder gets
no div., and as the profits, after providing 5 per cent, on
preference capital, would only run to 1)4 per cent, at the
best, he will probably have to wait sometime yet, though
his prospects are distinctly better. The upward move-
ment shows itself in every branch of the business. Note
circulation has increased by £29,000 during the half-year;
bills in circulation by £220,000 ; cash and liquid securities
are £236,000 better, cash alone being over £1,000,000,
despite the large repayments lately made in the way of
reconstructed deposits. The Bank of Victoria reckons on
releasing all its reconstructed deposits by some time next
year, and, judging by present appearances, it should be
able to do so without the slightest difficulty.
The Victoria’s is the first bank balance-sheet
issued under the new Vic. company law, and that strin-
gent measure makes it a great deal more distinct than
any previous document of the kind. It sets forth sepa-
rately Government deposits bearing interest and not
bearing interest, and private deposits under the same
two headings ; it sub-divides the liquid assets into eight
distinct items; shows separately bank premises (which
are put in at cost); other real estate (presumably acquired
under foreclosure) and the amount written off the original
cost thereof; advances, less bad and doubtful debts ;
shares; bills discounted; and contingent liabilities on
letters of credit, &c. The Victoria stands this investiga-
tion very well, the amount of “ other real estate” in par-
ticular being comparatively small and put into the
balance-sheet much below cost, and the item, “ shares in
other companies,” covering only £4200; but some other
Vic. balance-sheets, under the same process of dissection,
should be interesting reading. Also the auditors’ certifi-
cate states comprehensively that the sheet is correct —not
“ according to the books,” but with no reservation at all.
One noteworthy feature is that the Bank of Victoria has
still no reserve-fund, and now that things are distinctly
on the mend, a serious effort should certainly be made to
establish one.
* • * *
The first report of the Commercial Bank of Aus-
tralia under its latest reconstruction scheme is a very
cheering document compared to some which went before
it. Thanks to the large reduction made in the interest on
reconstructed deposits, or thanks partly to that and
partly to a new system of accounts, the net profits are
£41,636, whereas last half there was a loss of £24,054. A
2 per cent, dividend (£21,171) is paid on preference shares,
£29,000 is carried to an “ assets trust special reserve
account ” to provide for losses on the realisation of the
old assets, and the remainder, with £5474 previously on
hand, makes £5940 carried forward. The business of the
new bank, which is now kept separate from that of the old
Commercial, looks large and prosperous. Note circula-
tion has increased £lO,OOO inside six months. Deposits
seem to have also increased, though the new mode of
keeping accounts makes comparison rather difficult; and
liquid assets (£1,654,000, of which £1,207,000 is cash) have
improved by £24,000. The brightest feature in the report
is that, even apart from the saving under the reconstruc-
tion scheme, the Commercial seems to have turned
a corner which looked very unnegotiable a few
months ago. The saving by reduction of in-
terest is about £45,000, whereas the improvement
in net profits, compared with last half-year is appa-
rently over £65,000. Therefore on the figures it
would seem that the institution has rallied, with the
general improvement of things, to the extent of over
£20,000 —or else it has remained about stationary,
or else it has really improved £37,000. There are
so many possibilities about the new system of accounts
that until the next balance-sheet is available for purposes
of comparison it is impossible to be quite sure on these
points. At all events, the situation is that the Commer-
cial, apart from the saving by reduction of interest, which
is a large item in itself, has either become a great deal
better or practically no worse.
The assets of the old Commercial are still more
or less of a mystery. They amount to £5,825,904, which is
original book-value, less inner reserves (amount not
stated), and £40,249 “ balance at credit of special account
in new bank.” Against them are £3,618,148 of old deposits,
£23,852 accrued interest, and the balance of £2,224,153 is
an alleged asset of the new bank. If these figures mean
that the old assets have yielded £40,000 odd to cover
£23,000 interest due to depositors, then the bank’s posi-
tion has improved enormously ; if they mean something
else, then it hasn’t improved quite so much. The long-
expected Companies Act having passed, it is proposed to
write off £1,200,000 of ordinary capital, and to write down
the old bank’s assets by the same amount. Then
surplus profits, after paying the guaranteed 2 per cent,
div. to preference shareholders, will go to provide against
further losses, and as there will still remain £BOO,OOO of
ordinary capital paid up and some £600,000 to come in
which can be written off if necessary, there should be
more than sufficient margin to cover everything. But
considering that the Commercial has only liquidated
£50,000 worth of the old assets in the last six months, it is
evident that they are a very hard lot to realise, and
very unsaleable, and the inference is that a good
proportion are bad, and the rest are mostly of a kind
which have to wait for the revival. As no statement is
definitely issued as to the proportion reasonably supposed
to be good, it may be that the writing off of £1,200,000
will cover the bad ones, or that it is merely the biggest
amount which the board can venture to write off just
now. If the Commercial can keep on as at present build-
ing up a reserve against these assets, it must come out
right some day, but, in the absence of accurate infor-
mation, it is impossible to say exactly when.
* * * *
The Bank of North Queensland closed its half-
year with a net profit of £l7lB, which is an improvement
on the profits (£1703 and £1182) for the two previous half-
years, especially as premises account is being liberally
written down. Even with this improvement, however,
the amount would barely be equal to a dividend of 1% per
cent., and as this very meagre div. isn’t worth paying,
and there are still possibilities of more depreciation to be
provided for, and something in the way of a reserve is
much needed, the amount is wisely carried forward, and
makes, with previous accumulations, £7365 to credit of
profit and loss. Deposits have increased £3OOO during
the half-year, and the full amount offered (£50,000) of the
new 4J per cent, deposits (which are repayable only at
the option of the bank) has been taken up. It will be
interesting to note how this experiment pans out, though ;
so far, it Beems to be doing well enough. The B.N.Q.
directors reckoned that if ordinary fixed deposits are
taken at, say, 3)4 per cent., and one-quarter is retained
in cash to meet possible withdrawals, the bank only gets
the use of £75 out of every £lOO, while paying interest on
the whole amount, and thus the interest is really £4
13s. 4d. per cent. The interminable deposits require no
cash reserve against them, and may thus be really cheaper
at 4J than ordinary ones at 3J. Still the interest looks
decidedly high and even risky. The B.N.Q. has a reserve
in gold, silver, and State paper of over £87,000, which is
equal to 37 per cent, of all its callable liabilities. This
seems an absurdly high figure, and if the institution put
£20,000 into State securities and thus earned an additional
£6OO to £7OO a year, it might be wise policy. Also, if any
further issue of interminable deposits is proposed it
should be possible, in view of the gradual improvement
m the B.N.Q.’s position, to get the money at 4 per cent.
at least the experiment should be worth trying.
Apropos o f the contention that the State should
“ e ;k i ssue r of paper-money : The circulation of the
B.N Q. at its largest amounted to about £28,000. It was
abolished in ’93 when the Q. Government took over the
local paper-money industry ; yet, at the end of ’96 £309
worth of notes are still unpresented. Only £lB worth
have been presented in the last six months, so it seems
quite probable that £2OO or so will never turn up for
redemption, and will represent the leakage in one small
bank’s circulation inside BJ4 years. And considering how
many banks have a very much larger circulation and
many more years for the leakage to accumulate, there
seems to be a respectable item of revenue here awaiting
State annexation.
* * * •
The Trustees, Executors, and Agency Co.
(Melb.) looks better than it did. Profits for the
six months are £1779 against £1702 and £256 for two
previous periods. A dividend at 5 per cent, absorbs
£l5OO and leaves £279 to carry forward, making the
balance £4514, and the justification for this dividend is
very difficult to find. The co. has £60,000 capital and
£40,000 alleged reserves. But among its assets are £14,000
of preference shares and £lB,OOO of deposits in the Aus-
tralian Deposit and Mortgage Bank, and the operation of
the new Yic. Companies Act drags forth the information
that the whole £32,000 is merely face value. The
£lB,OOO of deposits are worth at present about £13,500 at
the outside. The £14,000 of preference shares are worth
just now not much more than nil. There is obviously
need for a very heavy writing off here, and, until this
item is put on a sound footing, the Trustees,
Executors, and Agency Co. should pay no divi-
dend, or, at least, should not dissipate more
than half its profits in dividends. The whole of the co’s
investments (£62,337) only return interest at a shade over
2 per cent, per annum, and even if the preference shares
—which yield nothing—are left out, the rate is very little
over 2£ per cent, per annum. As the £lB,OOO of deposits
pay 2\ per cent., the Govt, stock pays 4 per cent., and
the shire debentures pay 5 per cent., it certainly looks as
if some of the other investments were not paying at all,
and would also require writing down. Also, the fact that
£62,000 of advances yield £2O less than £59,000 did
last half-year isn’t cheering. One thing at least is
certain: the absurd £32,000 “face value” of Aus-
tralian D. and M. Bank securities wants writing
down to a very low figure; and till this is done the
balance-sheet of the Trustees, Executors and Agency Co.,
though the concern seems to have a large margin over
all possible losses, isn’t a thing to be taken too seriously.
The liquidators of the Colonial Bank of Maori-
land, who are now cleaning up what rags and dust remain
of that unhappy institution after its good business was
taken over by the 8.N.Z., report that things are not look-
ing well with the remains, and that for many years past
the Colonial’s alleged reserve fund of £65,000 “could
have no real existence.” Another evidence for the need
for a State audit. Is there any Australasian bank which
was independently investigated under any circumstances
whatever without the balance-sheet proving a shameful
lie?

The Victorian State Land Bank is issuing its
first £25,000 worth of bonds locally at par, bearing 3 per
cent., and the management is confident that the money
will roll in abundantly. Maoriland got the funds for its
land-bank in London; the interest was 3 per cent., but
the loan only realised about £93 14s. 6d. net. S.A. raised
its land-bank capital at par in Adelaide, but the interest
was 3£. If the Vic. scheme comes off, its land-bank will
make a better start than either of the other two.

At time of writing, although it has been officially
semi-denied and wholly beclouded with hypothetical
possibilities, it appears to be true that 2000 sovereigns
have mysteriously disappeared from a certain bank —from
the head office ; ay, even from that sacred receptacle
called “the treasury.” The report somehow got into the
local newspapers. But all the thrill was successfully
taken out of the sensation by alleging that it is possible
the missing “ two thou.” is an error in book-keeping.
Having stated which, the local papers dropped all refer-
ence to the matter. The latest whisper is that it is
possible that the sovs. might have been overpaid to some
other bank or banks in the “clearing house,” and that
the other banks have been asked to count their gold and
see. The bank’s treasury, by the way, can only be opened
by three officers together, each with a separate key.
Which roundaboutly recalls an Australian
mystery of some years ago. Two officials were work-
ing late at night at the bank. They had balanced their
cash and placed it in the safe. It took their two keys to
unlock (or lock) the safe ; yet, in the morning, £l7OO was
missing. Roughly speaking, the lost money consisted
of 1000 sovs. and 700 in notes. Months afterwards the
missing notes were found beneath a bush, tolerably de-
cayed ; but the sovereigns were never traced. The police
theory naturally was that the robber had been afraid to
negotiate the notes and had buried them successfully.
There is now little doubt that the theft was effected by a
very clever skeleton-key man who died only within the
past three years.
One smart Australian bank-robbery of 23 years
ago was done like t,his(so says a veteran money-changer) :
—“ Most bank-safes in branch establishments are on
wheels, in order that, in case of fire, they may be rushed
out swiftly into safety. Thus, the bottom of the safe is
from sin. to 9in. from the floor. Very well, then. You
and I are balancing-up cash, and have a key a-piece, both
keys being necessary before the safe can be locked or un-
locked. We find the cash right, and it is bagged. I take
the parcel from you, and appear to place it in the bottom
of the safe, whereas I really put it on the floor and push
it swiftly with my foot beneath the safe, the door of which
I close rapidly. I ‘ bustle ’ you that I’m in a hurry to be
off, and we lock and double-lock the safe all right. I
have duplicate keys of the street-door and of the door
leading to the room containing the safe, and I come back
again when all is quiet. Should I be caught, how easy is
it to say ‘Oh, I left my pipe,’ or anything else? I once
knew a man who tried that dodge (in Maoriland), but
the Scotch manager’s eagle-eye ‘ spotted ’ him. The
manager said nothing, but when his assistant was gone
he ‘ lifted ’ the cash himself, ‘ planted ’ it safely, and had
his dear friend arrested for getting into the bank after-
wards. What puzzled the police that time was, ‘ Where
did the man manage to pass over the cash ? He must
have had an accomplice.’ (1!).

Ed. Bulletin, —It is not true that during last
year there was not an ounce of reef gold got within a ten-
mile radius of the Broad Arrow. The Star of Australia,
for instance, crushed 30 tons for an average of over 10oz.,
and the Excelsior, Bardoc, which is being worked by the
prospectors, crushed a lot of stone for an average return
of 30z., and owing to defective machinery there was a
good deal more on the plates ; also the Austrian mine at
Bardoc gave 48oz. for 10 tons stone. There are many
more here and round the Arrow and Smithfield and Pad-
dington that will average 2oz. anyhow. There are few
batteries here as yet, but you’ll find the Arrow Proprie-
tary will give over 2oz. when the clean-up comes.—
Bardoc.

“ The old story that the Boulder was floated as
a ‘ cat ’ is set at rest by the manager’s discovery that the
pile of ore at grass at the old prospectors’ shaft, which re-
mained untouched for two years, is worth 3oz. or 4oz. per
ton.” Thus the Kalgoorlie correspondent of the Western
Mail. This, remarks a Bulletin correspondent, is the
sort of thing we are asked to swallow over here. I was
one of the first to peg a lease on Hannan’s, 'when Egan
found the Crcesus. I pegged out on the end of the Croesus
South, and frequently had a look at Brookman’s leases.
The work was then, and for long afterwards, confined to
the reefs ; the existence of the present lode as a gold-bear-
ing concern was unknown, and shares, which are an in-
fallible proof as to the value set upon a claim by the holders,
could be had at a song—in fact, as is well-known, they
simply weren’t marketable long after the property was
floated.
“Here,” continues the writer, “is another
item. ‘ It is understood that with regard to the recent re-
ported find of telluride ore in the N. Boulder that the
manager admits striking stone worth about 500 oz. per
ton.’ Why don’t the Kalgoorlie people let Londonderry-
like gabble slide, and allow their crushings to talk ?
These latter are good enough in themselves to boom the
field.”

Hannan’s True Blue Co., Ltd., should add the
word “Duck” to its title. It started well with a large
fat capital of £125,000, from which the vendors filled their
pockets and black bags with 93,400 fully-paids. The
working capital was £30,000. Five samples of stone gave
90oz. to the ton of prospectus, according to an expert’s
report. Another expert estimated “ the value of the ore
at soz. per ton from all parts of the mine.” In London
Financial Times a shareholder writes to say the Hannan’s
True Blue (Duck) “ has been a great disappointment to
the shareholders, and the prospects look very poor.” He
also remarked that the four directors drew £lOOO in fees
for the first year, and in view of the poor outlook ,he
thinks £5OO ample for the second year. Three-and-six a
sitting would be over-payment of any London director,
and then the sittings should be monthly.

W.A. Goldfields Water-Supply sickens. Ap-
proximately in hand, £lO,OOO, but if the co. falls across
any more snags like the Bayley’s Consols contract it won’t
have that long. Six months’ water-sales to October,
£2247, to earn which the co. spent £6324. £3OOO went in
one swoop over water bought from the aforesaid Consols,
the bulk evaporating before the co. could find a market.
Evidently the water-supply is another dead-horse creation
of the W.A. boom, and a mighty summer and a sizzling
thirst will have to seize Coolgardie and Kalgoorlie to help
the co. off the rocks. The concern seems principally Job-
son, who draws £l5OO a year, but Jobson doesn’t appear
to figure anywhere in the balance-sheet. Why ? Or have
they stowed the small man away as a sundry creditor ?

A strike at the Hit or Miss mine (W.A.) against
a reduction of wages. Well, from all the writer heara of
this mine, no wonder an attempt to reduce wages is made.
If the co. is sympathised with, then at ss. a week a miner
would be overpaid for working in the mine. If, on the
other hand, the miner is to get the sympathy, £5 a week
would not recompense him for working in so poor a show.
The manager, a well-known Bendigo man of the highest
credentials, lately resigned—he could not stand it. A few
days after he left a statement was issued in London declar-
ing that the failure was due to mismanagement, that the
mine was likely to turn out very valuable, as there was
20,000 tons in sight. This has every appearance of being a
lie. The manager was good enough for the big mines of Ben-
digo, and has the choice of half-a-dozen mines to manage
now. The old game —“ mine no good, blame the manager.”
Why is it that the last two big-tonnage crushings from
this mine have not been recorded in the usual 'quarters ?
Their average of less than 4dwt. to the ton is one reason.
The mine as it stands appears to be completely exhausted
of every ton of payable stone ; the future depends upon
striking new rich ore-streaks, such as those on which it
was sold for an awful price ; the ore in sight only assays
6dwt. The manager will defend his reputation, and is
determined that the deluded British shareholders shall
know the whole truth.

Exchanges by Ivanhoes dropping to below £lO. Occa-
sioned probably by phrases in the manager’s last report.
* * * *
At Norseman, The Bulletin hears, things
don’t improve rapidly. None of the mines on the main
line of reef are crushing. No. 1 North has its battery
ready, but no water. The Desirable, floated in England
lately (£250,000), has a 10-head battery going sometimes.
The Brookman crowd are in this.

as .a good prospect. When is N.S.W. likely to have any-

* * * *
The Block 10 concentrating mill promises to
establish the reputation predicted of it—that the strain
upon the working parts would he found too severe. A
rock-breaker smashed lately, and caused idleness for a
few days. But while it does go the work is among the
best done.
* * * *
On dit the B.H. South made a profit of nearly
£5OO from its recent milling —1353 tons crudes 571
tons concentrates, the mine’s top return to date. Any-
thing close up to a 3 to 1 average leaves the South about
30s. per ton to the good.
* * * *
Cause and effect. The Lady Brassey, an alleged
silver-mine on the Barrier, largely tipped on the holy
Sabbath (32,900 shares forfeited on first call): manager
says he can't quite tell the value of the mine until he un-
waters an old shaft, and there is no money for the work.
Pride of the Valley, a Sydney-owned show at Hannan’s,
No doubt years ago, when silver was 40 per cent, higher,
a very rich body of ore was left in the shaft as usual!
* * * *
Girilambone copper-mine (N.S.W.) buyers 10s.;
sellers 16s. Why this margin? Something gone wrong
with the works, perhaps. The manager fell ill, and
smelting ceased —a rather sick excuse. Ore said to be
troublesome to flux.
• • • •
Baker’s Creek, Hillgrove (N.S.W.), 701 tons,
4210 z. No divs. in this. Where is that wonderfully con-
sistent gold chute, if not in the next mine ? Shares down
to 6s. Eleanora, 664 tons, 5120 z.. and another div. an-
nounced ; now a well-managed concern. West Sunlight,
450 tons, 3280 z., better than usual. It needs it, having
made many a call, paid away tens of thousands in wages,
and never had the satisfaction of a div.
* * * *
It is not true that the Lagoon Creek and Paddy
Lackey mines have been sold ; it is a lease known as
Dunn’s Find which is being traded. The Lagoon is
already an English co. The Paddy Lackey has
been under offer to London people, who, for some
inscrutable reason, did not close. In boom-time, it is
the cheap and nasty mine the boomers want. The
Paddy Lackey is too good a mine to be given away.
Worked continuously for 15 years, and for the last 10
every crushing has left a big profit. Its oi .-body is over
500 ft. long, averages over 2ft. thick, and yields, year in
and out, an ounce; is free milling and easily worked,
depth 360 ft., and the ore as good as ever for over 500 ft,
along the bottom level.
* * *
An important development rumored to have
taken place in the Beacon n ie (N.S.W.), formerly called
Taylor’s Reward. What lo > ted near the surface to be a
very flat reef, has taken a s ulden downward turn, and
continues to carry heavy g Id. The secrecy attempted
in the running of Mrs show is very absurd, because it is
impossible to maintain it.

A correspondent who is prospecting for opal
about 65 miles N.E. of White Cliffs and on the same line
of hills, says that splendid specimens have been picked up
on the surface, but no seams have yet been found.
Warden Brown thinks payable opal will be found on
further search.

Deep delver Lansell smiling again over his 180
Bendigo. Trial stamping from the sft. of stone in the
3310 ft. level averaged Bdwts. Payable, so it’s no wonder
the Bendigo people are on the scream. Similar milling
from the 35ft. mass of spurs at 3200 ft. didn’t pan so well,
yielding just over 2)dwt. per ton. Lansell is said to be

development money out of the 180.

Port Augusta (S.A.) broke out into rejoicing
a fortnight aero upon the landing there of the first truck
of sulphides en route from the B.H. Central to the Cockle
Creek (Newcastle) works. Hitherto all the shipping
trade of the Barrier has been done by Ports Pirie and
Adelaide. Special railway concessions and a Govt, wharf-
lease on good terms took the co. to the Northern port.

SOCIETY
COOLALTA
CAMEO
This is the season when the Australian
Goyernors more or less quarter themselves and
their belongings on poor old Lord Gormanston. The
Bulletin is sorry for H.E. Before he accepted
the Governorship of the Isle of Sleep a Lot the
local authorities reduced the vice-regal salary to
<£2500 a year in all, on which H.E. has to main-
tain Govt. House. He distinctly bargained, in
accepting the salary named, that he was not to be
expected to entertain or to subscribe to public ob-
jects, but, all the same, he does entertain, more
or less, and when he refuses, as per contract, to
head subscription-lists, his subjects utter snorts of
scorn and derision. The Tasmanians don’t even
allow their Governor uncustomed liquor as is the
vogue on the Australian mainland. If The Bul-
letin were Governor of Tasmania under present
conditions it would have a large signboard
erected at Hobart Government House gates bear-
ing conspicuously the inscription : “Boarding-
House for Governors. Terms will be supplied
by the private secretary.”
* * * *
In Tasmania the Commissioner of Terri-
torial Police is also, metropolitan Police Magis-
trate. Thus it happens that the man who is the
guiding spirit of trie prosecution has to adjudicate
SYDNEY EYESORES AND EARACHES.
“I shall soon be at home over there."
upon the trustworthiness of the evidence and the
manner in which the police do their duty.
Alleged result : the territorial police bringing a
case before the metropolitan P.M. are uncon-
sciously favored by him, while the city policeman
has to prove his case right up to the maker’s name.
Whatever the actual result, the trouble looks like
a shadow of the “ System,” and badly wants some-
thing done to it.
* * * *
“ When I travel, I put on a black necktie,
because otherwise the railway porters are so in-
attentive. ” —Parson.
The Adelaide doctors are now trying to
persuade the medical men recently engaged to help
Drs. Napier and Ramsay Smith at the hospital
to come out on strike. They are also trying, with
the help of the Register, to foment strife among
the nurses.
* * * »
Last year t Adelaide hospital cost Govt.
£14,890 ; the public subscriptions amounted to
<£734 only. Yet the Register and the medical
profession have the cheek to ask that there shall
be no political control of the institution.

Signs of an agitation in Queensland for
having special railway carriages “ reserved for
colored people ” —black, brown, copper, yellow,
piebald, and skewbald. The white person in
Leperland can’t well afford.) to pay the same fare
as the colored gentleman.

A recent horse acci-
dent in King-street,
Sydney, attributed to
the sudden emergence
of a barrister’s grey wig
and flying gown, has
caused several people to
think out ways of pre-
venting such trouble in
future. The best way,
of course, is to abolish
wig and gown—though
even then some barris-
ters’ faces would scare
timid horses. The next
best way is to make the
men put on and take off
the properties within the
precincts of the court.
Several accidents have
happened in London by
reason of robed barristers
crossing from the Temple
to the Law Courts ; but
some time ago the County
Council took the trouble
in hand and cut an
underground tunnel be-
tween the two points
for the use of the scare-
ful apparitions.
» *
A Sydney man of
means and a suburban
property holder is mainly
responsible for the local
street barrel-organ nuis-
ance, writes a Bulletin
correspondent. He owns
something like twenty of
these instruments of tor-
ture, and lets them out
to the halt, the lame,
and the blind, at 6s. per
day ; and has in his em-
ployment a few men and
boys as regular hands, to
whom he pays a pound
and 15s. a week respec-
tively. The latter, of
course, have to make a
satisfactory return to
justify their wages.
Among the tribes of the Orient up Bris-
bane way, there are three factions, each one hating 1
the other two as only Easterns can. Smuggling*
is part of their business, and they inform on each
other with great enthusiasm. The other day
matters culminated in general assault and battery.
In the police-court cases that followed at least four
interpreters were required, and the unfortunate
P.M. had 6 Pidgeon }i English fired at him for
seven solid hours.
* * * *
The Anglican Bishop of British N". Guinea
is to be guaranteed <£loo a year for five years, and
until the guarantee is perfected Primate Smith
won’t pass the apron. Now, what does a bishop
want with £4OO a year among the niggers of
Papua? If he really wants to live among the
people <£loo a year would give him all local
luxuries. Besides, it’s a bit ridiculous to give a
Papuan bishop <£4oo while the Bishop of Armi-
dale must push along on half that.
NOT HER FAULT.
HE (just introduced): “Nice weather we’re having lately, Mrs. Kweene.”
SHE (sweet thirty-five) : “ Yes, the weather has been delightful. But lam not
married, Mr. De Smith."
HE (in a hurry to repair his mistake) : “ Well, I’m sure that’s not been your
fault.”
(And even now she doesn’t know which way he meant it.)
The humorless parson again. Brisbane
Courier advt.

» » » *
Queensland Government is putting up
£ 1500 to bring back 60 saddened Bananalanders
from Don Lane’s paradise in Paraguay. Forty
out of the 60 are children. One stranded pilgrim
has a wife and 11 offspring, which is a pretty large
encumbrance for a man to procesh with while he
goes round asking for work in a strange language
and in a land where the wages are next to nothing
paid in depreciated paper. A homeless mate with
11 children hustling for a job on a foreign strand,
where he is vaguely known as Don Smith, from
Australia, is the very incarnation of pathos.
♦ * * *
The confession of Phillips, who says that
he shot Brady, the recently-butchered Melbourne
bookkeeper, and then cut his throat with a dinner-
knife taken hurriedly out of the stock, and left
him dead in the warehouse, is an incongruous sort
of tale. Phillips, who was a weedy youth at a
microscopic salary, alleges that Brady, who had a
strong regard for the fair sex, led him into evil
habits, and he began to dissipate wildly on his 10s.
a week, and learned to go round every night with
the multitudinous Flossie. His wild extravagance
in this direction landed him in such embarrass-
ments that in the course of five years he became
.£1 short in his cash, and Brady harassed him
about this matter till the trouble ended in murder.
Brady himself had a salary of £4 10s. a week, so
it would seem that two men can have a great deal
. of wassail in Melbourne with £5 a week between
them, eked out by 20s. embezzled in instalments
spread over several years. The 205., Phillips says,
was mostly taken to buy an occasional clean collar
with which to meet the Flossie of the moment at
some street-corner. Therefore, it appears that if
he had been trained to wear a celluloid collar,
which can be washed at the sink, the tragedy
would never have happened.
The so-called confession teems with hysterical
references to “ successes with women ” ; this con-
tinual reiteration suggests a degenerate strain in
Phillips, for, purporting to be a repentant con-
fession of shame, it is, on the other hand, a
strongly-branded exhibition of diseased vanity
and self-conceit. Men who write showy confes-
sions of the kind are usually innate liars. A per-
fectly beautiful example bearing on which dogma
can be seen in a celebrated N S.W. confession of
a year or so ago.
The limelight document in question hardly
looks as if it was entirely written by Phillips him-
self, but seems to have been compiled by another
person from a story told him. And said story
would appear to have been subjected—however
unconsciously— to an amount of ornamentation and
doubtful interpretation. The present written
document is just such as to excite a diseased glori-
ficatory joy in a degenerate mind.
The Phillips confession is indeed a clumsy docu-
ment, and tends to show that a murderer should
neither edit his confessions himself nor let his
lawyer do it; but turn the job over to some smart
newspaper man, accustomed to calculating the
of written words upon the public mind.
When I first went to Cooper’s office I was a
bright, happy intelligent boy,” & c . What a
hideous png the confession makes out Brady’s
siayer to have been ? Little things like that dis-
gust the ordinary mortal, and often have more to
do with getting a man hanged than the actual
capital crime alleged against him.

The cruelties
of trade. It is not
uncommon on
Northern lines to
find a cattle-truck
packed with one
or two beasts over
the legitimate
number. Of
.course the super-
fluous weakling is
almost certain to
be trampled to
death before
reaching his des-
tinati o n. But
then he is still
available for
“ boiling - down ”
purposes, and it
pays better to boil down cattle (at present prices)

e proper number.

Some Kanakas in Bundaberg (Q.) fare-
welled Parson Eustace, the other day, by giving
him a gold ring and his wife a gold brooch ; also
this advice: “Do not go to Malayta ”—(he had
intended going there) —“ or you will be killed.
The Marat boys have determined to kill and eat
the first missionary who goes amongst them. Send
another missionary first and then, if you want to
follow, you will be safe ; because all of us like
you an d do not want you to be hurt. Good
old Kanaka Christians ! They’ve evidently been
told all about scapegoats, and King David s zeal
to try the courage of Uriah,

Crowned heads of Europe have always
fiven their royal patronage t© Ed. Pinaud, of
’aris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes
are now recognised as incomparably the best in
Europe. The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have
been “ Violette Reine ” (quite different from
“ Rhine Violet ”), and “Preciosa,” the sweetest
and most delicate perfumes ever made; in fact,
they are known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in Erance, and are
sure to be rushed by everybody.*

The other day the dead body of a swagman was found
floating down the Yarra. The corpse had a swag strapped
to its back and clutched a billy-can firmly in its rigid right
hand.
Swag upon his back,
Billy-can in hand,
He took the Outward Track
To Never-Never Land.
All of us must go
There, by land or sea—
How many will be so
Ready as was he ?
* * * *
One of the Nicest People, who—accident-
ally, no doubt —did not receive an invitation to a
Brisbane Governmentouse function, wrote His
Ex. a courteous letter expressing pleasure at her
exclusion. For she had thus avoided meeting
several notorious nlembers of the “ demi-blonde ”
who were present, by invitation. The local
dividing-line between the “demi-blonde” and the
elite being almost invisible, the poor Gov. is glad
that, for the present, his absence in Toowoomba
necessarily lessens such correspondence.
* * * *
Experienced reporters should be sent to
the police-courts ; juniors could copy the records
of the Supreme Court. The liberty of the subject
is in far greater danger in the lower than in the
upper courts, and yet the reporting strength is
sent to the latter and its weakness to the former.
In the lower courts law is trifled with and justice
travestied day after day. Glorified Bumbles miss
obvious points and potter into trivialities and im-
becilities, and only those of the public who are
present in the evil-smelling dens know anything
about it. The reporters are mostly satisfied with
copying out the official record.

X.Z. : “In a Melb. tram-car sat side
by side a priestess of Kali from Lonsdale-
street large, healthy-looking, attractive; a
priestess of the Salvation Corybantic cult-
small, hysterical-looking, anaemic, dowdy; and
an Archbishop—large, ‘ with fair, round belly
with good capon lined,’ for all the world like a
human edition of the priest’s horse. This text,
under three heads, preached its own sermon.”

Truly the domesticated female is cheap nowadays.

The influence of the Social Gimletists, or
some such objectionable institution, has blocked
the exhibition of the life studies at this year’s
show of the Melb. National Gallery students.
Half-a-dozen half-nude pictures somehow passed
the censor, but, as a general thing, the nude, or
semi-nude, has been rigidly excluded.

Old Government House, Parramatta Park,
has been leased by the park trustees to a lady for
seven years, the first five at a rental of =£so per
year and the remaining two at £75 per year.
“Old Govt. House” has not been occupied as
such since Dec., 1847, when Governor Charles
Eitzroy, in driving out of the Domain gates, upset
the carriage and killed his wife, Lady Mary
Fitzroy, and the A.D.C., Lieut. Masters.

The catechism circulated to 20,000 Yic.
public servants re private work is coming home to
roost laden with a weight of “No ! ” Barring
one or two who have absolutely declined to
answer, and one who wrote across the paper “ I
consider this an insult ! ” most of the civil ser-
vants notoriously-engaged in private work have
answered the awkward queries with a series of
more or less discreet lies.
Some of the private occupations that have been
pursued by public servants were such as would
constitute a good living in themselves. One ran
a lucrative bike factory; another a pub. with big
sweeps on the principal races ; while still another
went in for house-building contracts, and, more-
over, had Ministerial tolerance. There have been
several book-making ventures undertaken by
public servants, and it is on record that one
“curled darling” who made a losing book on a
Caulfield Cup blew his brains (sic) out.
A crowd of almost mendicant Church of
England parsons has been squeezing into the
Argus. The climax is reached by one who begs
for a paltry sum to pay for his life assurance.

A quiet little scandal is just working itself
out in N.S.W. Presbyterian circles. Details are
somewhat hard to arrive at, as the ‘ ‘ fathers and
brethren,” since a certain memorable episode with
a ewe lamb and a shepherd in it, only have dirty
linen washed when the doors are closed.
Pastor Edgar gave a limelight-show at
Wesley Church, Melb., the other night, of the
work of the Rescue Home. One picture was of a
picnic, with the ewe-lambs all joined in hands and
running round for a posy in the ring. Better than
the Inquisition. x

So Turner’s gone ! But need we all go, too ?
Succumb at last to laying-on of hands ?
The everlasting heavens still are blue ;
Still glow magnetic the far Outer Lands.
Let those who will still seek the self-sought tether,
Yet still some hear another life’s commands,
Still feel the heartstrings pull, and smell the
heather,
And swear the old oath, swearing all together,—
“ Brains, not brands I ”

MAYBE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I looked into a sweating-den last night
Where men and women slaved and suffered
bravely.
“Is this the way ? ” I asked a storeman gravely,
“My frank friend, Jones, became a man of
might ? ”
“ What! Jones ! the boss ! Oh, no, he’s reckoned
white.”
“He doesn’t know! This branch is run by
Wright.”
“ Oh, no !” (and slightly blazed that stolid store-
man)
“ The boss is white! —but we've a damn bad fore-
man 1 ”
And as I left, the earth-den rose in sight,
Its hosts of men and women slaving bravely,
And came the larger question still more gravely :
“ Does God—the great God—know how hard they
fight ?
Or some cat-hearted Deity—like Wright,
Unwatched—take from their sorrows wild de-
light ? ”
“ That’s so ” (some voice came echoing the store-
man),
“ The boss is white — but they've a d n bad fore-
man / ”

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Jan. 26, 1897.
My Dear Myee—
At the Vie. v. N.S.W. cricket-match on Satur-
day the most exciting scene of the afternoon was
the gallant attempt of Premier Reid to get a cup
of tea for Lady Hampden in the crowded tea-room
behind the new stand. Her Ex. was a model of
plain-dressing in black, and her escort was wear-
ing his_ auburn hair rather long; also, his Lorne
excursions had made him thinner. As every
woman on the stand seemed to want a cup of tea
at the same time as every other woman, and the
waitresses were few, wild confusion raged in the
cafe all the time. Cricket is a slow game to watch
when you don’t understand it. I heard one lady
explaining to another that the banked asphalt
cycling-track around the ground was made so that
balls thrown to the fence would roll back easily,
which made her apparent interest a little bewilder-
ing. On the whole, I agree with the indignant
Frenchman who sat out a match, had intricacies
explained, and rose impatiently, saying: “Bah
for your crickets. I mock myself at ’eem.”
A reception was held on Saturday evening on
board the huge German liner, Friedrich der
Grosse, on which several hundred guests held high
German jinks 60ft. above sea-level. To reach the
deck was like climbing a high mountain, and to
descend to the ladies’ cloak-room one plunged into
a long submarine street of state-rooms. There
is a vast quantity of gilding and splendor about
the whole ship, especially the ladies’ saloon, all
upholstered in brocade and adorned with scenes
from the life of the forbidding-eyed Fritz. Here
the wife of the agent, clad in pale blue, some
officers and uniformed foreigners, were standing
as hosts and nodding to as many as could get near
them. There was no name-shouting, and guests
found their way about the ship from explanatory
German remarks made by stewards. The Fried-
rich seems a beautiful ship to get lost in at sea ;
there are so many nooks and corners that the per-
centage of engagements ought to rank very high
each voyage.
The entertainment chiefly consisted of a band
and supper suggestive of the Fatherland. The
dishes were all invented in Germany and unex-
pectedly flavored with sauerkraut. I saw a girl
eating herring-salad with a puzzled expression
alternately with a piece of sweet cake. The gory-
looking dish was in great demand. The whole
company was determined to find out what Ger-
man cookery really is, and took egg-salad with
cheese-sandwich and lager-bier with everything.
There were thousands of brass-buttons and miles
of gold lace and many lobster coats glittering
around, so the men had it all their own way in
splendor. Some of the women neglected the
evening-dress command and kept on wraps. The
electric-light was very brilliant and glared merci-
lessly on all complexion-defects, even showing up
where the real hair left off and the false fringe
began.
The sightseers who rushed to see the German
jumbo, tied up to the Circular Quay on her arrival,
were more than pleased when a plump frau ad-
vanced to the bulwarks of an upper deck, dozens
of feet directly over their heads, and, while she
gazed curiously at the throng beneath her, dis-
closed to their delighted eyes a generous glimpse
of zebra-marked hose ! When an admiring “ Oh-
o-o-o-o ! ” rose from the assembled youths, the fair
immigrant leaned still further over the side, to
learn the cause of the mirth below—making
astounding revelations —whereupon another pro-
longed or more hilarious “ Oh-o-o-o-o ! ” greeted
the exhibition. The lady’s composure was serene;
but just here the awful truth burst upon her
phlegmatic spouse, and Herr Mein Gott made a
grab at the bewildered foreigner and hauled her
below.
An elderly dame was much scandalised, one
evening last week, on seeing a Jin de siecle couple
arrive, per wheel, at a fashionable cemetery.
Placing their machines against the door of a
family vault (for safety), they proceeded to the
grave of some dear departed (possibly the man’s
first wife), stood beside it for a decent space, with
lowered head and much wiping of weeping eyes—
remounted their bikes, and scorched back to
town 1
At the Palace of Varieties, the Winterton
sisters now come on in fresh-looking short skirts
of green silk all sprinkled over with pink starfish ;
the three Dele vines in the same turn are dressed d
la chappie with crushed hats, black knee-breeches,
and a cunning little white ribbon tied under the
left knee. The two girls wear a black ribbon in
the same place occasionally, revealed as they whirl
their skirts. The d.c. audience made a noble
effort at first to dress up to its surroundings, but
has given up the struggle, and now wanders in
each evening casually in the übiquitous blouse-
and-skirt, and the men just anyhow.
At a fashionable mountain resort last week a
local leader gave a picnic, for which large paste-
board invitations had been sent out for a week or
two. Every Nicest person invited turned up
expecting sylvan joy and great effects in catering.
But the gilt edge was completely rubbed off the
party when it was found to consist of a score of
fly-maddened revellers wrestling with extra-sized
mosquitoes in the interval of excitedly jumping up
from prowling bull-ants, and, for refreshments,
drinking tea out of pannikins and wrapping them-
selves around day-before-yesterday tarts and buns.
“A Woman’s Reason,” at the Criterion, con-
tains some pretty gowns and a satisfactory child
curled in the manner of such on the boards, but
natural and unaffected. A white Court dress with
plumes and regulation train makes Mrs. Brough,
as Nina, look young enough to wear that trying
tint by daylight. The real thing, as a matter of
fact, is quite ghastly and shuddery. Unclothed
white humanity under the cold gleam of a London
spring sun turns yellow and bony, and daylight
on evening-cut and tinsel makes 18 look 80. Nina
has also a blushing pink robe prettily swathed
about the bodice with black chiffon and gleaming
bands of jewelled gimp, and a tiny butterfly
bonnet. Hints as to sleeve-retrenchment may be
gained from the gowns ; they are all diminished
and flattened, and generally put down from the
high place they have held until lately on all the
robes ; in ball-dresses one wears arms au naturel,
with a bow on each shoulder and a bunch of frilly-
stuff half way down to the elbow. In day-frocks
there is a new stiffiness which gives a funnel-like
curve over each shoulder.
Many representatives of the noble army of
nodders were each filling his or her little seat at
the Criterion last Monday, which is a good night
to take the inquiring stranger to a city show and
point him out all the celebrities. A cultured
g;rson in the back-row brought in a decadent
odley-Head volume and read it absorbedly
between the acts. A bright-haired critic beamed
with interest on the palatially-arranged and
gorgeously-furnished mansion of the Ti'tberadge
manage. The porphyry columns are very real, but
I don’t quite grasp the why and wherefore of a
gilt-edged, plush-mounted nursery fireguard in a
millionaire’s best parlor.
On a crowded Sunday evening trip of a Manly
ferry-boat, a highly-dignified churchman was re-
turning to town after some special service. Near
him were sitting four elements of a real, thriving,
lively “push,” wh > beguiled the journey with
song, and their gi.ls clutched them round the
neck and joined shrilly in the chorus. The digni-
tary looked pained, and would have passed over
on the other side, only there the man with the
good voice was warbling “ Life’s Dream is O’er,”
with harmonious accompaniments. To hearken
to that sentimental lay, and then wonder hard
what on earth the words mean, puts in the time
nicely on a Manly trip. The churchman must
have noticed that everybody seemed to enjoy
comic songs more than hymns, and probably put
that down to the cussedness of human nature.
The feature of the fashionable Bong-Bong races
was a monkey. It belonged to a professional
“ spieler ” who had come to turn an honest penny,
which he did by getting up £1 raffles —ten shares
at two shillings each. The drawing of the lottery
was entrusted to the monkey, and the promoter
took the moderate commission of 20 per cent.
This little gamble became quite the rage and pro-
vided an excitement sadly lacking in the races
which formed the ostensible reason of the gather-
ing. The police, noticing the extent of the monkey-
proprietor's operations—he put through about .£OOO
m the two days—-were about to remove him from
the course, but when they beheld a live Governor
and a live Supreme Court Judge backing their
luck at the simian’s altar, they sensibly concluded
that in all probability the monkey and his box
and table didn’t constitute a “ place ” within the
meaning of the Act, and became deaf and blind
accordingly.
Take away the mother-in-law and the drunken
husband and the scolding wife and the undesired
twins, and the so-called comic-song collapses.
But sometimes a really great comic-song is born,
and the way it catches on shows that these
ditties “ fill a long-felt want. ” It must have some
real, permanent quality easily recognised, like the
splendid insolence of “ The Man that Broke the
Bank,” or the frank confession of being “bad”
that there is in the “Trinity Church,” or the
cynical pessimism of most of Coleman’s songs.
The newest comic-songs are grimly ironical, quite
unlike the vulgar riotousness of “Champagne
Charley,” for instance; most of them are in a
minor—there is a beautifully mournful phrase at
the beginning of “ I ain’t a-going to tell, the sort
of thing Marshall Hall would rave about were it
in a symphony. Comic-songs are the hymns of
Democracy. They voice the sordid, squalid troubles
of life, and the people listening recognise that the
singer is a man who doesn’t pretend to be any
better than they are —and applaud him in conse-
quence.
What a help a uniform is ! “Major ” Neild in
London was present at the distribution of prizes
for shooting, presented to the Tower Hamlets
rifles, and arrayed in his almost brand-new mili-
tary millinery, “ responded to an invitation,” and
addressed the assemblage. As plain “ Mr.”
Neild in a top-hat, frock-coat, and spats, he would
have been nothing, but an inch of cotton-wool
padding all around the inside of a scarlet coat—
an inch of cotton-wool padding is actually pre-
scribed in some cases by the N.S.W. military
regulations—gives courage as well as chest. That
tight, pincushiony effect is not attained by un-
assisted figure anymore than the artificially-fatted
stage calf. The row in Sydney just now about
the appointment as commanding officer is built on
the unquenchable desire of man for more uniform
and more chest-padding. The big struggle is who
shall wear the tin hat and feathers.
Nuns will consent to wear unbecoming uniforms
—but they are R.C. nuns. Pastor Edgar started
a Wesleyan sisterhood in Melb., and they have
quite lately doffed their cowls and taken to neat
felt hats. A feather or two will be added in time,
no doubt. Protestant sisterhoods always manage
to get something of the world into their attire,
and as they walk through the giddy, Binful throng
occasionally cast a glance of interest to see what is
going on in the male world. Shades of cloister
don’t fall on them so heavily as on the old, original
brand of brides of the church.
Girls who serve out food and drink to their
fellow man and woman for hire are divided into
three classes —“ladies,” “lydies,” and “ puffeck
lydies.” The new tea-room girl is always a
“ lady,” and therefore resents a movement being
forced on by the Brisbane (Q.) Worker to form
her into a union. The biz. is a superior sort of
bar-tending—barmaids are “ puffeck lydies ” —and
suits most of the damsels who deftly carry their
trays and a mild flirtation with favored men-
customers, most of whom are their partners at
swagger balls and “at homes ”in theevenings. Some
serve in tea-rooms for filthy lucre because papa
went bung during the depression, and many to
escape the dreary dullness of suburban respect-
ability. Most of the waitresses at the new cafe at
Sydney Cricket Ground are girls culled from
among Nice families.
The ’bus horses have been getting a friend to
agitate their wrongs in the daily papers. No
wonder ! It is horrible to see three poor, straining
animals tugging a tremendous load of fat men and
women up William-street hill under the lash. A
driver complains bitterly that it is the women who
make it still harder for the horses by stopping the
’bus every four or five yards to get m or out.
Even cable gripmen jib at pulling up every few
inches. One of them, I overheard, on seeing a
tenth parasol in one block waved at him, mutter :
“ Blest if they don’t want me to stop at every
house.”
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“The best-appointed dog-cart to be seen around
Melbourne is driven by the bachelor agent for
somebody’s soap, whose matrimonial attractions
have grown stronger than ever now that the other
handsome soap potentate, Mr. Francis I orrest, is
engaged to the youngest daughter of Tasmanian
Treasurer Fysh. Some two hundred spinsters,
calculating roughly, were on the lorrest track
until the news of his impending doom turned their
four hundred tearful eyes upon the Apollo in the
dog-cart. The competition for this obdurate sur-
vivor threatens to result in bloodshed. A small
army of girls may be expected to fall fainting
under his chariot-wheels at any moment if he
doesn’t give them a little encouragement, to go on
with. A hair apiece from the horse s tail would
be better than nothing.
“Mrs. Silas Harding, the .£30,000 a year
widow, will be missed by a number of needy out-
siders, as well as by sundry relatives of the late
Silas whom she has helped from the old man’s
money-bags, for if the unlucky lady died intestate,
as is understood, the miser’s leavings will revert
to her next of kin. A generally-received story
has it that Mrs. Harding captured her husband
whilst canvassing subscribers to a book, but the
truth is she made her famous haul in the capacity
of an attendant in one of the foreign courts of the
Melb. exhibition. Her charms, enhanced by a
fancy costume, were too much for the susceptible
widower, notwithstanding that she had an “ off ”
eye and suspiciously golden hair. Perhaps the
yellow tresses twined themselves round his heart!
Gold !
“Miss Muriel Bevan (daughter of the cherubic
Melbourne shepherd), w’ho went to Europe to
study music, expresses herself in good round terms
of dislike for Germany and the Deutschers. She is
disgusted with the manner iu which the men treat
their womankind. Miss Bevan will not stay in
the Faderland, anyhow, and unless she decides to
continue her studies in England she will return to
Melb. with her parents later in the year.
“ Dr. Neild, the Melbourne deputy city coroner,
and the old spoilt darling of the Austral Salon,
thinks seriously of writing an auto-biography.
The clever veteran has heaps of material for a
lively work, and if he only had the toughness
necessary to keep himself secluded from his bevy
of Salonites the volume might struggle into exist-
ence.
“Lady Brassey having raked in piles of cash
for the Blind Asylum, Mrs. Baronet Clarke now
starts to show that she also can lead charity on a
string by booming an Infectious Diseases Hospital.
As a matter of fact, she started the idea before
Lady Brassey made her bid for popularity at the
public expense, but the wife of the yachting
Governor, knowing a move or two, took the wind
out of her sails. Now the perky, plucky
bart.’s spouse bucks up and whips hard for sub-
scriptions for her idea. She’ll get ’em.
“ Antarctic-explorer Borchegrevink’s wife is the
ideal of what an explorer’s spouse ought to look
like. She is very tall and fair, with strong but
pretty features. She “ did ” Melbourne in about
three strides, clad (she, not the strides) in a silver-
grey travelling dress, with loose jacket, and a
jaunty, grey Alpine hat with a cock’s-feather in
it, and a pair of opera-glasses slung round her
shoulders. If her husband goes to find the South
Pole, she waits for him in Melbourne.
Yours affectionately, Cleo.
Eureka Creek (N.Q.) has furnished, in the
late David Alexander, a throw-back to the species
of miner whose favorite method of suicide was to
lay a dynamite plug on his tongue, light the
fuse, and await the seconds grimly until the top
of his head exploded.
Ellice group fishers, says Mr. Hedley, of
the Australian museum, have an ingenious and
successful bait—a mixture of cuttlefish ink and
European scent (or, in default of scent, kerosene)
painted over the hook. The little patch of dis-
colored water round it tricks the fish into the
notion that the hook is some diminutive succulent
squid.
Since the Federal question became the
immediate topic of the hour, every Sydney daily
paper has poured cold water on it more or less, the
Evening News alone excepted. In its leader of
January 25 the News lays down its views—which
are that the various provinces, despite local
rivalries, are Australian at heart or will learn to
be; that they can afford to trust each other ; that
there is no need for haggling over “guarantees”
to prevent them forming combinations to rob and
murder one another by the wayside; and that
when they are combined under a uniform system
all the various cities can hold their own well
enough by their own merits. Which is a dis-
tinctly sensible and practical idea. If Australia
can’t find trade and industries enough at all times
to keep five cities going then it is the poorest con-
tinent in this globe.
Another body, said to be that of one
Burgess, has been unearthed near Parkes (N.S. W.)
and it is also set down to the comprehensive Glen-
brook criminal. Two other men, said to have
been last seen in the company of that übiquitous
undertaker, are being searched for, and reports
continue to come in concerning persons who went
away with a mysterious prospector at various
tnnesand places, and have never since been heard
of. Ihe latest find had the usual bullet-wound at
the back of the head; apparently he had been,
like the others, delving at the hole which was to
be his grave, when his “ mate ” shot him from the
rear and then he dropped in and was covered over
m the usual way. No wonder this year’s wheat-
crop promises well in N.S. W.; the dead mate is a
wonderful fertiliser.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The VRC is this week considering the matter
of a regulation to secure the payment of jockeys'riding-
fees at scale. “Recent disclosures, says Melb. Age,
“show that the winners of some of the chief races on the
Vic turf have for a long time been unpaid their fees, and
it is well known that several jockeys have been put off
from time to time by their easy-going employers, despite
that the victory of their horses had enabled them to pay
the jockey liberally. There is more in the proposal, as it
affects the honesty of the turf than at first meets the
eye.” The Age might as well have mentioned that the
first “ recent disclosure ” ever published on this stinkful
subject was published in these columns ; also it was pub-
lished apropos to the “honesty (or dishonesty) of the
turf” in such a way that no eye which met it could help
perceivin'" the point. The Bulletin of Nov. 7, 1896, said
in “ Plain'English “ As for the true reason why cronk
runnin" is so general—viz., the inability of jockey-boys to
get the price of their riding-fees from anybody except
bookmakers, who will pay them to lose it is never even
hinted at in turfv columns. Many jockeys would starve
if they were confined to their legitimate professional
earnings seeing that their impecunious employers (nomi-
nal employers) don’t pay them anything for months at a
stretch. And, by the way, the V.R.O. might have the
candor to record on the minutes of Monday s committee
meeting the fact that the new rule re. jockeys' fees was
inspired, or at least hastened, by the brutal revelations of
a “ scurrilous Sydney newspaper.” Anyway, The Bulle-
tin will be glad to receive the blessing of a few grateful
jockey-boys. * * *
The Y.A.T.O. stewards, t’other Saturday, found
our old, young friend, Jimmy Hayes, guilty of “ malprac-
tices ” in connection with the running of two-year-old
Key, and disqualified him for four months. An unsatis-
factory word, as far as punters are concerned, but very
characteristic of racing government. “ Malpractices ”
presumably means something which the authorities know
of, but ain’t agoin’ to tell to the public.
Also, the ending of the Patricia scandal is rather
unsatisfactory. The V.R.O. have gone no further than
the excommunication of “ a well-known backer,” as though
the man in question were acting solely as agent for
himself in the bribery-and-corruption business. If he
played a lone hand in squaring the jockey and lessee of
the mare, and was simply clearing the way for another
animal that hehad backed, how came Patricia to retire from
even money to 10 to 1 in the betting? The policy of the
backer, the jockey, and the other man, under those circs.,
would have been to preserve the Patricia secret, keeping
the dead ’un favorite whilst the backer secured long odds
about his “ fancy.” Clearly the briber was working in co.
with one or more bookmakers, backing one candidate (if
he did back it) with the money milked from Patricia.
* • • •
That good but unlucky horse, Pharamond, has
been sent from Sydney to Tas. to run for Hobart Cup
next week, and, "if he be capable of beating his com-
patriot, Honiton, he should about win. Though the dis-
tance is rather far for him, he has only to meet “moder-
ates.”
* * * •
Sep. Miller’s most consistent and unlucky mare,
Ripple, got placed for just about the twentieth time with-
out winning on Saturday.
• * * *
N.Q. racing clubs (and the southern Q.T.C.
talks of following their example) have prohibited Ikey
Mo from droning his nasal chant on their racecourses.
First consequence : a dozen or so crack racers have
bidden the North an eternal good-bye. Will it strangle
gambling in the North ? Bah ?—will it abolish leprosy,
smallpox, Kanakas, tatnbaroora, Yokohama, sexuality and
Northern sin ?
* * • •
At all Yic. suburban pony meetings all the
ponies have to return to the trainers’ enclosure after their
preliminary canters. It is only after the final instructions
then given to the jockeys whether to gee or not that
heavy money goes on. Which proves that the man who
backs a pony when the betting opens is more than a fool,
and is bound to get broke.
* * * •
The two heavy Victorian winners over Quiver’s
alleged victory in the Viceroy’s Cup drew their money
the day after the Melbourne papers reported the false
result, and—such is luck 1—sailed for ’Ome a day later.
There was great joy in Jerusalem when the bookies had
also to part up on the real winner later on.
* * * •
Mr. Sam Hordern does not certainly bid for
favor with the racing public, whatever he may do in other
lines of business or sport. Last year in Challenge Stakes
the publio fancy, Cravat, was scratched at the last
moment, and now in the same race last Saturday similar
tactics were pursued re Miss Norah, who was the pick of
the public and a great favorite. The Skipper episode has
not been forgotten, so no wonder that the “ white and
blue spots ” are at present rather at a discount.
* * • •
A despised outsider in Talavera won the Chal-
lenge Stakes at S.T.C. Anniversary meeting at Rand-
wick, whilst a filly that has been running in selling
plates secured second money. The heavily-backed ones
finished “down the course,” much to the delight of Ikey,
who had a most successful day.
* • * •
At ’Ome they are not now taking any imported
Musket sires. Mr. S. Hordern’s Carbonado (Hotchkiss —
Onyx) recently fetched at auction but 40gs., which
wouldn’t pay his passage-money. Which should put an
end to the haphazard exportation of blood stock.
* * * *
Punters have had a very bad time of it lately in
N.S.W. Following close on the late scratchings of Cart-
ridge come the eliminations of Aurea, Wolluinumbi, and
Miss Norah from Challenge Stakes ; each having in turn
reigned as first favorite. Ante post-betting will soon be
killed at this rate.
* * * •

ing a good preparation for Liverpool Grand National
Steeplechase, and if he safely negotiates the awkward
brooks he should have a good chance of winning the
cross-country blue riband the first time of asking. Two
other Stralians, in Ebor and Norton, will also compete,
but they will find the distance rather too far.

Personal Items.
That ex-Governor Norman is “as poor as
a rat ” is the opinion of every sentimental Queens-
lander.
The won’t-be-knights of Victoria : Higin-
botham, Lalor, Service, Francis, Deakin, Murray-
Smith, perhaps Gillies, they say, and Berry is
sorry. Formidable list !
Justice and Chief-Justice —Simpson and
Barley—date their exacerbation of temper in each
other’s presence back to the days when Darley
was a Leg. Councillor.
A Queensland bishop regularly travels in a
carriage drawn by four ponies. And he often
leans out of the carriage and pleads pathetically
for a donation to the “ poor church.”
“ What’s in a name ? ” The real name of
the missing man Lesagh, supposed to be among
Butler-Harwood’s victims, is said to have been
Leichhardt. The lost Leichhardts !
Roehford, a New Guinea prospector who
has been reported missing, and is thought to have
lost the number of his mess, once owned the
defunct Daily Northern Standard (Townsville.
N.Q.).
After weary waiting, the Fletcher memorial
statue, to be erected in Newcastle, comes in sight.
The committee meet the last .£35 of the account
by putting their names down for £3 each. The
once puissant politician is well-nigh forgotten.
Mrs. Silas Harding was buried in Melb.
cemetery, and will have a monument “on her
own.” The old man shares a grave with his first
wife at Geelong, so when the two ladies meet on
the Awful Day they will require a formal intro-
duction to one another.
Anthony Forster, whose death is cabled,
was once editor and part-proprietor of S.A.
Register. He was M. P. for West Adelaide in
the ’Sixties, and one of Torrens’ best supporters
in his land-transfer reforms. Like Medway Day,
in later years, he was the Radical editor of a paper
owned by deep-dyed Tories. Wrote a history of
S.A., and only had one fault —a small vol. of
poems.
Q. Premier Nelson is locally dubbed
“ Nevermind ” Nelson, as a tribute to his fixed
profession that “nothing matters.” Next to
Tozer, Nelson is the easiest-going promiser and
the laxest performer of all the Q. Ministry. He
breathes a whiskyfied forgetfulness, and his capa-
city to sleep on undisturbed in a rowdy Assembly
has more than once provoked the jibes of Labor-
members.
Premier Turner is determined to stop civil
servants from writing for the press. In private
conversation, it was put to him that there might
be a man in the service with a special talent for
writing—a poet, for instance. “Well,” said
Turner (who never reads poetry or any such stuff),
“if he is so good at writing poetry, let him go
outside and earn his living at it, not take a salary
from the State while competing with outside
poets.” Turner evidently regards writing poetry
as something akin to growing cauliflowers or
fattening ducks.
WEISS, the Australian Billiard Champion.
Photo., Brand.
Pastor Bevan is off to Jerusalem. A bad
line : no trip makes so many sceptics. Familiarity
and contempt again. It is a good job we can’t see
Heaven —or the other place —until we die.

seller Frank Stuart, ex-Vic. M.L.A., as “ Frank
Stuart, the well-known Australian cyclist.”
The late George Harris, of Ultimo House,
Sydney, was the eldest of the rich family of that
name, and occupied, it is said, a peculiar position
with regard to the estate. He was a witness to
us father’s will, a circumstance which debarred
him from any legal share in his father’s bequest
±Sut the circumstance was not allowed to operate
against him, as the other members of the family
gave him an equal share.

Buchanan, the departing
pulpit-showman of Bris-
bane (Q.), should be even
more successful as a lec-
turer (he’s an Immigration
appointee of the Nelson

souled, bundle-of-nerves-

Assembly, and, ma con-
shens ! but ye’d lose yer
bet, if ye said there was
no a drappie even in the
vestry. Buchanan wouldn’t
do “ visiting ” work. The
elders (canny Scautch
bodies) had to find a help-
man to do that for him.
But didn’t he draw ? Just
like a man standing stark-
still in the street star-
ing at nothing ! And he brought the bawbees
into the collection-plates. He brought his kirk
out of debt and pit a bit intil its stocking. Ver-
dict of the elders: “Weel, weel; it’s the hond
o’ the Board ! He’s no a’thegither a bright an’ a
shinin’ example: but we dinna hae tae pit oor
honds intil oor breeks ivery now an’ agen! ”
Buchanan, too, has the prehensile aesthetic eye
