hensile aesthetic eye for—but this is anticipating.
Brisbane (Q.) Courier is
hugely advertising its ex-editor, C.
H. Buzacott, in his new venture
with the Rockhampton Record —
the bitterest anti-Brisbane scorpion
yet published.
A very prominent Australian
official has a most artistic glass
eye, which practically defies detec-
tion. The machine of State nearly
stopped short the other day, be-
cause a valet had carelessly emptied
the beautiful optic down a sink,
and the owner couldn’t appear in
public without it. He wasn’t pro-
vided with a spare eye, and it was
necessary to fish long and patiently
in the drain before the orb was re-
covered.
Frank Stuart, ex-M.L.A. for
East Melb., wept bitterly in spirit
when Premier Turner’s knighthood
arrived by cable. Stuart was hon.
member of the Munro Ministry at
the time that Turner was chosen
from the ranks to fill a vacant
billet. Langridge, the Minister of
Customs, had died, and Stuart,
reckoning himself the most desir-
able candidate for the portfolio, re-
tired from “ office ” in a huff when
he didn’t get it. Also, he quite
expected an earthquake to follow
his resignation. But the world
went on as usual, and Frank Stuart
was presently returned to his tailor-
ing establishment by the electors,
who had grown weary of him,
whereas George Turner came back
to the House a bigger man than
ever. And now this absurd inter-
loper of five years ago is Premier
and K.C.M.G., and looms up as
one of the biggest politicians in
Australia. The conviction that he
ought to be Turner, and Turner
ought to be nobody in particular,
is deep-rooted in Stuart’s breast.
Spiritist Dr. Peebles, now orating in Mel-
bourne, talks poor flapdoodle in his lectures. His
white hair and beard give him a sage-like appear-
ance, and he has a good platform style, but his
matter is washy, pointless rubbish. Besides, the
blow which the Mellon exposure gave to spiritism
in this country is too recent for any spookosopher
to make an impression.

MRS. WAYBACK (perfectly disgusted ): “ Oh, John, come away from
that horrid naked woman. What can you be thinking of?”
JOHN: “ I was — hie—just thinkin', my dear, what a lucky man her
husband must have been. No change in the fashion. No dress-
maker-bills to pay.”

Reading reports of
the bizarre questions
put to his visitors by
Li Hung Chang brings
up quite an uncanny
remembrance of the
late Parkes. Some of
his questions to de-
putations are now
famous—especially the
ones to clergymen (who,
by the way, were ever
his devoted admirers
and obedient servants).
It was a favorite de-
vice of his to fence
with awkward sub-
jects or demands by
asking ridiculous ques-
tions or quoting poetry.
Once he had a visit
from a crusty gentle-
man from Melbourne,
of musical proclivities,
who was trying to get
an answer about some
important business, the
object of his visit.
Parkes kept up a run-
ning fire of irrelevant
questions about books,
poetry, and told one or
two stories. The visi-
tor fidgetted about un-
til Parkes asked him
was he fond of music.
Touched upon his rul-
ing passion, he an-
swered: “Yes, Sir
Henry, lam. Passion-
ately ! ” “Do you play
hany hinstrument ? ”
said Parkes, graciously.
“Well, yes,” said the
stranger, modestly. “ I
play the flute and the
’cello a little.” “Oh !”
said Parkes, “ ’ave
you hever ’eard a duet
between a pianoforte
and a Jew’s ’arp?”
The visitor at once felt
for his hat, while the
old man grinned over
his victory.
Boy-murderer Phillips, Melb., is photo-
graphed in his surplice as a churoh chorister;
further zest is given to the picture by the fact that
it was taken only two months ago. A sweetly-
innocent, round, chubby face, the perfection of an
open countenance. Now then, ye phrenologers !
Deakin, M.P., who declined a knighthood,
once heard a friend declaring that some politician
who had been recently knighted had lost ground
politically thereby. Whereupon he replied :
“ While objecting to knighthood on principle, I
am not aware that a title is a disadvantage in a
Democracy.” This opinion, though philosophi-
cally probable, has not been confirmed by recent
Australian experience.
One eminent Melb. discounter can be got
at now and then with hopelessly cronk paper, if
the kite-flyer only exhibits a judicious admiration
of the 60 per shent man’s poetry—which, by the
way, is most awful poetry, many miles worse than
Fitzgibbon’s—or Isaiah Reginald Cohen’s novels.
It takes two or three months of well-managed
admiration to work the financier up, but when he
is worked up he will discount almost anything.
Anthony O’Grady Lefroy, who arrived in
Westralia half a century ago, when Col. Clarke,
father of the Vic. Agent-General, was Governor,
is dead, aged 81. In 1875 he was acting-Col. Sec-
retary, and subsequently held office as Col. Trea-
surer. He came of a high Tory family, one
member of which held the Chief-Justiceship of
the Irish Queen’s Bench, in the interests of his
party, when 90 years of age, and both deaf and
blind.
Ex-Treasurer Ward, of M.L., the man
who went under, is obviously recovering again.
His scandal has practically died out; at the recent
election his enthusiastic constituents returned him
by a big majority—Ward’s co. borrowed so much
money in the North to blew it in impecunious
Southland that it would be rank ingratitude if
they didn’t; and there is even talk of him resum-
ing the Treasurership, which is seemingly being
kept open for him.
Adelaide pressmen claim that they can ac-
curately report Gov. Buxton’s speeches before
they are delivered. They are mainly bun-struggle
orations, and embody a kindly reference to the
quality of the scones and the dreamy delights of
the tea, with a side reference to “ this indicahshun
of the divahne blahsings of Prahvidence,” and
conclude to the general effect that “ this bahzaah
exemplifies the fact that we live under a graht
and glahrious Queen ” !
Fred Weiss, who snatched the Australian
billiard championship from Memmott the other
week, has been always among the best two or three
native-born cueists since 1883, when he showed
himself much superior to Byrne in a match for the
Vic. championship. Nevertheless there was al-
ways a better man to beat him in test matches for
money until Memmott —the only Australian who,
up to now, has earned a billiard reputation in the
other hemisphere—finished 21 points behind him
in a game of 8000 up. Probably Weiss was for-
tunate in beating Memmott, all things considered,
but he did it, and did it very well, scoring 12
breaks of over a century (against Memmott’s 8)
and making the tallest one of the lot, viz, 232.
Such form as that is quite good enough to take to
England, where Weiss will shortly go touring on
his laurels.
“ YOUNG MAN , DON'T YOU FEEL WELL”
MR. DE BILLETY: “Ah, doctor, I feel as if something dreadful was going
to happen !”
EMINENT PRACTITIONER: “ Very likely; you are going to consult a quack.”
On the last hot Saturday a Melb. lady,
cycling along the road from Sorrento, observed
biking in the other direction a disreputable-looking
old man on a very muddy machine. The old
fellow had his coat off and tied around the handle-
bars with a reed. His face was red where it
wasn’t dust-coated, he was smothered in dust
from head to heel, and he was scorching, head
over the bar, evidently to catch the boat to Melb.,
having ridden round the coast. The dusty old
party was Hartley Williams, Victoria’s senior
judge. “No wonder,” said the lady, “he wasn’t
made Chief Justice.”

MELBOURNIANA.


THE BRIG PHYLLIS.
Melbourne, Wednesday.
Ttio.se im Ixiard the brig Phyllis, which was re-
cently succored at great peril by a boat’s crew from
th? Orient, are still having an exqiting time in their
endeavors to reach Melbourne. Captain Prideaux,
of the steamer Glaucus, which arrived here to-day
from Kembla, reports that when passing Cliffy
Island he saw signals hoisted denoting a ship in
distress to the westward, and at once bore down on
the vessel, which proved to be the brig in question.
It was then, ascertained that those on board the
Phyllis were in want <sf provisions, and a quantity of
food was placed on board by a boat from the
Glaucus, the steamer afterwards proceeding on its
way to Melbourne. Captain Prideaux states that
the crew of the brig presented a half-famished
appearance.
AN OCEAN TRAMP: THE NEWEST TERROR OE THE DEEP.
SKIPPER OF BRIG PHYLLIS (to any ship he meets): “Ship ahoy! Have you got such a thing as a biscuit?”

POLITICAL
POINTS
Does Pastor Fitchett stir up enmity
against France with an eye to the New Hebrides ?
The Windeyer clan is, it is stated, feeling
its way to set up one of its number in oppo-
sition to R. A. Price for Gloucester, at next
N.S.W. general election. The Windeyer estate,
Tomago, is in the electorate named.
“ Since the 1896 elections two or three
members of the Labor-party have evinced a strong
desire to get on the Treas ury benches, no matter
how, even in a Ministry led by such a person as
Boodlewraith’s Tozer.”—Brisbane (Q.) Worker.
A parliamentary candidate (Tas.) : “I am
opposed to the metropolitan drainage scheme. It
is only kept alive by Mr. Fysh to provide a billet
for his pet lamb, another of the sucking calves of
the State.” The candidate spoke from the seat of
an antediluvian Irish jaunting-car, very appro-
priately dug up for the occasion.
A brainy and strictly practical N.S.W.
Federal candidate is J, T. Walker, of the Bank of
N.S.W. He is almost the only one so far who has
definitely suggested that the Australian people
ehould trust their Australian Parliament, and not
regard it as a dangerous animal that must be kept
perpetually chained up lest it should break loose
and bite itself. Also, he is almost the only one,
bo far, apart from the Labor candidates, who is
neither a lawyer nor a M.P.
LIVE AND LEARN.
GUSSIE (from Sydney, to ox-conductor beating a bullock): " My good
man, my good man, why evah are you chastising the poor cow in
that mannah?”
STOCKMAN: “ Just knocking the bloomin' milk down — it’s bunged-up some-
where.”
GUSSIE: “ Gwacious; weally; beg pardon, old fellah. Thought you were
rebuking the poor cweatyah. Ah, well, live and learn, live and
learn. ”
It is highly desirable that the Australian
provinces should have a sound Federal Constitu-
tion. Hitherto it has been pretty well all sound.
And if Dan. O’Connor’s candidature be successful
there will be a good deal more sound still. Also
National spirit.
Brisbane (Q.) Worker advises the Q.
Labor-members to stonewall next session all
Ministerial legislation until the Govt, brings in an
Electoral Act Amendment Bill recognising the
“ one-adult-one-vote ” principle. The Boomerang
gave similar advice five years ago.
The Hobart and Launceston experiment
in voting according to a modified Hare system
worked out fairly well. At Hobart 2849 votes
were polled, of which 104 were informal. At
Launceston, of 1827 votes, 58 were informal. This
gives an average of something less than 3| per
cent, of incorrect ballots; which, considering the
fact that Tasmania is the worst educated of the
seven sisters, does not look unreasonable for a first
attempt.
Of the 37 members composing the new
Tas. Parliament the Braddon-Fysh Govt, claims
18 as followers, and the Opposition 12 ; the Inde-
pendents number 7. There is a bare possibility
of a change of Ministry, but the Govt, alleged
policy and that of the Opposition are so much
alike that any change made must orisrinate in
purely personal differences, or from t!.remote
chance of the Independents combining to shake
matters up.
When the Vic. Companies Act was passing
through its last Parliamentary stages, it was pur-
sued to the very end by the malediction of that
sound Tory, G. D. Garter, M.P., who declared
that no respectable man would care to be a direc-
tor under the new order of things. The first
bank balance-sheet published in the rigid form
prescribed by the Act was that of the Bank of
Victoria, with the name of G. D. Carter thereon
as director. So, presumably, Carter has thought
better of it —or else he isn’t respectable.
Wanted, the name of the N.S. Wales M.P.
who is so conscientious that he has paid a sum to
the Railway Department to cover the value of
journeys made by him by rail while on private
business. That member is really too honest to live
in a country like Australia. Consider the compli-
cations into which the lily-white conscience of the
sensitive creature may lead him. Suppose, one
day, when he is at home and only feels half in-
clined to attend Parliament, his wife tells him he
must go to town and buy a pound of bacon, and he
goes into town and attends Parliament after all
(which he wouldn’t have done but for the bacon-
commission), what allowance will he make the
State ?

Office reticence re Bren-

The little band of
Vic. Freetraders, which
is mainly Max Hirsch
and Murray Smith, per-
petually implores that
province to give up
nearly £1, 000,000 of Cus-
toms revenue gained in-
cidentally by Protective
duties, in order to enjoy
the great blessing of im-
ported Shoddy. The
reason why Hirsch and
co. are never likely to
succeed is that no one of
them has the courage or
the brains to explain
how this lost sum is to
be made up. N.S.W.
fills the gap principally
with its land - revenue,
but Vic. has no land-
value worth speaking of.
The gap could be filled
by raising the income-
tax to about 4s. in the
£, or by confiscating the
rents of practically all
the lands in the pro-
vince—in other words,
by land-nationalisation
without compensation.
But it would be a party
of considerable energy
that would openly face
either alternative, and
the Hirsch-Smith crowd
isn’t equal to it.
The N.S.W. delegates to the Federal Con-
vention should be : Bishop Smith, Cardinal Moran,
the Moderator of the Presbyterian Church, the
chairman of the Congregational Union, the presi-
dent of the Wesleyan Conference, the chairman of
the Baptist Assembly, Commandant Booth, the
secretary of the Social Purity Society, the presi-
dent of the W.C.T.U., and G. E. Ardill (the
wholesale and pious philanthropist). No country
can possibly progress except on broad religious
lines.
According to a list of Sydney prices pub-
lished in the Daily Telegraph on Ist January,
the removal of the duty of 6d. per bushel on wheat
has been accompanied by a rise of 6d. in the price;
•£1 a ton duty was taken off flour, and it went up
£2 10s. ; 3d. a gallon was taken off kerosene, and
it only went down l^d.—so the revenue loses 3d.,
which has to be taken from the people again by
some other form of taxation, while the consumer
only gains half that sum ; 3d. duty was taken off
China-oil, and the price only went down 2d ; 3d.
duty was taken off castor-oil, and the price went
up lid. ; £3 duty was taken off white lead, and
the price only dropped £2 10s. ; and £2 taken off
ordinary galvanised-iron reduced the price only
■£l 10s. In all these cases it would seem that =£l
collected at the Customs-house cost the consumer
much less than 205., though the same paper pub-
lishes articles by the guttural Hirsch on the
Horrors of Protection, in which it is alleged that
<£l collected as customs revenue costs the con-
sumer somewhere between ,£1 11s. and £5. Why
this vast discrepancy ?
“ MADE IN GERMANY”: A sketch on the arrival
of the s.s. Friedrich der Grosse.
To reform the Australian Upper Cham-
bers : turn them into lethal chambers for half-an-
hour, and then sweep them out.
Q. Socialist League has determined to
counter the Nelson project of immigration
from Great Britain by arranging that a state-
ment of Queensland’s “ true position ” shall
“ appear in all the important socialistic and demo-
cratic journals of Great Britain.”
Apropos of the jeremiads about the pros-
pective downfall of various Australian cities if
Federation comes to pass, and about the way the
Federal capital will mop up the trade and popula-
tion of other centres, the experience of Maoriland
is interesting. In the early days, M.L. had six
provincial capitals. Auckland had a short turn
at being the capital when provincialism was
abolished, and then, 30 years ago, the seat of
government shifted to Wellington, while Auckland
was left to perish. Thereafter- the latter place
proceeded to boom, and now it is the biggest
centre in M.L., with Dunedin second, Christchurch
third, and the windy capital only a good fourth.
The N.S.W. Protectionists who have
spoken on Federation up to date are a disappoint-
ing crowd. Dibbs is against Federation on large,
general, damnchicago principles. Lyne is vaguely
hostile and non-committal. R. E. O’Connor
wants the two most important items—the rail-
ways and public debts—left out. Copeland does
likewise, and goes one worse by boosting up the
candidature of Cardinal Moran, and proposing
that there should be no uniform franchise but
each province should elect its representatives any
way it pleases, so that the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment will be about the only legislature on earth
with no power to reform its own franchise. T. M.
Slattery, judging by his remarks in the correspon-
dence-column of the D.T., regards federation of
the railways and public debts as an open question.
So far, the Labor-party in N.S.W. seems far
more promising than either of the other crowds.
“Federation is in the air.” You don’t
say ? Queensland complains that Australia wants
to rob her of her rights and make her parochial.
Central Queensland complains that South Queens-
land has robbed and is robbing her of her rights,
and has made her sub-provincial. West Central
Queensland complains that Central Queensland is
worse to the West than ever the South was to the
Centre. Result: Queensland is pulling away
from the rest of Australia; Central Queensland is
pulling away from South Queensland ; and West
Central Queensland is raising the hatchet to cut
the ligature that binds it to East Central Queens-
land. Add to this that the North, the bad old
North, has always been clamoring for “its own,”
and you complete the present picture ; while there
is the prospect that, in the near future, “the
Gulf,” with Normanton as its head, will be singing
to Townsville, Port Douglas, Cairns and Cook-
town —“ We don’t want to play m your yard.”
The very Port Darwin store-
keepers, who were lately urging their
M’sP. to pass the Colored Immigration
Restriction Act, are now asking Govt,
to waive its provisions in order to
admit of the importation of Chinese
carpenters from Singapore. Govt.,
however, does not consider the cy-
clone a sufficient excuse for the employ-
ment of Chinese cheap labor, and is
forcing the Port Darwin folk to be con-
sistent by sending up white labor
from Adelaide.
Because of a visible rift between
some of the Q. Labor members and
the advanced socialist section, Bris-
bane Courier editorially and patron
isingly advises the former to abandon-
“ socialism in our time,” to “join the
Ministerialists (!), and, in that alliance
(1 !), to share in doing better work
than has yet been done by Parlia-
ment since self-government was aston-
ished.” Small wonder that editor
Ward’s friends are so frequently con-
cerned about his health.
Ex-Premier Dibbs says that he
will not be a candidate for the Federal
Convention. Has any one heard any-
thing of a demand for Dibbs in such
a capacity? Also, considering a re-
cent speech in which the long ex-re-
publican denounced Federation as a
means of finding billets _ for hungry
politicians, and Federationists as a
crowd of conspirators against the
blessed Empire, would any person who
knew the circumstances be such a great
particular lunatic as to give Dibbs a
single vote? Dibbs may not be a
candidate even though he says he
won’t, but if he is one it is the duty of
every Federationist to leave him care-
fully at the bottom of the poll. The
reason why the bottom of the poll is
the right place for Dibbs on such an
occasion is that there is no place lower
down in which to plant him. Let us
now proceed to damn Chicago.
Although there was a wild extravagance in
the Gympie (Q.) Truth’s recent supposititious next
Tozer Ministry, the abundance of smoke warrants
the belief in a fire. Apparently, Tozer is scheming
to form a third party, and is taking full advantage
of Premier Nelson’s and Attorney-General Byrnes’
absence in (or towards) Tasmania. Nelson will
soon resign despite all entreaties, and is likely to
recommend Gov. Lamington to send for Byrnes.
But Byrnes has made himself disliked. If he is
sent for, the splits will definitely shape themselves.
“Mines and Railways ” Philp’s appointment as
Acting-Treasurer is the one clever move on Nel-
son’s part in this connection. Philp is not self-
assertive, but he is liked, and can command a
following, while the Labor-party regards him as
almost neutral in its fight against Fat Man.
Leischeit, or Leichhardt, hitherto known as Lesah,
or Lesagh. He is believed to have been mur-
dered and buried by Butler, alias Harwood,
the alleged assassin of Lee Weller and Preston.
In the Tas. general election 14 members
were returned unopposed. For the 23 contested
seats only about 11,000 voters recorded their
wishes. Result: Less than 7000 of the 50,000
1 asmanian male bread-winners have elected the
new Parliament. Withal, seven brand-new mem-
bers have been sent in, indicating that if all Tas.
electors cou.d be kept awake long enough to record
then votes, the old members would have been
totally abolished and a new lot sent in to battle
with the House of Landlords. And, as the new
members are decidedly more democratic than
their predecessors (three of them owe their elec-
tion to their endorsement of the Labor platform),
i really seems that Tasmania is not quite so sound
asleep as it used to be.

The Bulletin is assured, with reference
to a recent paragraph, that Mr. Walter D. White,
late secretary of N.S. W. Public Works
is in no way related to Mr. Charles E. Lyne.
President Lackey, of N.S.W. Leg. Council,
is suggested as a Federal Convention candidate.
You might as well put up the defunct Garden
Honey. Mr. Lackey is politically dead. In fact,
he never was politically quite alive, and his light,
such as it was, went out for ever when they gilded
the lily by adding the unnecessary Sir unto the
Lackey.
J. R. Fairfax, of S.M. Herald, threatens
to put on the red cap of revolution. The
public cut up the road leading to his private
oss V a l e , and Mister James demands
ttiat the local council shall repair the damage.
Ihe council says it won’t, so James threatens to
block the public off. If he would keep a man on
the road reading aloud a Herald “leader” the
public would quickly take another way.

Indisputable Facts.
According to our Insurance Standard 50 per cent,
of authors, 42 per cent, of clergymen, 30 per cent,
of lawyers, 27 per cent, of teachers, and 24 per
cent, of doctors, reach the age of 70 years. Many
explanations have been offered for the longevity
of intellectual men. Having experience as well as
intelligence, they understand the science of pre-
serving health by avoidance of those acts of care-
lessness and neglect which tend to shorten life.
By an intelligent diversity of pursuits, taking re-
quired rest, and by a careful regimen, one helps
to keep the body in good condition. It is know-
ledge how to live and how to quickly check the
first approaching signs of ailments, wtiich prolongs
life. Mr. John W. Walsh, of the Australian
Widows’ Life Assurance Co., 263 George-street,
Sydney, N.S.W., says: “I suffered very much
from chronic indigestion for the past four years.
After returning from a trip to Adelaide, say fifteen
month ago, I met an old friend of mine, Mr. I. D.
Nelson, of Pearl Bay, who advised me to try War-
ner’s Safe Cure, as both he and some members of
his family had been suffering from a similar com-
plaint, and had been completely cured by it. I
tried about six bottles, and am pleased to tell you
I feel now completely cured, and will always have
the greatest pleasure in recommending it to my
numerous friends throughout the colonies.”*

The utter unfitness of the Melbourne Cricket
Ground for cycling has been demonstrated so frequently
that there is no longer any excuse for the organisers of
meetings, and now such should certainly be held respon-
sible for accidents, and stand in readiness for a man-
slaughter trial in case of a fatality. The L.V.W., as the
governing body of Vic. cycling, would naturally be
expected to set a good example, but, unable to withstand
the prospects of a big gate, it held its annual meeting
there on the two last Saturdays. A sprinkling of rain
fell each day, with the result that the best man won in
scarcely a single race, and the majority of heats consisted
of tangled heaps of broken bikes and hairy legs massed at
intervals along the track.
During every bicycle-race dozens of collisions
occur—where handles or pedals strike without doing any
more harm on a decent track than altering a competitor’s
course by a couple of inches. But put the men on a flat,
grass surface on a wet day, and the slightest touch causes
a skid and the fall of one or more.
The League Cup final was a dreary fiasco. Up
to the middle of the last lap there was every prospect of
a great finish, when second, third, fourth, and fifth fell
in a heap, and allowed L. Barker, on 240, to win by a
street. The probabilities are that Barker would have
annexed the prize anyhow, but he would have had to
battle hard with Taylor, one of the fallen. The winner is
the 20-year-old son of Mrs. Barker, proprietress of Mel-
bourne’s boss pork-shop. Last September, Joe Royal, the
trainer who first brought Parsons to the front, picked
Barker out as the most promising of the season’s crop of
novices, and he has had him in hand ever since. Royal
prophesies great things for the lad.
Parsons started in the same event, and, though
he was vaguely understood to have recovered his lost
form, he ran badly and finished nowhere. Parsons’ light
is out.
The best race of the day was the 25-mile cham-
pionship, for which 41 riders tried, though it was divested
of much interest by Martin, Walne, and Lesna, the
Frenchman, who made his first appearance in Australia,
falling ere six miles were turned off. Grantor did a mar-
vellous amount of pacing, and, though one by one the
competitors dropped out, R. Johnstone, with a huge
ironical 1 on his back, rode last until a mile from home.
Then he made his rush, but couldn’t stay it out, Porta,
Gargurevich, and Kellow passing him at the bell. The
scene then was the most exciting since Mai. M’Lean
beat Martin last season. Though it was a quarter past 6,
the crowd, which had stayed to see out the race, jumped
crankily in the air, yelling for its various champions.
“ Gargy ” made a great effort in the last 50yds., but the
Italian shook him off and won by half-a-iength. The
ground was rushed, cheering was kept up for fully five
minutes, and the grinning Porta was carried in.
One thing is certain : with his recent rapid
improvement Porta stands on a level with the best. The
next handicap will see him on the same mark as Bil-
martin.
Little Gargurevich, scarcely sft. 3in. but
splendidly proportioned, would have been just as popular
a winner as Porta. “Garfish” deserves a big win; he
has never won more than a £lO prize in his life, and very
few of them, yet he is a first-class man, and is handi-
capped accordingly.
Ken Lewis, who leaves for England in March,
is to be accorded a benefit meeting. Lewis is a native of
Kyneton, Vic., but,’ on the strength of having spent a
year in M.L., he is boomed by all sports promoters as a
Maorilander.
A man who deserves a benefit if any cyclist
does is David Kirk, a first-class man, but one who always
had luck against him. Kirk fell while training in Nov.
last and, injuring bis spine, has been in Melb. Hospital
ever since. He will never ride again—and, it is feared,
will never quite recover. Now that the boom is on to
overlook Kirk’s claim would be a shame.
* * * •
There were 32 falls at Saturday’s L. V.W. meet-
ing on the M.C.C.G.
® * * * *
S.M. Herald advocates the adoption of the
Viennese (Austrian) system of preventing bike smashes,
by which every cyclist must pass an exam, in pro-
ficiency. Those who pass are supplied with a diploma
bearing their photos. Those who don’t are forbidden to
ride in the streets till they do. Pretty in theory ; but the
man who usually comes to grief is not the novice, but the
other fellow, who, having mastered his machine, begins
to put on “ side,” to carry a portmanteau in one hand, and
lift his hat to strange women with the other, and to scoot
at full speed among trams and ’buses while watching a
dog-fight over his left shoulder.
* * * *
The first day of N.S.W. v. Vic. was in general
a day of good bowling, poor batting, poor fielding.
N.S.W. began the alleged batting by Donnan hitting a ball
on his wicket first thing, by Mackenzie poking about half-
an-hour for 0 runs, by Kelly getting caught second ball,
by Iredale playing very badly and doing his utter worst
by running Gregory out. The only N.S.W. batsmen to
shape against Roche and Trumble were Noble and
M’Kibbin. Noble brilliantly cut and drove all the Vic.
bowling, but was missed by Carlton and Trumble. Ire-
dale was missed three times until, with his score at 22,
Roche swept his bails off with a fast break-back. The
only man who scored without giving a chance was
M’Kibbin. Had half the chances been taken N.S.W.
wouldn’t have reached 100 runs. As it was, the first
innings totalled 159, of which the colt, Noble, scored 71.
The hepo of the innings was this youngster. He
went in facing a rot, facing confident bowlers who had
sent the first four of the N.S.W. batsmen back to the
pavilion for 23 runs. The first ball from Trumble just
missed the off stump, but the colt sent the second to the
fence, and from that on the limber 23-year-old played a
plucky, aggressive game, stopped the rot, and showed his
side that although Roche and Trumble could “ do a bit”
on the good wicket, their bowling could be smitten.
Noble is the youngster who made that fine 152 (not out)
against Stoddart and Co.
Roche, who got 6 wickets for 63 first innings,
is a red-headed young man of medium stature. Having-
lost his first and second fingers and part of his thumb, he
is a bowling curiosity. He turns his slow ones well from
the off, and he shoots down quite a first-class fast one at
judicious intervals.
The start of the Vic. first innings was inaus-
picious enough, especially as the Vic’s had the fourth
wicket in front of them. There was a howl from the
crowd when M’Leod hit M’Kibbin on and one wicket was
down for 9. M’Leod walked back hanging his head down
in disgust and carrying one hand in his pocket. Graham,
who followed, pretty well knocked the cover off some of
Bilowell’s balls, stepping in to the young farmer and
driving him with great dash. Bruce soon left, so Graham
and Worrall, each of whom had made a century the
Saturday previous, partnered each other. Graham, how-
ever,’went out for 17, and had a slice of luck in getting
them ; and Worrall must needs step in and make a creaser
of a ball and retire for 15. Four for 68; but Trott and
Laver stayed the procession.
* * • *
Bruce batted out of form first innings for his 16
runs. Bruce is none too popular with the Sydneyites;
he is rememb'ered as the subtle logician who, when pick-
ing the last Aus. Eleven, threw out Lyons because the
English season might be wet, and Turner because the
English season might be dry.
Though many misses occurred, a few catches
reached exhibition standard. Trott, at point, fielding
with all his old cleverness throughout, caught Garrett
high up left hand —the ball would have struck the
boundary. M’Leod ran a long way to catch Turner off a
very hard drive ; his speed and catching effort brought
him down, and he rolled over and over with the ball in
his hand. Johns nipped both Noble and Coningham off
fast and bumpish balls from Trumble. Johns kept wicket
handsomely first innings—Kelly’s work afterwards was
amateurish by comparison. What a mannerism Johns
has of throwing his hands up whether he gets the ball or
not 1
Everyone knows how Graham can hit. But
M’Kibbin took Graham’s hardest off his own bowling—the
day’s sensation it was. Coningham caught Bruce off
M’Kibbin in the slips, took a fast low ball left-handed
with the deftness and ease of a juggler’s trick.
The men whom the Sydney crowd greeted most
cordially were Trott, Gregory, and Coningham. Trott,
on coming out to bat, received a tremendous welcome ;
he had to keep taking his hand out of his pocket and
raising his cap all the time of his march to the wicket. It
was the heartiest and most sincere welcome that a player
has received from a Sydney crowd for many a long day.
The ovation par excellence was when Gregory
made his historical 201 against Stoddart and Co. Gregory,
being the midget, receives spontaneous recognition
everywhere ; when Syd. used to drive big Richardson to
the boundary in England the crowd would cheer itself
hoarse. By the way, the little man used the one bat all
through the recent tour, scoring some 1700 runs off it.
Cricket has now and then a tincture of humor
in it. When Turner went on to bowl last Saturday he
sent down four preliminary balls with professional
solemnity ; then carefully measured his run by stepping it
out beforehand ; then marked himself a starting-point
with his foot; grasped the ball tightly, raised his arm
aloft, ran up and delivered. Four ! Trott rather likes
Turner, and it was the sweetest of drives this one. .
Coningham had bad luck and the sympathy of
the crowd on Saturday—three catches missed off him in
two overs, for two of which Iredale’s yarning in the slips
was responsible.
The time of excitement was when N.S.W., 110
runs behind, came out for the second innings. Would
the home team make a fight, or would the first innings
rot—4 for 23—repeat itself? The brunt of the battle was
borne by Gregory and Donnan ; they put on 70 runs for
the second wicket, and put heart into their partisans.
When Gregory was 35 Trott made a desperate effort to
catch him. It was a hard, fast chance; Trott got one
hand and then the other on the ball quite half-a-dozen
times before he lost it.
Graham is a wonderful shot from the field at the
wicket. Two or three times while standing against the
fence he threw- the ball in at lightning pace full on the
stumps, sending the bails flying. Once Gregory was
almost out to one of these sensational returns.
Johns’ English trip has seasoned his nerves ;
some of his work was artistically perfect—catching
Coningham and Gregory, for example. And he stumped
his man so quickly that the batsman’s stroke and the
flipping off the bails seemed one act.
The Victorian first innings was typical of
cricket. Worrall and Graham, each of whom had scored
a century the Saturday previous, were sent back for 15
and 17 respectively ; four were down for 68. Then Trott
and Laver put on 175, when Trott fell to a catch, which
went straight into Gregory’s hands in the most convenient
style. And then, without another run being scored,
Laver marched back after his captain, his agricultural leg-
scoop missing a fast leg-break from Coningham. Four
for 68, five for 243, six for 243, seven for 245, 10 for 269 ;
M’Kibbin in his last few overs getting four wickets for 7
runs.
Donnan came out with his teeth set for his
second innings, and, as he was eager to hit, Trumble gave
him off-theory. But Donnan wasn’t having any off-
theory ; be let them all pass, standing there with bat up-
raised, and Trott anxiously waiting at point.

Clem. Hill made 194, not out, for his club the
day after his return from Sydney.

A. H. Roberts, an Adelaide broker, is financing
the team of baseballers about to do America, although
the ropes are apparently being pulled in Melb. Roberts
is an old Norwood footballer, and made much money out
of Westralia.
* * * *
Charlie Samuels, the black ex-champion sprinter,
recently discharged from Callan Park Asylum, Sydney, is
to be deported to Queensland, his native province, at the
expense of the Aborigines Protection Board.
* • * *
Premier Turner refused his paternal permission
to his son taking part in bicycle races for money, but
Speaker Berry’s son goes for the dollars.
* * * •
“No one I ever saw,” remarks the veteran C.
Bannermann, “ could upset a side like Lyons. Let him be
in five minutes and be has tied his opponents in a knot.
I remember on one of the occasions when I stood umpire
for N.S.W. in Adelaide. Lyons came in ; took block ; hit
a four ; wiped his forehead ; hit another four ; re-wiped
his forehead ; hit two or three more fours which the
fieldsmen stopped for singles ; hit a fiver ; changed ends ;
wiped his forehead and hitched up his pants. I looked
up at the board thinking that he must be 20 ; he was 50 1
It was the biggest surprise I ever got in cricket.” Apropos,
Syd. Gregory relates that- once when Lyons was batting
in England, Gregory went from the pavilion into the dress-
ing-room for his pipe and coat. He put on his coat,
filled his pipe, lighted it, took half a dozen draws, and
came back. The game was over. The mighty smiter
had played the devil in those few moments on a bad
wicket.
» * * *
Eighteen-year-old Lane’s win of the 220-yards
amateur swimming championship in 2min. 46sec. was a
great performance for a youngster. Lane finished in
front of such men as Gormly and Hellings, and should he
keep a level head there should be a great swimming
future ahead of him.
* * * *
Ted Flack, the Australian crack harrier (writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent) has decided to
give up all competitive running and to concentrate his
energies on becoming an expert accountant and a pillar
of strength to the paternal firm. Excluding an occa-
sional sprint with the Thames Harriers, the only running
he does now is up tracks of figures. A natural runner,
not too robust physically, training added little to his
capacities of speed and endurance, and he would never
get beyond the third-class pedestrian division in England.
His defeat by a modern “Matoosekos” Greek at the
Olympic Games was a severe blow to his pedestrian pride,
which finally fell to pieces when King George of Greece
shook his hand in vigorous sympathy. The Swanston-
street fruit-sellers were at last avenged.

As pointed out by Mr. Thackeray, when the
big smelting works at the tidal lagoons, Lakes Macquarie
and Illawarra, are in full blast the fish will be banished
and the angler will know the lakes no more. It is already
necessary to set aside and stringently protect a few more
of the small coastal lagoons in readiness for the depletion
of the large ones. And Sydney Harbour itself has been
utterly spoilt as a fishing ground by the sunken nets o
the “Dagoes,” aided by the “ .neshmEr -nets oftne
whites. On that once-famous bream-groun , • h't-

even the local wharves are now feneed in b -' nle * n f flgh .
and this by the very people who live on the sale of hsh
ing-tackle and the letting out of boats.

In the palmv days of the Sydney ring a young
man came to to learn boxing ; he explained that
he considered the accomplishment useful,
curred. A long course was done : the aspirant paid ;
praised. In due time a match was made with anotner
blossoming pugilist of another stable. The fight " a
all concerned very satisfactory ; at its conclusion the m
satisfied person groped round the ring : side for his teac
—he had to feel his way because both eyes were Closed-
Finding the instructor, he spat out one of his few r -
maining front teeth, and reproachfully said : Look at
me and my bugle—what a father of a picklin you ve
dropped me into ! And you kidded me I was a wonder.
“So you are,” bounced the teacher; “ what have you .to
growl about —you’ve learnt the art of self-defence why,
you’ve taken enough punchin’ for ten men 1 ”

“ * Bob ’ Wain, builder and part-owner of the
Ettie, has,” The Bulletin hears, “ never sailed inher.
About 14 years ago he was in a boating accident in More-
ton Bay, when three lives were lost. He never r J,carnt to
swim, and prefers to follow races per steamer. The Ettie
has had a very unsuccessful season, not winning a single
race. This is perhaps accounted for by the absence of
‘ Fred ’ Clark, her skipper, who has been away in the
North. He is the son of Ned Clarke, senr., an old Sydney
Harbor identity, one of the ‘ Podge’ Newtown days. The
Ettie is a real ‘ hummer ’ in all weather, barring a jump
when her boomerang keel “puts her away” to leeward.
Just as “bicycle ” came down to “ bike,” so has
“ motor-car” dropped to “ mocar.”
* • * •
Jarvis was within an ace of being killed by that
ball from lightning Jones in the S.A.-Vic. match. Dr.
Barrett says that had it hit half an inch lower down
Jarvis would have crossed the river. As it is, the bone is
bent like the handle of a jug, and A.H. says he is taking
no more “ Jonah.”
* * • *
Mr., H. W. Bryant, a Melb. barrister, during a
recent fishing-trip at the Great Lake, Tasmania, caught
38 English trout, weighing 351 Jib., the average being
9Jlb. On one day the haul weighed 132 Jib.
» * * *
The recent insolvency of a certain cricketer has
an obvious moral for the young Australian athlete.
Bankrupt, a capable chemist, became famous in the field
of sport, and sought the bubble reputation at the wickets
and elsewhere to the detriment of his trade. Finally he
opened a tobacco-shop (he is a non-smoker) upon a micro-
scopic capital, trusting to his sporting friends to rally up;
but they apparently stayed away with great unanimity.
Moral: Lay to your book.
* * * *
Pedlar Palmer, the champion English feather-
weight, has been giving exhibition spars with his younger
brother at the outer London “ halls.” As might be ex-
pected, he showed little from which to take a sure line as
to his capabilities. He is a ratty-looking, prematurely-
aged customer with the orthodox “ leading left ” and the
insufficiently-used, lacking-steam “ right.” Clever and
quick with both feet and head, he takes some hitting, but
the Griffo-that-might-have-been would have supplied and
delivered the necessary quietus within, at the outside, three
rounds. Palmer, junior, is a very likely lad for 7st. 61b.,
and may be heard of, but the Pedlar, who is a bookmaker
at intervals, will lose to the first multum-in-parvo of
youthful stamina that he encounters.
* * * *
R. : “Apropos your mention of the elaborate
epitaphic tablet to the famous gee-gee, Old Coppin, in a
Melb. stable. Old Coppin’s polished skull, duly legended,
reposes in Melb. National Museum, on the right of the
entrance door, among the skeletons of the rhinoceros and
other strange beasts, for all to see and admire.”

Dear Bulletin, —You are a bit out re the ages
of the younger members of the S.A. Eleven. Evans is
quite 25 ; Bailey, 25; Travers, 26; Homburg, 23; and
Hill, 20. Still, they are youthful, in appearance and
judging from results, at cricket.—H.

The Lady who Does the Washing.
There lives at number 20, Riverdale-road, Plum-
stead, a Mrs. J. Pettett, who has an interesting
experience to relate. Mrs. Pettett has resided in
Plumstead for the last six or seven years, and is a
highly respectable lady, esteemed by the inhabi-
tants of the locality.
About three months ago she caught a chill,
which resulted in pleurisy and dropsy. An able
doctor was called, but she gradualy became worse,
enduring acute agony and growing daily more and
more enfeebled and wasted—a mere shadow of her
former self. Soon it began to be whispered
amongst the kind friends who ministered by her
bed of sickness : “ The poor thing cannot be for
long in this world.” As a last resource the hus-
band, having heard of the beneficial effects of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People, procured
some of them in the city. The result (to which
Mrs. Pettett and her friends gladly testify) was
truly marvellous. She was quickly transformed
from a helpless, bed-ridden, pain-racked creature
to a strong and healthy woman, who can, to use a
colloquialism, “ do a hard day’s work at home with
any woman.” Before taking the Pills she had
spent pounds and pounds on doctors’ medicine,
but had only received momentary relief.
To a West Ham Herald reporter Mrs. Pettett
herself gave information of incalculable service to
suffering humanity.
“ About three years ago my health," she said,
“ began to fail me; I became weak, habitually
languid, and depressed. Life was a burden to me.
At length, about a year ago, I consulted my doctor,
on whose advice I took change of air and scene,
but they did me no good. My appetite failed,
and I began to gradually waste away. t For some
little time I had noticed a peculiar numbness in
my hands, which were often as dead to me.
Shortly before last Easter I 'caught a cold, and
pleurisy and dropsy ensued.. My legs and feet
were swollen to almost twice their usual size, and
the pleurisy caused me excruciating pain. I took
to my bed, scarcely able to move hand or foot. I
subsisted on nothing but tea and water.
“ Why did you send for Dr. Williams’ Pills ? ”
“We were induced to do so by some neighbors
who had tried them, and reported truly marvellous
things of their effect in ‘building up’ the system.
I procured the Pills merely as the last resource.
But the results were wonderful. I took three
Pills per day ; the numbness left me after the first
day, and in three or four days the pleurisy and
dropsy had so far gone that I, who had been
hitherto helpless as a baby, was able to leave my
bed. Gradually both pleurisy and dropsy disap-
peared, and they have not troubled me since. My
appetite is now excellent; and I can perform all
my household duties, including the ‘washing.’”

Invalids
and
the aged.
Melbourne and Stanley streets, South
Brisbane, are rapidly being handed over to the
Syrian and the Indian. Most of the shops “near
the Bridge ” are occupied by the alien, but even
yet an odd white child may be seen playing in the
gutter with the hordes of colored kiddies.
Dear Bulletin,—“ The Banjo’s ” remark
in his excellent station sketch “ White When He’s
Wanted ” “ had it not been for that indefinable,
self-reliant look one might have taken him for a
swagman,” sticks in my throat! If “Banjo”
meant a “ sundowner ” or a “ whaler ” (men who
never work) he should have said so. Drovers, like
all bush workers, are swagmen—sometimes. So,
if this “self-reliant look” exists at all swagmen
must be burdened with it as well, unless the
drover man hides it in the bottom of his boots
when he’s out of a job. True, horsemen are more
respected in the bush than swagmen ; and drovers,
shearers and horse-breakers who travel mostly on
horseback look down with infinite contempt upon
the man who walks and carries his swag. But
even the proudest horseman makes a splash
occasionally and “ jumps his moke aver
the bar,” and has to walk away from
the shanty and the keeper’s daughter with
his bundle up, in search of any kind of work
to raise the price of more horseflesh. I recently
met a chap I knew further out when I used to
follow droving. He was riding the kind of a horse
most people like to lose, and no one cares about
finding—a poor, miserable, toothless crock. You
could count his ribs blindfolded, and hang up
tin plates all over him. His lower lip was hang-
ing down his chest, and his eyes were always
half-closed. The whole frame seemed only kept
together by an old greenhide saddle-girth, and
threatened to fall in a huddled heap the moment
the girth was unbuckled. “ Don’t like this part
of the country,” said my drover friend, as he
awoke his noble steed, who’d fallen fast asleep the
minute he stopped, with a jerk of the hay-band
reins; “ nothing but miserable swag-humping
fellers about these here parts.” That night at the
station (I got there before him on foot) he planted
his heap of bones in the scrub and sneaked to the
store with his ration-bags. “It don’t look well,”
he whispered into my ear, “ for a horseman to be
seen cadging a pint o’ dust.” —6 xB.
Item from a Canadian paper :
For years I have been searching- over the world for
genuine Christians. At one time I thought I had found
them in Mrs. Girling and the Shakers, but their faith did
not hold out among the privations of the New Forest.
Then I thought that Count Leo Tolstoi might pass mus-
ter as one, but it is evident he spends too much of his
time in literature. At last I seem to have got on the
track. There is a small Catholic monastery at Ottawa,
Canada, the inhabitants of which are known as grave-
diggers. The Daily Mail says the members of this
organisation number a dozen all told, but Jesuitical rule
is no more severe than theirs. Their one garment is a
loose brown robe. They wear neither hats, shoes, nor
stockings, and each day they perform a little of the task
of digging their own graves. Every morning at dawn, be
it fine weather or bad, each monk takes the iron spade
from his cell and wends his way to the garden, where he
solemnly delves in the dewy ground and slowly raises a
spadeful of earth from the spot where one day his
own body will be laid. Day by day, shovelful by
shovelful, each brother digs his grave, and if he does not
die by the time the grave is finished he begins, shovelful
by shovelful, to return the cast-up soil. Should death
come while the grave is unfinished, the dead man’s com-
rades complete the task for him. Then, after hours of
silent prayer, the uncoffined body is lowered into the
ground, clad in the garments it has worn during life.
After breakfast each member of the sect slings two cotton
bags over his shoulder and starts out on his regular tour
of begging. The rules of the society forbid its members
to do any sort of worldly labor. They are not permitted
to own a penny’s worth of anything, and none can eat any
article which is not received by begging. The brothers
pass almost their entire time in begging and praying.
This, I fancy, is as near an approach to genuine Chris-
tianity as can be found outside lunatic asylums.
Three prizes for Christmas short stories
given by the Otago Witness (M.L.) brought to
the surface 80 competitors, and the adjudicator
reported that out of this batch the stones unwor-
thy of publication could be counted upon the
fingers of one hand. Maorilanders should outlaw
any person who gives encouragement to 75 ama-
teur ink-slingers at one fell swoop.

Illustrative of how the cunningest of le-
vanters from the law leave loopholes of identifica-
tion : A big Northern official got wrong in his
arithmetic and had to lie low. Police and detec-
tives were watching his suspected haunts, and all
precautions were taken that he shouldn t cross
over to “ the other side ”of a river. Escape any
other way was nearly impossible, and an attempt
highly improbable. However, the levanter, with
the assistance of some friends, was shaved, dis-
guised, and clothed in a digger’s costume. He
was got over the river, furnished with funds, and
actually got on a distant diggings, where he was
to keep quiet until the scent was cold, after which
he was to “clear for Guam.” But a suspicious
constable arrested him, and the only clue was his
boots. He couldn’t bear bluchers and stuck to
the old style of cities. The bobby noted it, and
pondered, and gazed, and studied the Police
Gazette.

DANISH HERBALIST.

Herbal Remedies

BLOOD & NERVES.
AN INTERESTING NOTICE OF A MOST

MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.
FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.
- - - MIRACULO'.'S CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfaline Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise in
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Blood, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New r Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W.A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane, which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common ? ” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Mr. Button writes later:—“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,

Colin Archer, of Laurwig, Norway, builder
of Nansen’s Fram, is a blood relation of the well-
known Queensland pioneering family of the same
Scottish name. Baron Oscar Dickson, the great
monetary aider of Polar exploration, is also of
Caledonian descent. Northern Scots, Icelanders,
Norroway-men, and Danskers were ever great in-
termarryers, which accounts for Scotch names
being as common in the Norse-Swedish peninsula
as Scandinavian names in Scotland and the
Orkneys.
John Sands’ Sydney Directory (just issued
for 1897) has naturally no local rival. It is so
good that enterprise could hardly make a better.
And when comparison is made with the direc-
tories of other large cities, and accuracy checked
by continual reference through a year’s business,
it is seen that Sands’ Sydney Directory is one of
the very first in its useful field. It does very
great credit to the industry and intelligence of its
editor and compilers. This year it is further
strengthened by ajist of N.S.W. pastoral proper-
ties, with their owners.

CAPITAL : —Subscribed, £150,000. Further Reserve Liability, £150,000. Paid-up, £60,000.

Notice is hereby given that the Thirty-eighth ORDINARY HALF-YEARLY MEETING of the shareholders of the
above Company will, be held in the Board-room, at the Company’s Offices, No. 412 Collins-street, Melbourne, on
THURSDAY, 28th INST., at 11 o’clock. Business —(1) To receive the Directors’ Report and Balance-Sheet for the
half-year ended 31st December, 1896. (2) To elect two Auditors in the place of Messrs. Paterson and Cleveland, who
retire, but who are eligible for re-election, and offer themselves accordingly. (3) To consider any other business that
may be brought before the meeting of which due notice shall have been given.
REPORT.
Your Directors are glad to report a continued improvement in the business of the Company during the past half-
y )ar. Shareholders will notice a certificate by the Directors and a declaration by the Manager appended to the balance-
s.ieet, as well as alterations in the form of the Auditors’ certificate and the balance-sheet itself, which are all required
by the Companies Act Amendment Act 1896. This Act also requires a statement of contingent liabilities and con-
tingent assets. As in some trustee companies, notably the oldest-established—viz., those which are carrying on
operations in South Africa—the whole of the trust funds are regarded as direct liabilities, and the trust investments
ts assets, your Directors, in order to be within the provisions of the Act, have deemed it advisable to include as con-
tingencies the total amount of clients’ business, in regard to which, however, as you are aware, the Company is only
lesponsible for the proper administration. The amount remaining at credit of Profit and Loss Account and available
for distribution is £6014 9s. 7d., which the Directors recommend should be appropriated as follows : —To dividend on
£60,000 for half-year ended 31st December, 1896, at the rate of 5 per cent, per annum, £1500; to balance to be
carried forward, £4514 9s. 7d. ; total, £6014 9s. 7d.—By order of the Board, JAS. BORROWMAN, Manager. 412
Collins-street, Melbourne, 18th January, 1897.

We hereby certify that we have examined and compared the Books, Accounts, Vouchers, and Securities of the
Company for the half-year ended 31st December, 1896, and that, so far as we are in a position to form an opinion, the
Balance-Sheet and Accounts have been drawn up in accordance with the provisions of the Companies Acts, and
present a correct view of the state of the Company’s affairs on that date.
We further certify that we have not observed nor become acquainted during the audit with any breach of the
Companies Acts connpitted by the Company or any director, manager, auditor, or shareholder thereof, and
that all our requisitions as auditors, including those with regard to the private balance-sheet, have been complied with.
We also certify that we have made a continuous audit of the receipts and disbursements in connection with the
affairs of the clients of the Company for the six months ended 31st December, 1896 ; we have further ascertained that
there were ion" 30th June, 1896, securities (other than real estate) in the hands of the Company, belonging to its
clients, corresponding-with those shown by the books at that date, and have commenced and are proceeding with a
similar examination of these securities as on 31st December, 1896.

The Board of Management submit their Eighth Report to the
Sheet for the half year ending 31st December, 1896, duly certified
panies Act now in force

£49,339 2 2
The profits for the past half year, have been affected by the low rates of the money market, but show an improve-
ment of £2859 19s. 5d., as compared with the previous half year. After providing for the dividend to Preference
Shareholders of £10,419, the sum of £7509 7s. Id. remains of the half year’s profits, which the Board recommends be
added to the balance of Profit and Loss Account carried forward, making same £38,920 2s. 2d., as above stated.
The policy of releasing the Bank’s long-dated Deposit Receipts has been followed by the Board during the half
year, with very satisfactory results. Three instalments of 10 per cent, each have now been paid off, and a fourths-is
announced for the 12th April, which will largely reduce the deposits falling due in 1899 —all earlier maturing receipts
being cleared off by this and previous payments. The Board has pleasure in stating that the money thus released has
been freely redeposited with the Bank at lower rates than it was previously bearing.
The Branch at Alberton has been closed during the half year.
The Dividend on Preference Shares will be payable in Melbourne on and after the 3rd February, and at the
branches and in London on receipt of advices.
The transfer books of the Company will be closed from the 20th inst. to the 2nd February, both days inclusive.
EDWARD MILLER, Chairman.
J. D. LAW, General Manager.
The Bank cf Victoria Limited,
Melbourne, 12th January, 1897.

DRAPERY GOODS
to the value of £2 and upwards that can go by Parcels
Post, with the exception of certain heavy or bulky low-
priced lines, are sent by us to any Parcels Post Office in
the Colony POSTAGE FREE. Goods are thus delivered
to all parts of the Colony at Brisbane Closing-out Prices
a boon which country residents will fully appreciate.
During CLOSING-OUT SALE it is often useless to order
from Samples; when once lines are sold they cannot be
replaced. In sending samples, therefore, first, second,
and third choices should he made in each instance ; and,
in the event of all being sold, authority should be given
to us to substitute the nearest. Our best judgment is
alwa3'S used in substituting, and goods the nearest, both
as regards price and pattern sent.

The value of some Yank medical diplomas.
An Eclectic Medical School in Milwaukee (Wis-
consin) is sending out circulars to pharmacists
and others beyond U.S.A. offering medical
diplomas for sale. This precious document says
that the regular price of an M.D. diploma is £7,
but for countries which recognise the title of Dr.
but not the right to practise the price is reduced to
<£2. This diploma is “ good, lawful and valid in
Wisconsin, Kansas, Idaho, Wyoming, Michigan,
and Indiana.”
Dear Bulletin,— -fte your par. and the
tactics of time-payment jewellery-canvassers.
One device is to post, by registered packet, a
watch or other article—which, after some discus-
sion, has been refused —to the canvassed party
and cah a few weeks later for payment. If the
agent cannot then induce the recipient to keep the
article, he will bounce about its damaged condi-
tion, ask why in thunder it wasn't returned, and
“ perform ” generally. The London papers con-
tinually refer to, this class of house-to-house pest,
which somehow seems to have been acclimatised
here.—Yours, &c., Victim.
The Rev. Father Slattery, scientist of St.
Stanislaus’ College, Bathurst, N.S.W., is an Irish-
man from Waterford, 38 years old. As a priest
he belongs to the Vincentian order, and he was
educated chiefly at Paris, where are the head-
quarters of the. order, and at Castleknock, the
head-quarters of the Irish province (which in-
cludes England). His experiments with the
x rays of Prof. Rontgeii have been remarkably
successful; and, working on the cabled hints, it
appears that he advanced as quickly on the path
of discovery as any European experimentalist. It
is claimed that he was first to discover the “ Tesla
effect” of the x rays, and first in Australia to
make a fluorescent screen. He works with a fine
6-inch electrical coil, soon to be superseded by a
12-inch. So far as here known, only two 12-inch
coils are at present in use for surgical purposes,
one at Vienna and one at Berlin. Several surgical
operations have already been successfully per-
formed with the aid of the x rays manipulated by
Father Slattery.
Dear Bulletin, —An ex-Victorian, who
has seen most of the N. W. Australian sheep and
sheep-stations, says that to guard against future
loss in times of drought, which are frequent in
this uncertain, sun-dried waste, he would suggest
to the squatters that a cross between their sheep
and the native bear of eastern Australia would
prove most beneficial. Firstly, it would improve
the already existing quality of the hair on their
sheep (average price of ‘ ‘ wool ” from the N. W. for
the last four years has been 4|d. per lb.). Secondly,
in times of drought like the present (no rain for
last ten months), little or no grass being left, it
would enable the sheep to climb trees and feed on
the leaves, for surely they would have at lease two
feet with claws. The sheep now get up the trees
as far as they can, a la goat, in quest of leaves,
etc., so, with the addition of a clawed foot or two,
they would be capable of topping rather formid-
able trees. The greatest drawback to this cross
would present itself at mustering-time, when, in-
stead of horses and dogs, the squatters would have
to substitute niggers and tomahawks, in view of
which it behoves them to pay a little more atten-
tion to their already existing studs of niggers, and
see that all the offspring are trained, regardless of
color, to become useful with the tomahawk at an
early age. Another likely advantage from the
cross is that many of the lambs would not require
tailing. This matter is worth serious considera-
tion.—Yours, Mangrove, Cossack, W.A.

A man was brought before the Fitzroy
(Vic.) court, the other day, charged with stealing
a pair of handcuffs. He had been arrested, and
had made his escape with the “mittens” on him.
Next, we shall hear that somebody is arrested on a
charge of embezzling a policeman.

The Oracle ' s Cousin.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
The creature’s color? “Oh, a kind of
whitish, with ” the Wodono looked round him
for an illustration, and found one in the little
patches of sunlight cast upon the floor through the
interstices of the trellis-worlc door of his domicile.
The openings were lozenge-shaped, but the patches
of light admitted by those furthest from the
whitewood floor studded it, of course, with little
circular discs of illumination.
To these Tablan pointed. “ The extraordinary
animal,” he explained in his soft Javanese, “ was
marked exactly like that —little round spots, still
lighter, upon a lightish ground.”
The Oracle, with an incredulous smile, solemnly
shook his superior cranium.
“There is no known animal,” he pronounced,
“marked with any geometrical figure.”
“ But these,” objected the inerudite Learner,
“ are circles.”
“And isn’t the circle a geometrical figure?”
scoffed the Oracle. “Produced by a plane, pass-
ing through a cone, parallel to the base of the
cone ! ”
The Learner, after a few moments of humbled
silence, struggled out from beneath this boulder of
solid geometry with the diffident suggestion that
the creature might be something in the leopard
way.
“ Leopards have a kind of circular markings,
you know.”
The Oracle didn’t know anything of the kind.
“The leopard’s spots —but, here, Tablan—how
high do you say the thing is ? ”
The Wodono, who was about sft. high himself,
pointed to the top of his head.
“And its skull —is it like that?” The Oracle
stooped to the “harmless necessary”—rubbing
against his legs—and tapped her between the ears.
“No —like this ! ” Tablan held out his hands,
pressed palm to palm, with the thumbs sticking
up like ears.
The sun, striking strong into the hut through
the single window, flung the shadow of the
Wodono’s manual exemplification distinctly upon
a piece of white sarong stuff stretched upon the
opposite wall, and the Learner, suddenly catching
sight of the silh/mette, started, grinned, and was
grave again in an instant.
“Its tail, again, Tablan?” he asked, eagerly.
“ What did you say its tail was like ? ”
Tablan’s illustrative faculty appeared to develope
rapidly with exercise. With a rapid movement he
unfastened his conday. “ There! ” he said,
triumphantly, as the coarse, ebony-black hair
tumbled down upon his shoulders. “ That's its
tail! ”
“ I thought as much ! ” was on the Learner’s
tongue ; but, at sight of the Oracle’s supercilious
stare, he checked the exclamation, for an idea had
come to him which rendered it necessary that he
should go outside and snigger gently amongst the
penang-palms.
“ I’ve got this thing right by the handle ! ” he
said to himself ; “ and it’s just about made to order
for taking two or three storeys off our cock-a-
doodle friend’s infernal self-conceit ! I’ll get a
bulge on him to-night that he’ll never hear the end
of while he stays in Java. What’s the jackass
want now ? ”
“ I am surprised,” said the Oracle, coming out
to him ’ n the twilight, “ that you do not see the
obvious attempt to impose upon us with this pre-
posterous tale. Our credit as representatives of
the superior intelligence would suffer lamentably,
I fear, if it depended solely upon you for support. ’
ou re . < B 11 . te right, old man—-I mno equa o
the responsibility. But just you keep up your end
°f the stick as effectually as yoiive cmg c °' n o>
w ° n t matter about me. Ive a grea cea
to learn from you, I know ; but I m very wi mg,
and you ve done me a lot of good already,
* * ~ ~ ..
Now then, Tablan, the Learner said
when supper was nearly over, “ when shall we
start? There’ll be a moon, won’t there ? ” _
The moon, the Wodono explained, wouldn t be
of much use to them. There was generally a
cloak on the sky for Weeks before the beginning
of the rains. But there might be light enough to
see what they were about.
The Oracle, full of fish and self-importance, put
up an astonished monocle. “ You don’t mean to
say that, after all, you’ve profited so little by my
observations” ——
“ That I’m going to have a look for this wonder-
ful beast! Well, yes ! Mymifidssofeeble .com-
pared to yours, you see, that I can t rest satisfied
until I’ve cleared this thing up. It s a kind pf
small curiosity, you know ! ” . t( _
“ Oh, well,” the mollified Oracle admitted, I
must, of course, make allowances, for others.,
When do you expect to get back ? ;
“We? You, too, old man ! The whole thing s
beneath your notice, of course; but, if you stay
behind, it will be put down to well, to funk ! ,
“Pho! my good fellow the opinion of this old
savage ” ——
“Doesn’t matter, eh. No but the young
savage there—you don’t want her to think you a
coward, I’ll bet,” and the Learner grinned as he
pointed to Dara.
Dara was the Wodono s daughter somewhat
dumpy of figure and of feature, but. clear-skinned,
splendid-eyed, and notably espibgle. bhe laughed
when she saw the white men looking, and pointed
significantly to the Oracle’s gun. She didn’t need
to understand the language ; she read the men .
The Oracle had that temperamental weakness
which is always wondering what the other sex will
think. Also, he had made special impression, he
flattered himself, upon this plump, little, velvet-
eyed damsel m the gold-broidered head-kerchief
and snowy kabaya.
I think I had better go, after all, he said
“ just as a check upon that undisciplined lmagina-
tion of yours ! ” * « *
. . ~ „„„
A faint, diffused moonlight was strugg l g
through the clouds as the five men went down the
winding path through the sugar-cane—the W odono
and his two sons with spears and krisses—then the
white men with their rifles. Through a mango-
grove next, where the evil-smelling katunqs, nap-
ping deliriously m their faces, set the Wodono
cursing heartily over the havoc which he said
these brutes were making of his fruit-crop.
“ Also this accursed beast I have told ye of
cometh every night to destroy Ahe young rice,
The padi-fields are close at hand, and there shall
we find him. Silence now—his ears are sharp !
At the outer edge of the grove was a steep slope
from the bottom of which the rice-fields-divided
like a gigantic chess-board—stretched away m a
dead level till they were lost in the vagueness of
the night. iVlore like lakes than fields they were,
for the water, with the young rice just showing
above it, lay inches deep upon the soil as yet.
Close at hand there was a faint chattering of
bebeck-ducks ; the night wind sighed mournfully
across the watery waste ; and the Oracle—a good
deal shaken up already by his contact with the
flying foxes—shivered as the long, despairing wail
of the padi-bird arose as fearfully in the distance
as the cry of a lost spirit.
“ I think,” he said, as Tablan led the way out
upon one of the narrow intersecting embank-
ments, “ I think I will remain here. The whole
expedition is ridiculous.”
“ There’s certainly a part of it ridiculous,” the
other white man said, with contemptuous signifi-
cance. “ Stay here, then ; and don’t wander, so
that we may pick you up coming back.”
“But—but if it should come this way!—the
wild animal, I mean. ”
“Thought you didn’t believe in it! Well, you
have your rifle, haven’t you? Just ‘discipline
your imagination’ a bit, you know. Come on,
Tablan ! ”
Along the sides of the squares they went—
turning, here and there, an angle, and heading
this way and that, -till the Learner began to doubt
if he could ever regain the starting-point alone.
But to the Wodono it was apparently all plain
sailing, for, after about twenty minutes’ trotting,
he stopped suddenly at a point which had appar-
ently no special features to identify it by.
■ “ Sit, now,” he said. “This way he comes
from the jungle over there, which thou canst not
see. Get thy gun; be ready, white lord; for these
my young men fear to look upon the creature.”
The “young men,” seated on the little bank,
with their feet in the water, were obviously
enough wishing themselves well out of the busi-
ness, when their father suddenly stooped his head
to the leirel of his knees. “Listen!” he said,
and the white man. straining his ears, was just
able to catch a far-away splashing, as of some
animal slowly treading towards them through the
water. Nearer and nearer came the sound, until
the quaking of the young fellows became visible
to the naked eye, and even Tablan looked
anxiously to the white man’s gun, as the splash-
ing, after ceasing for a moment, sounded suddenly
close at hand. _
“ It has crossed the bank into the next field,”
the Wodono whispered, and the Learner—think-
ing that possibly, after all, he might have made a
mistake—felt a thrill of expectation as he stood
up with the rifle at the ready.
“Splunsh-splunsh !”—up close to the next
division, and then upon the bank, about 60 yards
away, the thing loomed shapelessly up against
the dim background of sky. Down, then, into the
water again, and steadily towards them, and still
all the white man could make out was that the
creature was large and of white color, until, hav-
ing probably winded them, it stopped and
straightened up' a longish neck to look round,
Keenly the Learner peered for a moment before
he threw up the rifle—fired—and burst into a guf-
faw as the terrible beast—uttering the noise which
Tablan had described as “like unto a man laugh-
ing ’’—splashed suddenly round—made half-a-
dozen bounds to the nearest bank—popped over it
like a hurdle-racer—and went away on the other
side with a rythmic pounding of the water that
could only have come from an animal with which
the Learner was already well-acquainted.
The Oracle was not w r here they had left
him, but at the hut they found him —hysterically
full of the fact that the terrible animal had
rushed past him at a tremendous pace, though, by
great good luck, it hadn’t caught sight of him.
“ What did you see of it ? ” the Learner asked.
“Nothing, except that it was of a lightish color
and had a longish neck.”
“ Just so ! Well, come along and show us where
it passed you. We’ll bring a couple of damar-
torches and have a look at the footprints.”
“No ! —no ! It might come back ! ”
“No matter if it should, man! It’s an old
friend of yours ; in fact, I believe it’s your
cousin ! ”
“ This man,” said the Oracle to himself, “has
gone suddenly mad ! I never thought his intellect
was very strong ! But it is best to humor the
poor creature ! ”
* * * *
The place was soon found, and the torches
held low to the footprints—far apart and deeply
planted, as of an animal at top-speed—but still
quite unmistakeable.
“ Wh-why,” stammered the Oracle —“it was a
horse 1 ”
“ Looks like it—doesn’t it ? ”
The Oracle pored over the prints for half-a-
minute before he turned up a bewildered coun-
tenance.
“ But what did you mean by saying it was my
cousin ? ”
“The horse,” said the Learner, “is generally
considered the cousin of the ass ! ”
* « » *
“You see,” the Learner explained when
he told us the yarn, “ I had heard before about a
moke that had wandered away from the Dutch
settlement at Broik and had got up into the Liaka
country, scaring the souls out of the natives, for
they’d never seen such a creature before. A fine
dapple-grey he was ; but it wasn’t Tablan’s com-
parison of his marking to the sun-spots that gave
me the clue. It was the shadow of the old boy’s
hands, laid flat together, with the thumbs sticking
upwards for ears. It doesn’t make an aristocratic-
looking horse’s head, but it’s [remarkablygsugges-
tive of the horse’s cousin. Just put your hands
together and try ! ”
