A WONDERFUL RESCUE AT MELTHAM.
A MILL MEL’S DOOM AVERTED.
[From The Huddersfield Weekly News.]
“ Meltham is ringing with it ! One of the most remarkable
things that ever came within my knowledge.” These words were
CAN ACTUALLY WALK.
spoken by a prominent
member of the Mel-
tbam Local Board to
a representative of the
Huddersfield Week-
ly News, who called
upon him to obtain
some information upon
Local Board matters.
The next words aston-
ished him even more.
“ Yes, the girl was
practically dead; she
had no use whatever
in her legs, and for I
believe six years had
been as helpless as a
child. Prior to her
illness she had worked
in the mill of Messrs.
Jonas Brook and Bro-
thers, and I and every-
one who knew her fully
believed, when we
heard three years ago
that the doctors had
given her up, that she
was doomed ; imagine,
then, our astonishment
whqn we learned about
a month ago that she
was not only in good
health and spirits, but
actually able to walk.” The reporter asked the Local Board
member if he would give him the particulars of the case. The
latter said that the girl’s name was Emma Haigh ; that she lived
at Green’s-end, Meltham. Our representative, therefore, visited
Meltham and investigated the matter, having full authority to
probe the matter and ascertain if there were any truth in the
reports that were flying about. On knocking, the door was opened
by a stout and matronly-looking woman. “Come in, sir,” she
said, and, accepting the invitation, he stepped into the home .of a
working-man, with carpeted floor, sat down on an easy long-
settle, and found himself vis-a-vis with a comely, bright-eyed
brunette of about twenty-five summers.
“Well, and how are you to-day?” said our representative.
“Oh, I’m very well, indeed,” replied Miss Haigh; and then she
gave him the facts of her case. Some seven or eight years ago
she was down with rheumatism, and for five or six weeks she was
unable to go to her work. When she had been at work about a
fortnight, her ankles and legs began to pain her, and she was
compelled to leave her work, and finally again take to her bed.
The. doctor attended her, and tried all the remedies he could think
of, but without avail, and the poor girl endured terrible pain in
her drawn-up limbs. On the advice of the doctor, her mother
took the girl to the Huddersfield Infirmary ; but, to their sorrow
and dismay, she was discharged from that institution as an incur-
able. “ Home once more, probably to die ! ” as the girl said
pathetically ; and she was put into bed, from which she did not
rise for six long and weary years—years of anguish and suffering
which only those who have suffered the pains which follow in the
train of rheumatism have any conception of. Her parents tried
everything they could get, and she swallowed gallons of one kind
of medicine and another, but all to no purpose. One day her
brother came in in a very excited way, and said that he believed
he had found something which would cure her ; and as he spoke
he produced a copy of the Weekly News, and pointed out an
article headed “ Narrow Escape of a Holmfirth Girl.” The brother
read the article aloud, and as he went on Miss Haigh said she
found that the case was a similar one to hers, and hope rose high
in her breast that once more, by the medium of the specific men-
tioned therein (Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills- for Pale People), she
might be able to walk out and breathe the fresh air of heaven.
The mother, nearly as excited as her daughter, almost flew to the
chemist and asked for some of the Pills. Before the first box was
finished, Miss Haigh said she felt a change coming over her, and
by the time she had taken four boxes she could sit up in bed and
dress her own hair. Two more boxes, and she was able to get up,
dress, and walk about the floor with the aid of crutches; “and
now, look here,” said Miss Haigh, and she got up from her chair
and walked sprightly across the floor.
“My heart jumped into my mouth,” said the mother, with
glistening eyes ; and can you wonder, when I saw her as she is
arK ] k n, ’ n '' that it is through the instrumentality of Dr.
Williams Pmk Pills that she is able to walk, that I feel so happy
and grateful to him.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured numerous cases of paralysis,
locomotor ataxy, spinal disease, rheumatism, and sciatica; also of
diseases arising from impoverishment and vitiated humors of the
blood, which cause scrofula, rickets, chronic erysipelas, consumption
of the bowels and lungs, anaemia, pale and sallow complexion,
general muscular weakness, loss of appetite, palpitations, pains in
the back, nervous headache, early decay, all forms of female
weakness and hysteria. These Pills are not purgative.

After the Mood .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Here, in this bend of the creek, in the rushes and
long lush grasses,
Wild white violets nestle, and musk in the
water-weeds;
Here there is stillness, and shelter—for the wander-
ing wind as it passes
Is caught by the tall green flax, and dies in the
raupo and reeds.
Only the roar of the creek, half-hidden in flax and
toi,
Swirling in deep, dark pools under the nigger-
head,
Only the bleat of sheep, and the distant drover’s
cooee,
Only the bark of dogs, to break the sleep of the
dead !
Shelter, and stillness, else; and over the level
plain,
Over the hedges and homesteads, and paddocks
of wheat and rye,
Shoulder and peak and glacier, range upon range
again,
Blue rise the Alps in the distance, kissing the
soft blue sky.
This is the place where we found him—here, with
his face to the skies,
Cast by the furious flood like a broken straw on
the bank;
Here at the pitiless sun he stared with unseeing
eyes—
Neither despairing nor pleading, but horribly,
hopelessly blank.
Snow? we had plenty of snow that winter of
seventy-one:
Snow on the lowlands, and snow on the high-
lands, and snow on the range ;
Never a month of spring, for all with a rush and
a run,
Winter changed into summer —folk called it a
cursed change:
For a warm nor’easter blew the whole of a windy
week,
Melted the Alpine snows, and after a day of
doubt,
We woke in the noisy night to the rush and roar
of the creek —
Woke in the wild, wet night to know that the
floods were out.
We in the homestead watched, after that weary
night,
Waited and watched through the day while the
water rose to the door ;
Watched, while the children shouted, and wel-
comed the flood with delight—
Sailed their paper-boats, and paddled about on
the floor.
On rushed the yellow flood, crashing, and dashing,
and hurling
Timber, and logs, and posts, in the whirl of the
foaming deep,
Then, as the day wore on, we heard thro’ the roar
of the swirling,
Piteous, the low of cattle, and terrified bleat of
sheep.
Then, when the flood went down, the road and
the paddocks were strewn
With timber and broken branches, half-buried
in silt and mud;
Carcases hither and thither, palings and posts torn
down,
And the wild flowers crushed and broken to
trace the course of the flood.
This is the place where he lay with his wan, white
face to the skies,
Caught here against a gorse-stump amongst the
reeds on the bank ;
Here to the pitiless sun he stared with unseeing
eyes, .
Neither despairing nor pleading, but horribly,
hopelessly blank.
And here we stood in silence, the shepherd Jim
and I
Stood, and stared at the stillness in the staring
face of the dead;
And Jim knelt down in the rushes, and closed the
expressionless eye,
And covered the corpse with his coat —“For the
sake of the mother,” he said.
Only a pipe in his pocket, and matches sodden
and damp;
Never a mark nor sign to trace him, his home,
or his name;
“ Only a swagger,” we heard, and nobody misses
a tramp
Houseless and friendless—who cared whither he
went or came ?
We buried him here where we found him, for the
parson was miles away,
While the wild wind rustled the flax-blades, and
gorse-blossoms scented the air ;
Here, with the drooping wild-flowers, that glorious
sweet spring day,
We left the nameless swagger, with never a
dirge nor prayer.
Gentleman, swagger, clown what difference
perishing thus ? *
In the face of the pitiful present, what were the
things of the past ?
Gentle or simple—what matter? it was nothing
to him or to us,
We are all of us gentle, maybe, and simple too,
at the last!
What were the odds to him ? Did it fare with him
better or worse.
Rolled like a log down the creek, choked by the
fierce yellow wave,
Flung in the ooze on the bank, caught in a snag
of the gorse, '
Laid by ungentle hands away in a nameless
grave ?
Yet the shepherd Jim and I had looked on the
face of the dead,—
Looked on the dogged jaw, and forehead solid
and square:
There was will in the resolute mouth, and brain in
the massive head—
Drowned like a rat in the creek, and that powe.-
and intellect there !
And somewhere, out in the distance, was there a
mother or wife
Waiting, and watching, and praying, as only
women can pray ;
Waiting, and watching, and praying in vain for
a wasted life,
For that unknown tramp who perished—how
many miles away ?
What was the good of it all ?—of intellect, power,
and strength ?
What had he done with his life? Why was he
born to the world ?
What was the use of it all?—to live for a space,
and at length ,
Lie like a log in the mud where the refuse and
rubbish are hurled ?
Ay, you may weep and pray, you women, and
weep again,
Weep for the wasted talent, weep for the useless
The whole wide world weeps with you, the whole
world’s tears are vain
Even as yours, O Mother! even as yours, O
Wife !
We plod through the daily routine, we see in our
own dull way (
That our useless lives are useful m the life ot
the human race; .
Our influence lasts for ever, our virtues and vices
may
Bear fruit in our children’s children, and set
them each in his place.
Oh, answerless riddle of riddles ! as the purpose-
less years rolled by
We also have vexed our souls since the human
epoch began,
Who live, eat, drink, and are merry, who suffer,
and sin, and die,
Content to be amongst many—then how for the
hundredth man ?
The man who should rise and lead us, the many,
the common crowd,
Who should leave his mark upon us by right of
a powerfuller brain ;
The man who, with broader thought and higher
feeling endowed,
Was—only an unknown swagger, whose exist-
ence was void and vain !
Ah, well! let him sleep in peace where the water-
weeds and the mosses
Nestle under the raupo in the quiet bend of the
cr©ok *
Life is a difficult thing with its longings, its loves,
and its losses;
May Death prove an easier matter to all of us,
strong or weak !
M.L.
DORA WILCOX.
A Patch from a Western Canvas.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Keep your eyes on that old fellow with the three
spears,” said Tom Oscott (alias Coen Tom),
“and be ready to drop one or two if they dart
away.” But the nigs, stood in a cluster and did
not attempt to break away. Some of the gins
still carried items of spoil from the looted camp,
though for the most part these had been thrown
away in the short flight from the dispersers. The
adults kept up a constant chattering ; the pican-
ninies clung round the bare legs of their mothers
and chattered in louder and shriller key.
“Holy Joe,” whose father, it was said, was a
parson in some little Welsh village, entered a
protest. “ Only one or two —just to frighten
them,” he said; “that will be enough to teach
them a lesson.”
“Yes, we’ll teach them a lesson, don t fret,
said Tom. Now, then, chaps— all ready . ”
There was an almost instantaneous crack of four
Winchesters and a Martini. Jack’s horse, which
would not stand fire, reared on its hind legs like a
volunteer field-officer’s charger, and Jack dropped
his gun unceremoniously and hung on. There
were yells from the natives. One of them had
dropped bis spears and was staggering hither and
S3E? with his hands on his stomach like a boy
who had been kicked at football. Iwo orner
wero dovvn. gcattered kke a gW arm of flies. The
fun began. Tom galloped after one lot of*b"»T
a man and a woman and a boy , ma ’ ( j eo ff
dently members of the same family ‘ rlb
in the direction of a patch of cork-bark scru .
The boy lagged behind, hesitating, and got m the
wav of the horse, which went over him, and left
him lying still. The man and the gin reached the
sc b There were three shots, and presently
Tom rode back, pushing fresh cartridges into the
magazine of his Winchester. Mick O Gee and
Scot tv Binks who had also been doing some
shooting, returned from different directions to the
spot whence the mob had scattered and all dis-
mounted and sat under the scanty shade of a
salmon-tree. There was one captive, a gin, who,
sullen and badly scared, said nothing.
Tom wanted it to be target practice, but O Gee
objected. “This is old Mary,” he said, “that put
me on to the gnamma hole out back of Dooley s
find—and if you shoot her I’ll be inclined to drop
y °Tom was annoyed at this consideration for a
nigger, and said so ; but the rest deprecated quar-
rellfng over such a trifle. So Tom madeanother
proposal, to which O’Gee could offer no objection.
Tom led the gin a few hundred yards away
behind a screen of cork-bark bushes, and she lay
down. Then he put his heavy foot upon her neck
and pressed. Her eyeballs started from her head,
and hoarse, inarticulate cries came from her
throat; her body writhed and struggled like a
kangaroo on which a dog has fastened, and her
hands tore spasmodically and with deadly earnest-
ness at the foot which was crushing out her life.
Meanwhile Tom had lifted a large stone, and
poising it above his head for an instant, dashed it
downward with all his strength The skull
cracked like an eggshell. The stone rolled over, skin
and blood adhering to its surface. Mary lay still.
“Where’s the gin?—got away, did she?
en “ l Wefl, she might have if I hadn’t knocked her
on the head,” answered Tom.
Q’(q ee would have pursued the enquiry, but just
then Holy Joe diverted attention. He had picked
up his gun and had cleaned it carefully from the
sand and gravel which had been scooped into the
muzzle when he had unceremoniously flung it to
the ground in order to sit his horse. He stood
alongside the salmon-tree, having satisfied himself
that the barrel was undamaged, and, putting a
cartridge into the breach, fired at one of the
bodies of the niggers who had been killed by the
first shot—about 80yds. away. The body heaved
slightly and the dust flew up on the other side,
showing that the missile had passed through.
“ You fool,” said Tom, cutting short
O’Gee’s enquiry, “ and cartridges worth 6d. each.
Why the don’t you shoot at live ones when
you would be doing some good, instead of plugging
dead niggers ! ”
W.A.
H.C.
